\begin{document}

\title[SQP for Optimal Transport]{Beyond Brenier: Sequential Quadratic Programming for General-Cost Monge Optimal Transport}
%Sequential Quadratic Programming for the Monge Optimal Transport Problem
\author{Zihe Zhou \and Harsha Honnappa \and Raghu Pasupathy}
\date{March 2026}
\address{315 N Grant St, West Lafayette, IN 47907-2023, United States}
\email{\{zhou408,honnappa,pasupath\}@purdue.edu}
\maketitle
\let\thefootnote\relax
\footnotetext{MSC2020: Primary 90C15, Secondary 93E20, 60H30.} %%%%%%%%%%

\begin{abstract}
  The Monge optimal transport (OT) problem seeks to optimize transportation cost from a source probability measure to a target probability measure. The optimization is over a space of transport maps and the transportation cost is defined by a cost functional of the maps. As recent interest arises in OT, many works focus on tackling the OT problem computationally using finite-dimensional approximations. In this work, we present the infinite-dimensional formulation of the OT problem over a Banach space. We provide explicit expressions of the first and second-order variation of the objective functional, and of the functional constraint with respect to the transport map. We propose a Sequential Quadratic Programming (SQP) framework for this infinite-dimensional problem. We show that subject to reasonable regularity assumptions, our framework satisfies known conditions for local convergence. Moreover, we demonstrate that a merit functional with sufficient constraint penalization serves as an effective step-size monitor within SQP iterations, leading to global convergence towards critical points. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first attempt at a globally convergent SQP operator recursion over infinite-dimensional spaces.
\end{abstract} %%%%%%%%%

\bigskip


\section{Introduction and Perspective}

Optimal transport (OT) provides a mathematical framework for transforming probability measures in a cost-optimal manner. Given probability measures $\mu$ on $\mathcal{X}$ and $\nu$ on $\mathcal{Y}$, and a measurable cost function $c:\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{Y}\to\mathbb R\cup\{+\infty\}$, the modern formulation due to Kantorovich considers couplings $\pi$ on $\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{Y}$ with prescribed marginals. The Kantorovich problem is
\begin{equation}
\inf_{\pi\in\Pi(\mu,\nu)} \int_{\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{Y}} c(x,y)\,d\pi(x,y),
\end{equation}
where $\Pi(\mu,\nu)$ denotes the set of all probability measures on $\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{Y}$ with marginals $\mu$ and $\nu$. This problem is a linear program over a convex feasible set. Under mild regularity conditions on the cost (e.g., lower semicontinuity and integrability), the feasible set is weakly compact and strong duality holds, yielding a well-understood dual formulation and guaranteed existence of optimal solutions \citep{Villani2003,Villani2009}. These structural properties have enabled the development of reliable and scalable algorithms, including linear and interior-point methods for discrete OT, first-order primal--dual schemes, and, after entropic regularization, highly efficient Sinkhorn-type algorithms \citep{Cuturi2013,PeyreCuturi2019}.

\subsection{Monge OT Often Induces Nonconvexity}
The classical Monge formulation predates Kantorovich and seeks a deterministic transport map $s:\mathcal{X}\to\mathcal{Y}$ satisfying the pushforward constraint $s_\#\mu=\nu$, minimizing
\begin{equation}\label{monge}
\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x,s(x))\,d\mu(x).
\end{equation}
While conceptually appealing and often required in applications, the Monge problem is inherently nonconvex because the pushforward constraint $s_\#\mu=\nu$ is nonlinear, and the set of feasible maps is not convex. As a result, existence, uniqueness, and algorithmic tractability are substantially more delicate than in the Kantorovich setting.

A notable exception arises in the Brenier setting, where $\mathcal{X}=\mathcal{Y}=\mathbb R^d$, the cost is quadratic, and $\mu$ is absolutely continuous with respect to Lebesgue measure. In this case, the optimal Kantorovich solution is induced by a unique Monge map that can be expressed as the gradient of a convex potential \citep{Brenier1991,McCann1995}. This representation restores convex structure at the level of the potential and underlies a substantial literature on convex optimization and Newton-type solvers for the associated Monge--Amp\`ere equation \citep{FroeseOberman2011,Benamou2014}. However, this convexity is highly fragile and does not persist beyond this specific setting.

Outside the Brenier regime, convexity typically breaks down even when Monge maps exist. For nonquadratic costs, e.g., $c(x,y)=\|x-y\|^p$ with $p\neq 2$ or more general costs satisfying a twist condition, existence and uniqueness of Monge solutions may still hold under suitable regularity assumptions, but the resulting formulations rely on $c$-convex geometry and no longer correspond to convex optimization problems \citep{GangboMcCann1996,Villani2009}. Similarly, when the source measure $\mu$ is singular, as in discrete or empirical measures, Brenier's theorem fails even for quadratic cost. Kantorovich solutions typically split mass, whereas Monge solutions lead to genuinely nonconvex optimization problems.

Despite these difficulties, there are many settings in which a deterministic Monge map is explicitly required. In economics and matching theory, agents must be matched deterministically and cost or surplus functions are rarely quadratic \citep{Galichon2016}. In data-driven applications such as domain adaptation, generative modeling, and simulation, the source measure is often empirical, yet the goal is to learn a deterministic transformation rather than a probabilistic coupling \citep{Arjovsky2017,PeyreCuturi2019}. In control, particle transport, and dynamical systems, one frequently seeks an explicit flow map rather than a relaxed transport plan. In these contexts, the Kantorovich formulation provides a useful convex relaxation, but its solutions are not directly deployable, and common post-processing heuristics (e.g., barycentric projection) only partially address the modeling requirements.

\subsection{Why SQP Methods?} There appears to be a clear methodological gap between the modeling demands that motivate Monge optimal transport and the algorithmic tools currently available for solving the resulting problems outside the convex Brenier regime. While the Kantorovich formulation admits powerful convex relaxations and enjoys global strong duality, these guarantees rely on optimization over couplings and generally fail to transfer to the Monge formulation once deterministic maps are imposed. In such cases, the restriction to Monge maps introduces genuine nonconvexity, and global strong duality with respect to the Kantorovich dual typically breaks down, reflecting the possibility of mass splitting in the relaxed problem. This failure is structural rather than pathological and is analogous to the loss of strong duality commonly encountered in nonconvex constrained optimization when convexity assumptions are violated \citep{Luenberger1969,Bertsekas1999}.

Importantly, the absence of global strong duality does not preclude the existence of meaningful dual structure at the level relevant for algorithmic design. In nonlinear programming, it is well understood that even for nonconvex problems, local saddle-point structure and strong duality in a \emph{local} sense persist under appropriate constraint qualifications and second-order sufficient conditions \citep{Luenberger1969,NocedalWright2006,Bertsekas1999}. When such conditions hold, Lagrange multipliers exist, the Karush--Kuhn--Tucker (KKT) conditions characterize local optima, and the associated Lagrangian provides a valid local dual model. This notion of local duality, rather than global convex duality, is precisely the foundation on which sequential quadratic programming (SQP) methods are built.

Viewed through this lens, Monge optimal transport fits naturally within the scope of constrained nonconvex optimization. In an SQP framework, the transport map (or an associated potential or parameterization) is treated as the primary decision variable, the nonlinear pushforward constraint is enforced explicitly and linearized at each iterate, and the resulting subproblems take the form of structured quadratic programs that approximate the Lagrangian to second order while preserving the linearized constraints \citep{NocedalWright2006,Bertsekas1999}. Standard globalization strategies, e.g., merit functions, line search, or trust regions, can then be employed to ensure convergence from remote starting points, even in the absence of global convexity \citep{Luenberger1969}. While global strong duality may fail for Monge optimal transport, the persistence of local duality and second-order structure provides a rigorous theoretical foundation for SQP-type methods, suggesting a principled pathway for developing algorithms with provable local convergence guarantees in regimes where deterministic transport maps are indispensable and convexity is absent.

A key feature of our framework is that it does not assume the twist condition on the cost function. In one dimension, when the cost satisfies the twist condition, specifically, when the mixed derivative $D^2_{xy}c$ has a definite sign, the structure of the optimal Monge map is determined a priori see \citep[Theorem 3.2]{guillen2010five}. If $D^2_{xy}c<0$, the optimal map is monotone increasing and given by the quantile coupling $s^*(x)=G^{-1}\circ F(x)$; if $D^2_{xy}c>0$, it is monotone decreasing and given by $s^*(x)=G^{-1}\circ(1-F(x))$. 
The regime our framework targets where such structural guarantees are absent. When the mixed derivative $D^2_{xy}c$ does not have a uniform sign, the monotonicity of the optimal map is unknown and neither inverse-CDF formula applies. In this paper, the twist condition is not a standing assumption, but enters only in Section~\ref{sec:cp}, where we invoke classical existence and uniqueness results to ensure that the Monge problem admits a well-defined optimizer $s^*$ against which our computational approximation can be benchmarked. The SQP framework itself requires smoothness of the cost and admissibility of the transport map (monotonicity and nondegeneracy, as formalized through the problem domain $\Omega$), but does not require the twist condition or any a priori knowledge of which monotonicity class the optimizer belongs to.

\subsection{Summary of Insight and Contribution}
Our goal is to study and solve the general cost Monge OT problem with with (i) a theoretical analysis posed directly in the infinite-dimensional space of transport maps, and
(ii) a computational scheme that is implementable and whose objective values provably approach the true objective as the approximation and discretization is refined. 

We assume that $\mathcal{X}, \mathcal{Y} \subset \mathbb R$. Working directly over the space of transport maps, we impose measure preservation through the pushforward constraint:

% \begin{align*}
%     &\min_{s\in\mathcal{S}} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s(x))\,f(x)dx\\
%     &\text{s.t.}\qquad Pf(s)\equiv g,
% \end{align*}
\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s\in\Omega} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x,s(x))\,d\mu(x)\\
    &\text{s.t.}\quad s_\#\mu=\nu,
\end{align*}
Throughout, we assume $\mu$ and $\nu$ are absolutely continuous with respect to Lebesgue measure on $\mathcal{X}$,
so we may write $d\mu(x)=f(x)dx$ and $d\nu(y)=g(y)\,dy$ for densities $f$ and $g$.
Under absolute continuity, the pushforward constraint $s_{\#}\mu=\nu$ can be expressed equivalently at the level of
densities. To this end, we introduce the Perron--Frobenius (pushforward) operator $Pf(s)$, which maps an input density
$d\mu=f$ to the density of the pushforward measure $s_{\#}\mu$. With this notation,
\[
s_{\#}\mu=\nu
\quad \Longleftrightarrow \quad
Pf(s) = g \ \ \text{(a.e. on } \mathcal{X}\text{)}.
\]
Hence, the measure-preserving constraint can be written as the infinite-dimensional equality constraint
$Pf(s) - g = 0$.
Most classical results that characterize the solution of Monge OT require ``nice'' cost structures, most notably transportation costs of the form $c(x,y)=\tilde c(x-y)$ with $\tilde c$ quadratic or strictly convex
(e.g., \cite{brenier1991polar,GangboMcCann1996}).
In contrast, we do not require strict convexity of the cost on the translation $|x-y|$, nor do we require $c(x, y)$ to take the form of $c(x,y)=\tilde c(x-y)$.
Instead, we work under milder conditions on $c$, such as smoothness and injectivity properties, which allow us to treat a broader class of costs while remaining in the Monge setting. The key contributions of this paper are as follows:

%A key observation in this work is on the local geometry of the feasible set: depending on the choice of the approximation space for $\mathcal{S}$, feasible points can be isolated, and feasibility cannot be preserved by small local perturbation. These two features motivate an approach that can move through infeasible region and handle nonconvexity. We therefore adopt a sequential quadratic programming (SQP) approach, which solves a sequence of sub-problems at each iteration of the method, and gradually approaches the true solution.

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=*, label=\arabic*.]

\item (Local Geometry of the Feasible Sets) 
A central structural fact in our analysis is that the first-variation operator of the pushforward constraint is injective, and that this injectivity has different geometric implications in finite- versus infinite-dimensional spaces.
When the solution space is finite-dimensional, injectivity of the firs-variation operator $Pf_s$ of $Pf(\cdot)$ implies that feasible points are isolated. Intuitively, if the Jacobian has full column rank, then the tangent
space is trivial:
\[
Pf_s(h)=0 \quad \Longrightarrow \quad h=0, \quad \mu\text{--a.e}
\]
so there is no nonzero first-order direction that preserves the constraint. Geometrically, one can think of the feasible set as a collection of isolated points in parameter space and moving in any direction immediately breaks feasibility.
In an infinite-dimensional function space, however, the same conclusion no longer follows from injectivity alone.
The finite-dimensional isolation argument relies on a key fact of the injective operator, namely a positive minimum modulus:
\[
\inf_{\|h\|=1}\|Pf_s(h)\| \;>\; 0,
\]
which provides a uniform lower bound and forces the null space to be robustly trivial. In infinite dimensions, injectivity does not imply a positive minimum modulus as one can construct a sequence
$\{h_n\}\subset \mathcal{S}$ with $\|h_n\|=1$ such that
\[
\|Pf_s(h_n)\| \to 0 \quad \text{as } n\to\infty,
\]
so the linearized constraint can be made arbitrarily small along nontrivial directions. As a consequence, the sufficient condition behind the finite-dimensional isolation result breaks down, and feasible points are not necessarily isolated in the infinite-dimensional setting.

\item (Consistency of the Computational Objective)
The original optimization problem is posed directly over transport maps and includes a pushforward constraint. While this formulation is conceptually well-posed, it is computationally intractable in its native form: the decision variable lives in an infinite-dimensional function space, and the pushforward constraint is a functional equality constraint. To make the problem solvable, we introduce a finite-dimensional computational objective that approximates the original one, and we prove that this approximation becomes exact in the limit as the approximation and discretization are refined. 

We start by recording standard existence and uniqueness results for the continuous Monge problem. Since our consistency analysis compares the computational approximation to the true objective, we first ensure that the continuous problem admits a unique optimizer $s^*$ (unique $\mu$--a.e.). Two complementary regimes are relevant in our setting. For strictly convex transportation costs of the form
$c(x,y)=\tilde c(x-y)$, strict convexity of $\tilde c$ yields uniqueness of the optimal transport map and an almost-everywhere inverse map via \cite{GangboMcCann1996}. To cover objectives beyond strict convexity and beyond the transportation form, we also rely on the existence and uniqueness theorem of \cite{philippis2013regularity}. In that regime we do not assume convexity; instead we impose regularity and injectivity conditions on $c$, which are
sufficient to guarantee a unique optimal transport map. Under additional non-degeneracy and H\"older regularity of the cost, the optimal map is an almost-everywhere $C^{1,\alpha}_{\mathrm{loc}}$ bijection between full-measure subsets of $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)$. This bijection property is a key input for discretization: it legitimizes a pointwise change of variables
representation of the pushforward constraint involving $s^{-1}$ and its derivative, which can then be enforced on a grid over the support of $g$.

We bridge the theoretical and the computational objectives by introducing two intermediate objectives. First, we restrict the transport maps to a finite-dimensional family $\mathcal{S}_{n\infty}$ (e.g., $n$ degrees piecewise linear maps), isolating the approximation error from representing an infinite-dimensional map by finitely many parameters. Second, we replace the functional pushforward constraint
by finitely many pointwise constraints enforced on a grid, isolating the approximation error from enforcing the constraint only at a
discrete set of locations. These two approximations naturally define intermediate problems that isolate each source of error, and the
final computational problem combines both.

The convergence proof relies on the error decomposition brought by the two intermediate problems. We start by only enforcing the functional
pushforward constraint with a finite set of pointwise equality constraints over a grid, and then additionally restrict the transport map to a finite-dimensional subspace. Denote the corresponding optimal values by
$J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ (finite-dimensional constraint) and $J(s_{nm}^*)$ (finite-dimensional solution space). Two ingredients drive the argument. First, under our regularity assumptions, $Pf(s)$ admits a continuous representative; therefore,
if $Pf(s)=g$ holds on a refining sequence of grids whose union is dense in $\mathrm{supp}(g)$, then $Pf(s)=g$ holds everywhere. This yields
the pointwise limit
\[
J(s_{\infty m}^*)\;\longrightarrow\;J(s^*)\qquad (\text{as }m\to\infty),
\]
i.e., the constraint gridding-only optimal value converges to the true optimal value. Second, Lipschitz continuity of $J$ with respect to $\|\cdot\|_{\infty}$ implies that a uniform approximation error in the map yields a proportional error in the objective value; specifically,
\[
|J(s)-J(\hat s)| \le K\,\|s-\hat s\|_{\infty} .
\] Using this and monotonicity of feasible sets in $n$,
we prove that for each fixed $m$,
\[
J(s_{nm}^*)\;\longrightarrow\;J(s_{\infty m}^*)\qquad (\text{as }n\to\infty),
\]
and moreover this convergence is \emph{uniform in $m$}. With these two facts in hand, the Moore--Osgood theorem allows us to exchange limits and conclude
\[
\lim_{n\to\infty}\lim_{m\to\infty} J(s_{nm}^*)
\;=\;
\lim_{m\to\infty}\lim_{n\to\infty} J(s_{nm}^*)
\;=\;
J(s^*),
\]
i.e., the optimal objective value of the computational problem converges to the optimal value of the original infinite-dimensional problem
as $n,m\to\infty$.


Finally, we quantify the approximation with explicit rates. For fixed grid size $m$, we show
\[
\big|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\big|\;=\;O\!\left(\frac{1}{n}\right),
\]
by constructing a feasible piecewise linear approximation of $s_{\infty m}^*$ with sup-norm error $O(1/n)$ and invoking the
Lipschitz continuity of $J$. For the pushforward constraint approximation, we show
\[
\big|J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(s^*)\big|\;=\;O\!\left(\frac{1}{m}\right),
\]
by refining the grid and making localized corrections that enforce each newly added constraint (as we increase the number of grid points) while preserving the earlier ones. Each correction is controlled in $\|\cdot\|_{\infty}$, and Lipschitz continuity of $J$ makes the resulting objective changes summable, yielding the
$O(1/m)$ rate.

\item (SQP Framework for General Cost Monge OT)
We develop a SQP framework for the general cost Monge OT, and we establish convergence guarantees in both the functional and implementable finite-dimensional settings. 
In the infinite-dimensional setting, we formulate a functional SQP step. Using the first- and second-order Fr\'echet derivative formulas, we verify the local convergence conditions of \cite{alt1990lagrange} at a local solution, which yields a well-defined SQP step and a local convergence result for the resulting iterates. To obtain globalization, we introduce a merit functional that balances objective decrease and constraint violation, and we show that for a sufficiently large penalty parameter, each SQP step produces a descent direction for the merit functional. Coupled with an Armijo line search, this yields global convergence of the SQP iterates to a critical point of the merit functional from an arbitrary initialization, this is to be differentiated from convergence to the global optimizer.
In the finite-dimensional setting, we derive the discrete SQP iteration for the computational problem by writing the quadratic subproblem and its KKT linear system. We then characterize standard regularity assumptions under which the discrete SQP method can converge locally with appropriate grid choice for the constraint: when the constraint Jacobian satisfies LICQ and a second-order sufficient condition holds at a solution pair, the iterates converge quadratically with the exact Lagrangian Hessian, following the classical SQP theory in \cite{wright1999numerical}. Together, these results provide an optimization framework for nonconvex Monge OT that is grounded in explicit variational calculus and comes with both theoretical and computational convergence guarantees.

\item (First and Second Variations) 
At each SQP iteration, we solve a local quadratic program obtained by (i) a second-order approximation of the objective and (ii) a first-order approximation of the pushforward constraint. Concretely, given the current iterate $s\in\mathcal{S}$ and an
increment direction $h\in\mathcal{S}$, SQP uses the local expansions:
\[
J(s+h)\;\approx\; J(s) + J_s(h) + \tfrac12\,H_s(h,h),
\qquad
Pf(s+h)\;\approx\; Pf(s) + Pf_s(h),
\]
where $J_s\in\mathcal{S}^*$ and $H_s\in\mathcal{S}^{**}$ are the first and second Fr\'echet derivatives of $J$, and $Pf_s$ is the Fr\'echet derivative of the pushforward operator. This leads to a Newton-Lagrange system (equivalently, the KKT conditions of the quadratic subproblem) that couples the quadratic objective with linearized constraint. As a foundation for this construction, we derive explicit first- and second-order variations (which are Fr\'echet) for both the Monge objective functional and the pushforward constraint operator. These formulas serve as the basic components for the SQP subproblems that support the convergence analysis, and also entail the local geometry of feasibility, as described above.
\end{enumerate}


\section{Example Settings}

In this section, we outline several example applications where the Monge OT in~\eqref{monge} becomes the natural formulation. As noted, nonconvexities in~\eqref{monge} can be caused either due to the cost $c(\cdot,\cdot)$ not being quadratic, or due to the source measure $\mu$ being singular. The examples below are chosen to exemplify these different sources of nonconvexity. 

\subsection{Deterministic Matching}
\label{sec:econ_monge_example}

As a first example, consider a classical matching problem arising in economics, where the objective is to deterministically assign agents from one population to agents in another so as to maximize aggregate surplus. Let worker types be represented by $x \in \mathcal{X} \subset \mathbb R^d$, distributed according to a probability measure $\mu$, and job (or firm) types by $y \in \mathcal{Y} \subset \mathbb R^d$, distributed according to $\nu$. A feasible assignment is given by a measurable map
$s : \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}, s_\# \mu = \nu,$
so that each worker type is matched to exactly one job type and the induced distribution of jobs matches $\nu$.

A widely used specification for the match surplus (or equivalently, negative cost) takes the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:econ_cost}
c(x,y)
=
\phi(x)
+
\psi(y)
-
x^\top A y
+
r(x,y),
\end{equation}
where each term has a clear economic interpretation: $\phi:\mathcal{X} \to \mathbb R$ represents worker-specific productivity or outside options that are independent of the match; $\psi:\mathcal{Y} \to \mathbb R$ represents job-specific productivity or reservation utilities; $A \in \mathbb R^{d\times d}$ encodes bilinear complementarities between worker and job characteristics; and $r(x,y)$ captures nonlinear interaction effects, frictions, or institutional features that are not well represented by a purely bilinear model. (Since the term $\phi(x)$ does not depend on $s$, and since $\int_{\mathcal{X}} \psi(s(x))\, d\mu(x) = \int_{\mathcal{Y}} \psi(y)d\nu(y)$, the terms $\phi$ and $\psi$ in~\eqref{eq:econ_cost} do not affect the optimizer, but they are included for modeling completeness and economic interpretation.)

In many such economic settings, couplings that split mass are inadmissible, as they correspond to random or mixed assignments of worker types to job types. Consequently, the Monge formulation in~\eqref{monge} is the more meaningful model, whereas the Kantorovich formulation serves only as a relaxation. Under suitable regularity conditions on $\mu$ and $\nu$ (e.g., absolute continuity) and mild assumptions on the cost, such as a \emph{twist condition} $y \mapsto \nabla_x c(x,y)$ being injective for each $x,$ it is known that a unique optimal Monge map exists for problem \eqref{monge}. The bilinear term $-x^\top A y$ typically enforces this condition, and small or smooth nonlinear perturbations $r(x,y)$ preserve it. However, the problem (while well-defined) becomes nonconvex if $r(x,y)$ is nonquadratic, and the classical Brenier representation of the optimal map as the gradient of a convex function fails.

\subsection{Transportation and Routing}
\label{sec:transport_monge_example}

We next consider a class of transportation and routing problems in which individual agents, vehicles, or flow units must be assigned deterministically to routes, schedules, or trajectories. Let $x \in \mathcal{X} \subset \mathbb R^d$ denote an agent descriptor such as an origin-destination pair, departure time, or state vector, distributed according to a probability measure $\mu$. Let $y \in \mathcal{Y} \subset \mathbb R^m$ represent a transportation outcome, such as a route choice, arrival time, or trajectory summary, with target distribution $\nu$. A feasible transportation policy is given by a measurable map $s : \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}, s_\# \mu = \nu,$ so that each agent is assigned a unique transportation outcome and the aggregate distribution of outcomes matches prescribed system-level requirements.

A representative cost functional takes the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:transport_cost}
c(x,y)
=
\ell(x,y)
+
g\bigl(y,\,R(s)\bigr),
\end{equation}
where $\ell(x,y)$ represents an individual travel cost (e.g., travel time, distance, or delay), and $g(y,R(s))$ captures congestion effects, capacity penalties, or other system-level interactions that depend on the aggregate flow induced by the assignment $s$. Here, $J(s)$ denotes a suitable flow or load functional determined by the pushforward measure $s_\#\mu$, that is, $R(s) \equiv J(s_\#\mu)$ and $R$ is often a linear functional.

As in the first example, since randomized or mass-splitting assignments are not operationally meaningful here, the Monge formulation in~\eqref{monge} provides the appropriate modeling framework. Under suitable regularity conditions, e.g., smooth $\ell$ and monotonicity properties of $g$, optimal assignments may exist and be unique, particularly when the induced marginal $\nu$ lies in the interior of the feasible flow polytope. However, the problem is often nonconvex because the presence of congestion or capacity effects introduces nonlinear dependence of the cost on the induced pushforward measure. Even when $\ell(x,y)$ is convex in $y$, the aggregate interaction term $g(y,R(s))$ typically destroys any underlying convex structure.

\subsection{Deterministic Map Learning}
\label{sec:ml_monge_example}

We finally consider a class of problems arising in machine learning and data-driven modeling, where the objective is to learn a deterministic transformation between probability distributions. Let $\mu$ denote a source distribution supported on $\mathcal{X} \subset \mathbb R^d$, and let $\nu$ denote a target distribution supported on $\mathcal{Y} \subset \mathbb R^m$. In many applications, $\mu$ is an empirical measure of the form 
$\mu = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \delta_{x_i},$
corresponding to a finite dataset, while $\nu$ may be continuous or empirical. A feasible model is given by a deterministic map $s : \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}, s_\# \mu \approx \nu,$
which transforms each data point $x_i$ into an output $s(x_i)$ so that the induced distribution matches the target.

In contrast to the Kantorovich formulation, which permits mass splitting and randomized couplings, many machine learning applications require an explicit deterministic transformation. Examples include normalizing flows, generative models, and domain adaptation, where a map $s$ must be evaluated pointwise to generate samples, transfer representations, or deploy a learned model. In such settings, transport plans are not directly implementable, and the Monge formulation in~\eqref{monge} provides the appropriate modeling framework.

A representative objective takes the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ml_cost}
\min_{s \in \mathcal{S}}
\left\{
\int_{\mathcal{X}} c\bigl(x,s(x)\bigr)\,d\mu(x)
\;+\;
\lambda\,\mathsf D\!\left(s_\#\mu,\,\nu\right)
\right\},
\end{equation}
where $c(x,y)$ is a pointwise loss function, $\mathsf D(\cdot,\cdot)$ is a discrepancy or divergence between probability measures (e.g., an integral probability metric or moment-matching penalty), and $\mathcal{S}$ denotes a parameterized class of maps, such as neural networks or other function approximators.

Even when the loss $c(x,y)$ is convex in $y$ and the divergence $\mathsf D$ is convex in its arguments, problem~\eqref{eq:ml_cost} is generally nonconvex. First, the source measure $\mu$ is singular, so the classical Brenier theory does not apply even for quadratic cost, and the optimal Kantorovich solution typically splits mass. Imposing a deterministic map therefore introduces a nonconvex restriction on the feasible set. Second, the parameterization of $s$ through a finite-dimensional family $\mathcal{S}$ leads to additional nonconvexity in the decision variables. As a result, the optimization problem lies outside the convex regimes traditionally associated with optimal transport, despite being motivated by distributional matching objectives.



\subsection{Physics-Inspired Transport Costs}
\label{sec:physics_monge_example}

Our final class of examples arises from physical systems in which the transport cost $c(x,y)$ is determined by an underlying physical law rather than prescribed by modeling convenience. We present three example settings, in optics, classical mechanics, and quantum mechanics, and, in each case, the cost takes the problem outside the quadratic Brenier regime, and the Monge formulation is the physically meaningful one. Specifically, the optimizer seeks a deterministic assignment of source configurations to target configurations, not a probabilistic coupling.

\paragraph{\it Optical path costs.}
In freeform illumination design~\citep{yadav2019monge,romijn2019freeform,prins2015least}, a lens or reflector must redirect a source intensity $f(x)$ into a prescribed target intensity $g(y)$. Each ray entering at position $x$ and arriving at $y$ incurs a cost equal to its optical path length. For a cylindrical lens with screen distance $D$, this yields
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lens-cost-sec2}
    c_{\textup{lens}}(x,y) = \sqrt{(x-y)^2 + D^2},
\end{equation}
which interpolates between the quadratic cost ($|x-y| \ll D$) and the $L^1$ cost ($|x-y| \gg D$). The far-field reflector antenna problem~\citep{wang2004design,glimm2003optical} gives rise to the logarithmic cost
\begin{equation}\label{eq:reflector-cost-sec2}
    c_{\textup{refl}}(x,y) = -\log|x - y|,
\end{equation}
posed naturally on the sphere $S^{n-1}$, where source and target occupy disjoint hemispheres and the cost is bounded. In both cases, the Monge formulation is the physically correct one: each ray must be assigned a unique destination, and mass-splitting (i.e., a single ray arriving at multiple locations) is not physically realizable.

Both costs satisfy the twist condition ($D^2_{xy}c < 0$ away from the diagonal), so the Gangbo--McCann theory~\citep{GangboMcCann1996} applies and optimal maps exist and are unique. However, the costs are genuinely non-quadratic, and the optimal map is not the gradient of a convex function in any simple sense. The lens cost has a twist strength $|D^2_{xy}c_{\textup{lens}}|$ that decays as $|x-y|^{-3}$ for large displacement, while the reflector cost has a singularity at $x = y$. The regularity theory for the reflector cost is well-developed: Wang~\citep{wang2004design} established existence via a Monge--Amp\`ere formulation, and Loeper~\citep{loeper2011regularity} showed that the Ma--Trudinger--Wang condition is satisfied on the sphere, yielding smooth optimal maps for smooth data~\citep{ma2005regularity,loeper2009regularity}.

\paragraph{\it Lagrangian obstacle costs.}
A more challenging setting arises when the cost is not given in closed form but is itself defined through a variational problem. Consider transporting mass from $x$ to $y$ along a path $\gamma:[0,1] \to \mathbb{R}$ through a potential landscape $V(z)$. The cost is the minimum-action path:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lagrangian-cost-sec2}
    c(x,y) = \inf_{\substack{\gamma \in C^1([0,1]) \\ \gamma(0)=x,\, \gamma(1)=y}}
    \int_0^1 \bigl[\lambda\,|\dot\gamma(t)|^2 + V(\gamma(t))\bigr]\, dt,
\end{equation}
where $\lambda > 0$ controls the kinetic energy penalty and $V \geq 0$ is a potential barrier. This is the action functional of classical mechanics; the optimal path satisfies the Euler--Lagrange equation, and $c(x,y)$ is the corresponding Hamilton--Jacobi value function. The Monge problem is then a \emph{bilevel optimization}: the outer problem chooses the transport map $s$, while the inner problem~\eqref{eq:lagrangian-cost-sec2} computes the cost of each assignment.

When $V = 0$, the inner problem yields straight-line paths and $c(x,y) = \lambda(x-y)^2$, recovering the quadratic cost. A nonzero barrier $V$ deforms the cost in two ways that take it outside the Gangbo--McCann framework. First, the cost function is a value function of a variational problem; by the theory of Hamilton--Jacobi equations, such value functions are Lipschitz but not necessarily $C^1$ at points where multiple optimal paths exist, violating the smoothness hypotheses of the twist condition. Second, even where $c$ is smooth, the twist condition can fail locally: in regions where the barrier forces all paths through a common bottleneck, the mapping $y \mapsto D_x c(x,y)$ loses injectivity. Since one cannot verify a priori whether the optimal transport plan avoids the twist-failure region, the standard sufficient conditions for existence and uniqueness are not applicable.

This class of problems has been studied in the recent machine learning literature under the name ``optimal transport with Lagrangian costs''~\citep{pooladian2024neural,asadulaev2024neural,fan2021scalable}, where both the transport map and the inner paths are parameterized by neural networks. As in the preceding examples, the Monge formulation is the natural one: the physical system requires a deterministic assignment of source positions to destinations, not a probabilistic coupling.

\paragraph{\it Coulomb repulsive costs.}
The most extreme departure from the Brenier regime arises in the strong-interaction limit of Density Functional Theory (DFT), where the ground-state energy of $N$ electrons with one-body density $\rho$ is determined by a multi-marginal optimal transport problem with Coulomb cost~\citep{Seidl1999,ButCDP2012}. For $N = 2$ electrons in one dimension, this reduces to finding a map $f$ pushing $\rho/2$ to itself that minimizes the Coulomb repulsion:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:sce-cost-sec2}
    \min_{f:\; f_\#(\rho/2) = \rho/2} \int \frac{1}{|x - f(x)|}\,\frac{\rho(x)}{2}\,dx.
\end{equation}
The optimal map $f$ is the \emph{co-motion function}~\citep{Seidl1999}: $f(x) = F^{-1}(1 - F(x))$, where $F$ is the CDF of $\rho$. This is the anti-quantile coupling, a monotone \emph{decreasing} map that pairs each electron with its ``opposite'' in the density distribution.

The Coulomb cost $c(x,y) = 1/|x-y|$ violates the hypotheses of the Gangbo--McCann theory in essentially every way. The cost is singular on the diagonal ($c \to +\infty$ as $x \to y$); it is convex and decreasing in $|x-y|$ (repulsive), the opposite regime from the attractive costs for which classical theory is developed; the mixed partial $D^2_{xy}c = 2/|x-y|^3 > 0$ has the wrong sign for the standard twist condition; and the self-transport constraint $f_\# \mu = \mu$ is degenerate for classical uniqueness results. Nonetheless, the problem is well-posed for bimodal densities with sufficient separation: the co-motion function avoids the singularity by mapping each atomic mode to the opposite mode, and the mathematical structure has been analyzed by Colombo, De Pascale, and Di Marino~\citep{ColomboDeP2015}.

The Monge formulation is again the physically correct one. In the SCE framework, the co-motion function encodes the deterministic relationship between electron positions in the strongly correlated ground state; couplings that split mass correspond to partially correlated states that do not arise in the strong-interaction limit. Beyond the two-electron case, the $N$-electron problem gives rise to a multi-marginal optimal transport problem with pairwise Coulomb cost $\sum_{i < j} 1/|x_i - x_j|$, where computational cost scales exponentially in $N$ and the existence of optimal maps in general three-dimensional settings remains open~\citep{ButCDP2012}.

\paragraph{\it Common structure.}
The three settings above share a common structure: the cost is determined by a physical law, the Monge formulation is the natural one, and the problem lies outside the regime where Brenier's theorem or the Gangbo--McCann theory provides a complete answer. For the optical costs, the twist condition holds but the cost is non-quadratic, so the optimal map is not the gradient of a convex function. For the Lagrangian cost, the twist condition fails locally, so existence and uniqueness cannot be guaranteed by standard theory. For the Coulomb cost, the twist has the wrong sign, the cost is singular, and the transport is self-referential. In each case, a direct optimization approach to the Monge problem --- one that does not rely on structural properties of the cost such as the twist condition or $c$-convexity --- provides the appropriate computational framework.


\section{Mathematical Preliminaries}\label{sec:pre}
\subsection{Notation}\label{subsec:notation}
Let $\mathcal{X}\subseteq\mathbb R$ be compact. Let $\mu$ and $\nu$ be two probability measures on $\mathcal{X}$, and assume they are absolutely continuous
with respect to Lebesgue measure with density functions $f,g\in \mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})$, respectively; that is,
\[
d\mu(x)=f(x)dx, \qquad d\nu(x)=g(x)dx.
\]

Let $\mathcal{Z} := L^1(\mathcal{X})$ and define the mean-zero subspace $\mathcal{Z}_0:= \left\{w \in L^1(\mathcal{X}):\int_{\mathcal{X}} w(x)\,dx = 0\right\}$. 
Since $\mathcal{Z} = L^1(\mathcal{X})$, its Banach dual is $\mathcal{Z}^* := \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{Z},\mathbb R)$, the space of bounded linear functionals on $\mathcal{Z}$.
Moreover, by the standard $L^1$--$L^\infty$ duality, $\mathcal{Z}^*$ is isometrically isomorphic to $L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ (denoted by $\mathcal{Z}^* \cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$),
in the sense that each $\lambda\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ induces a functional $z^* \in \mathcal{Z}^*$ via the pairing
\[
z^*(\eta) := \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x)\,\eta(x)dx, \qquad \forall\,\eta \in \mathcal{Z},
\]
and every $z^* \in \mathcal{Z}^*$ admits such a representation for some $\lambda\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$, unique up to equality almost everywhere~\citep[Theorem~6.15]{folland1999real}.
With this identification, we define the dual pairing
\[
\langle z^*,\eta\rangle_{\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{Z}} := z^*(\eta) = \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x)\,\eta(x)dx, \qquad z^*\in \mathcal{Z}^*,\ \eta\in \mathcal{Z}.
\]
with the associated operator norm
\[
\|z^*\|_{\mathcal{Z}^*} := \sup\Big\{ \langle z^*,\eta\rangle_{\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{Z}} : \|\eta\|_{\mathcal{Z}}\le 1\Big\} = \|\lambda\|_{L^\infty(\mathcal{X})}.
\]

The dual space of $\mathcal{Z}_0$ is $\mathcal{Z}_0^* \cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}$.
This quotient structure arises because every $w \in \mathcal{Z}_0$ integrates to zero, so adding a constant to a representative $\lambda \in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ does not change the induced functional on $\mathcal{Z}_0$:
\[
\int_{\mathcal{X}} \big(\lambda(x)+c\big)w(x)\,dx
=
\int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x)w(x)\,dx.
\]
Accordingly, elements of $\mathcal{Z}_0^*$ are represented by equivalence classes $[\lambda] \in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}$.

We consider the space of transport maps to be a subspace $\mathcal{S}\subseteq L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ to ensure they map the compact set $\mathcal{X}$ into itself. Because our analysis relies on inner products for variational calculus and uniform bounds for approximations, we utilize a two-norm framework. Throughout this paper, the default norm $\| \cdot \|$ on any element $h\in\mathcal{S}$ denotes the $L^2(\mu)$ norm:
\[ 
\|h\| := \left(\int_{\mathcal{X}}|h(x)|^2 f(x)dx\right)^{1/2}, 
\]
where $|\cdot|$ denotes the absolute value on $\mathbb{R}$. The associated inner product is
\[
\langle a,b\rangle_{L^2(\mu)} := \int_{\mathcal{X}} a(x)b(x)f(x)dx. 
\]
When uniform bounds are required (such as for establishing the Lipschitz continuity of the objective functional), we explicitly invoke the supremum norm, denoted by $\| \cdot \|_\infty$.

We define the mean-zero subspace of $L^2(\mu)$ as $L^2_0(\mu) := \left\{ \psi \in L^2(\mu) : \mathbb{E}[\psi(Y)] = 0 \right\}$, and the mean-zero elements of the transport map space as $\mathcal{S}_0 := \{h \in \mathcal{S} : \mathbb{E}[h(Y)] = 0\}$.

For any vector $v\in\mathbb{R}^d$, the same notation $\|v\|$ denotes the Euclidean norm,
\[
\|v\| := \|v\|_2 := \Big(\sum_{i=1}^d v_i^2\Big)^{1/2},
\]
and $\langle u,v\rangle := u^\top v$ denotes the standard Euclidean inner product.
For densities (elements of $\mathcal{Z}$) we define the $L^1$ norm with respect to Lebesgue measure.
\[
\|z\| _{\mathcal{Z}}:=\int_{\mathcal{X}}|z(x)|dx,\qquad z\in \mathcal{Z}.
\]
Denote by $\mathcal{B}(U,V)$ the space of bounded linear maps from $U$ to $V$.

For $L\in \mathcal{S}^*:=\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathbb R)$, define
\[
\|L\|_{\mathcal{S}^*} :=\sup_{\|h\|=1}|L(h)|.
\]
For $I\in \mathcal{S}^{**}:=\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{S}^*)$, define
\[
\|I\|_{\mathcal{S}^{**}} :=\sup_{\|h\|=1}\|I(h)\|_{\mathcal{S}^*}.
\]

For $A\in\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z})$, we define the induced operator norm by
\[
\|A\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z})} := \sup_{\|h\|=1}\|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}}.
\]
Likewise, for $A\in\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)$, we define
\[
\|A\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)} := \sup_{\|h\|=1}\|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}.
\]

Let \(C^{k,\alpha}_{\mathrm{loc}}\) denote the space of functions whose derivatives up to order \(k\) exist and whose \(k\)th derivatives are locally \(\alpha\)-H\"older continuous. We write \(W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal X)\) for the Sobolev space of essentially bounded functions with essentially bounded weak derivatives, equipped with the norm
\[
\|s\|_{W^{1,\infty}}
:=
\|s\|_{L^\infty}+\|s'\|_{L^\infty}.
\]
Since \(\mathcal X\) is compact, one has the continuous embeddings
\[
W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal X)\hookrightarrow L^\infty(\mathcal X)\hookrightarrow L^2(\mu).
\]
Hence every \(s\in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal X)\) belongs in particular to \(L^\infty(\mathcal X)\cap L^2(\mu)\). Therefore, any estimate in the sequel that only requires \(L^\infty\)- or \(L^2(\mu)\)-control remains applicable to functions in \(W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal X)\). Finally, \(ACLR(c,d)\) denotes the class of absolutely continuous functions on \((c,d)\) vanishing at both endpoints \(c\) and \(d\).

Lastly, $AC_{LR}(c,d)$ denotes the class of absolutely continuous functions on $(c,d)$ vanishing at both endpoints $c$ and $d$.


\section{Problem Formulation}\label{sec:formulation}
We now introduce the precise Monge optimal transport formulation that will be used throughout the paper.
Let $\mathcal{X}\subseteq \mathbb R$, and let $c(\cdot,\cdot):\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}\rightarrow\mathbb R$ be a cost function. Let $\mathcal{Z} := L^1(\mathcal{X})$ and $\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})\subset \mathcal{Z} $ be the set of probability density functions on $\mathbb R$.

Let $\mu$ and $\nu$ be two probability measures on $\mathcal{X}$, and assume they are absolutely continuous
with respect to Lebesgue measure with density functions $f,g\in \mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})$, respectively; that is,
\[
d\mu(x)=f(x)dx, \qquad d\nu(x)=g(x)dx.
\]
Let $\mathcal{S}\subseteq L^2(\mu)$ be a normed space of transport maps $s:\mathcal{X}\to\mathcal{X}$. Consider the objective functional $J:\mathcal{S}\rightarrow \mathbb R$ defined as 
\[
J(s) :=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s(x))f(x)dx.
\]
We denote its first and second variation operators by
\[
J':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{S}^*, \qquad J'':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{S}^{**},
\]
and for $s\in\mathcal{S}$ write
\[
J'(s)=:J_s\in\mathcal{S}^*, \qquad J''(s)=:H_s\in\mathcal{S}^{**}.
\]
We say that $J$ is Fr\'echet differentiable at $s \in\mathcal{S}$ if for all $h\in\mathcal{S}$, 
\[
\big|J(s+h)-J(s)-J_s(h)\big|=o(\|h\|),
\]
and twice Fr\'echet differentiable at $s$ if, in addition,
\[
\|J'(s+h)-J'(s)-H_s(h)\|_{\mathcal{S}^*}=o(\|h\|).
\]

Recall the Perron--Frobenius (PF) operator $Pf:\mathcal{S}\rightarrow \mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})$ from \cite{boyarsky2012laws},
\[
Pf(s) := \left\{\frac{d}{dx}\int_{s^{-1}(A)} f(y)dy , x\in \mathbb R \right\}
\]
where $A =(-\infty, x]$.  The function $Pf(s)$ is the density of the pushforward measure $s_{\#}\mu$. 
Since $Pf(s)$ is a probability density, we regard $Pf$ as a map into $\mathcal{Z}$ (which includes $\mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})$), while its first variation takes values in $\mathcal{Z}_0$.
Its first and second variation operators are
\[
Pf':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0), \qquad Pf'':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{B}\!\big(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)\big),
\]
and for $s\in\mathcal{S}$ we write
\[
Pf'(s)=:Pf_s\in \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0), \qquad Pf''(s)=:Qf_s\in \mathcal{B}\!\big(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)\big).
\]
We say that $Pf$ is Fr\'echet differentiable at $s$ if for all $h\in\mathcal{S}$,
\[
\|Pf(s+h)-Pf(s)-Pf_s(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}}=o(\|h\|),
\]
and twice Fr\'echet differentiable at $s$ if, in addition,
\[
\|Pf'(s+h)-Pf'(s)-Qf_s(h)\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z})} =o(\|h\|).
\]

We call a transport map $s\in\mathcal{S}$ measure-preserving (with respect to $\mu$ and $\nu$) if $s_{\#}\mu=\nu$.
When $d\mu(x)=f(x)dx$ and $d\nu(y)=g(y)\,dy$, this is equivalent to the following identity: for every Lebesgue measurable set $A\subseteq \mathcal{X}$,
\[
\int_{s^{-1}(A)} f(x)dx = \int_A g(y)\,dy,
\]
i.e.,
\[
\mu\big(s^{-1}(A)\big)=\nu(A).
\]
In density form, the same condition can be expressed using the Perron--Frobenius (pushforward) operator:
\[
Pf(s) = g \qquad \text{(Lebesgue a.e. on $\mathcal{X}$)}.
\]
Thus, the statements $s_{\#}\mu=\nu$, the set-wise identity above, and $Pf(s) = g$ are equivalent ways of expressing the same measure-preserving constraint. The pushforward density $Pf(s)$ is well-defined and continuous when $s$ is monotone and nondegenerate  (see Lemma~\ref{lem:pf-continuous-version}). For the local convergence analysis of Section~\ref{subsec:loc_conv}, we further require these structural properties to persist on an entire neighborhood of the minimizer $s_0$, so that $Pf(s)$ and its Fr\'echet derivatives are defined for all $s$ near $s_0$. We therefore introduce the problem domain
\[
\Omega
:=
\bigl\{\, s \in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X}) : s \text{ is monotone and } |s'(x)| \ge c_s > 0 \text{ for some } c_s>0, \text{ a.e.\ } x\in\mathcal{X} \,\bigr\}.
\]
Equivalently, $\Omega = \Omega_+\cup\Omega_-$ where $\Omega_\pm := \{s\in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X}):\pm s'(x)\ge c_s>0 \text{ for some }c_s>0,\text{ a.e.}\}$. Since the $W^{1,\infty}$-norm controls the derivative uniformly, $\Omega$ is open in $W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X})$: if $s_0\in \Omega_+$ with $s_0'(x)\ge c>0$ a.e., then for any $\tilde s$ with $\|\tilde s-s_0\|_{W^{1,\infty}}<c/2$, we have $\tilde s'(x)\ge c/2>0$ a.e., so $\tilde s\in \Omega_+$.  This openness guarantees that sufficiently small perturbations of any admissible transport map remain admissible.

All optimization problems in this paper are posed over $\Omega$ or its finite-dimensional restriction $\Omega_n:=\mathcal{S}_n\cap\Omega$ (defined in Section~\ref{subsec:approx}). The role of $\Omega$ is purely one of \emph{admissibility}: it ensures that $Pf(s)$ and its derivatives are well-defined throughout the feasible region. All norms, inner products, and convergence statements continue to use $L^2(\mu)$ and $L^\infty$ as defined above.

We can thus reformulate the measure-preserving constraint into an infinite-dimensional equality constraint and our objective now becomes:

\begin{comment}
    \begin{align*}
    &\min_{s\in\mathcal{S}} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s(x))f(x)dx \tag{P}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & Pf(s)-g =0_{\mathcal{Z}},
\end{align*}
where $Pf(s)-g$ is thought of as a map from $\mathcal{S}$ to $\mathcal{Z}$. $\mathcal{Z}$ is the range of $Pf(s)-g$. $0_{\mathcal{Z}}$ is the zero element in $\mathcal{Z}$. Note that $\mathcal{Z}$ is a subset of the space of real-valued, integrable functions that integrate to zero.

Let $Z := L^1(\mathbb R)$ and define the closed subspace
\[
Z_0 := \Big\{ \eta \in Z \;:\; \int_{\mathbb R} \eta(x)dx = 0 \Big\}.
\]
Since $Pf(s),g\in \mathcal{P}(\mathcal{X})\subset L^1(\mathbb R)$, we have $Pf(s)-g\in Z_0$ for all $s\in\mathcal{S}$.
Accordingly, define the equality constraint mapping $\mathcal{H}:\mathcal{S}\to Z_0$ by
\[
\mathcal{H}(s) := Pf(s)-g,
\]
and rewrite (P) as $\mathcal{H}(s)=0_{Z_0}$.
\end{comment}


\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s\in\Omega} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s(x))f(x)dx \tag{P}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & \mathcal{H}(s)=0_{\mathcal{Z}},
\end{align*}

where $0_{\mathcal{Z}}$ is the zero element in $\mathcal{Z}$, and the equality constraint mapping $\mathcal{H}:\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{Z}$ is defined by
\[
\mathcal{H}(s) := Pf(s)-g \in \mathcal{Z}.
\]
Accordingly, the Lagrangian of (P) is the mapping $\mathcal{L}:\mathcal{S}\times \mathcal{Z}^*\to \mathbb R$ defined by \citep[cf.][{\S}9.3]{luenberger1969optimization}
\[
\mathcal{L}(s,z^*) := J(s) + \langle z^*,\mathcal{H}(s)\rangle_{\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{Z}}, \qquad z^*\in \mathcal{Z}^*.
\]
When $z^*$ is represented by a function $\lambda\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ (i.e., $z^*\equiv \lambda$ under $\mathcal{Z}^*\cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$), the Lagrangian of (P) can be written in the integral form
\[
\mathcal{L}(s,\lambda) = J(s) + \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x)\,\mathcal{H}(s)(x)dx.
\]

\section{Key Assumptions}\label{subsec:assume}
In this section, we list some assumptions. 

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:domain-support}
Let $\mathcal{X}\subset \mathbb R$ be compact. The supports $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ are nonempty open intervals in $\mathcal{X}$.
\end{assumption}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:density-cont}
The functions $f$ and $g$ are continuous probability densities on $\mathcal{X}$.
\end{assumption}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:cost-lipschitz}
The cost function $c(\cdot,\cdot):\mathcal{X} \times\mathcal{X}\rightarrow\mathbb R$ is $K$-Lipschitz in its second argument.
\end{assumption} 

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:cost-second}
The cost function $c(\cdot,\cdot):\mathcal{X}\times\mathcal{X}\to\mathbb R$ is twice continuously differentiable in its second argument.
There exist constants $L,M>0$ such that for all $x\in\mathcal{X}$ and all $y_1,y_2\in\mathcal{X}$,
\[
|D_y c(x,y_1)-D_y c(x,y_2)|\le L\,|y_1-y_2|, \qquad |D^2_{yy}c(x,y_1)-D^2_{yy}c(x,y_2)|\le M\,|y_1-y_2|.
\]
\end{assumption}


\begin{assumption}\label{ass:philippis-C2}
The cost function  $c(\cdot,\cdot):\mathcal{X} \times\mathcal{X}\rightarrow\mathbb R$ is twice continuously differentiable on
$\mathrm{supp}(f)\times \mathrm{supp}(g)$, and
\[
c \in C^2\!\big(\mathrm{supp}(f)\times \mathrm{supp}(g)\big), \qquad \|c\|_{C^2(\mathrm{supp}(f)\times \mathrm{supp}(g))}<\infty,
\]
where, for $X,Y\subseteq \mathcal{X}$,
\[
\|c\|_{C^2(X\times Y)} :=\sup_{(x,y)\in X\times Y}\Bigl( |c(x,y)| +\|\nabla c(x,y)\| +\|D^2 c(x,y)\|_{\mathrm{op}} \Bigr).
\]
Here $\nabla c(x,y)$ denotes the gradient of $c$ with respect to $(x,y)$, and
$D^2 c(x,y)$ denotes the corresponding Hessian matrix. The matrix norm $\|\cdot\|_{\mathrm{op}}$ is the operator norm induced by the Euclidean norm $\|\cdot\|$.
\end{assumption}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:mono}
$\Omega$ consists of monotone functions $s:\mathrm{supp}(f)\to\mathrm{supp}(g)$ such that $|s'(x)|\ge c_s>0$ for some $c_s>0$, Lebesgue a.e.\ $x\in\mathcal{X}$.
\end{assumption}

\section{Fr\'echet Derivatives of the Objective and Constraint}\label{sec:deriv}
In this section, we derive the first and second Fr\'echet derivatives for both the Monge objective functional $J(s)$ and the Perron--Frobenius pushforward operator $Pf(s)$. These explicit operator representations serve as the computational and theoretical foundation for the local quadratic approximations utilized in the remainder of the paper.
\begin{theorem}\label{thm: J1deriv} 
    Under Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, the map $J_s(\cdot): \mathcal{S}\rightarrow\mathbb{R}$ defined by
    \begin{align*}
        J_s(h) := \int_{\mathcal{X}}h(x)\, D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\, f(x)dx
    \end{align*}
    is the Fr\'echet derivative of $J(\cdot)$ at $s$. 
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm: J2deriv} 
    Under Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-second}, the bilinear form $H_s(\cdot)(\cdot): \mathcal{S}\times\mathcal{S}\rightarrow\mathbb{R}$ defined by
    \begin{align*}
        H_s(h)(\Tilde{h}) :=\int_{\mathcal{X}}\Tilde{h}(x)\,h(x)\, D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s(x)\big)\, f(x)dx    
    \end{align*} 
    is the Fr\'echet derivative of $J_s(h)$ at $s$. Equivalently, $H_s(\cdot)(\cdot)$ defines an operator
    $H_s(\cdot)(\cdot):\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{S}^*$ via $h\mapsto (\Tilde h\mapsto H_s(h)(\Tilde h))$.
\end{theorem}
Note that $H_s(h)(\cdot)\in\mathcal{S}^*$ is a real-valued functional on $\mathcal{S}$ and $H_s(\cdot)(\cdot)\in\mathcal{S}^{**}$ can be viewed as an operator on $\mathcal{S}^*$.

\begin{theorem}\label{thm: PF1deriv}
Let $Y$ be a random variable with density $f$ on the compact set $\mathcal{X}\subset\mathbb R$.
Fix $s\in\mathcal{S}$. Assume:

\begin{enumerate}[label=(A\arabic*),itemsep=0.4em]
\item
The characteristic function of the random variable $s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)]$ is integrable, and there exists $K>0$ such that for every
$0<|\epsilon|<K$ the characteristic function of $(s+\epsilon h)(Y)-\mathbb E[(s+\epsilon h)(Y)]$ is integrable.

\item
The random variable $s(Y)$ admits a once continuously differentiable density on $\mathcal{X}$, denoted
$g_s(x):=Pf(s)(x)$, with $g_s'\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$.

\item
There exists a function $M_1\in L^1(\mathbb R)$ such that for all $h\in\mathcal{S}$ and all $0<|\epsilon|<K$,
\begin{align}\label{eq:M1_dom}
|t|\,
\left|
\mathbb E\!\left[
e^{it\left((s+\epsilon h)(Y)-\mathbb E[(s+\epsilon h)(Y)]\right)}\,
\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)
\right]
\right|
\;\le\; M_1(t)\,\|h\|.
\end{align}
\end{enumerate}
Then $Pf$ is Fr\'echet differentiable at $s$ as a map $Pf:\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{Z}$, meaning there exists a bounded linear operator $Pf_s \in \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)$ such that:
\[
\|Pf(s+h) - Pf(s) - Pf_s(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}} = o(\|h\|)
\]
as $\|h\| \to 0$. This Fr\'echet derivative $Pf'(s) := Pf_s$ is evaluated at $h\in\mathcal{S}$ is given by
\begin{align}~\label{eq:first-var}
Pf_s(h)(x)
=&\;\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R} -e^{itx}\,e^{-it\,\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\!\left[
it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)
\right]\,dt\\
&\;-\;g_s'(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)] ,
\qquad x\in\mathcal{X}.
\end{align}
\end{theorem}

\begin{comment}
Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} establishes the general form of the Fr\'echet derivative of the Perron--Frobenius operator,
expressed in terms of expectations involving the characteristic function of $s(Y)$.
We now restrict attention to the subspace 
of strictly increasing and differentiable transport maps. 
Within this setting, the derivative admits a closed-form expression that depends only on the 
density $f$ and the inverse map $s^{-1}$.
\begin{corollary}\label{cor: PF1deriv}  
Assume $\bar{\mathcal{S}}\subset\mathcal{S}$ is a subspace comprised of strictly increasing and differentiable functions. Then
\[
g_s(x) = Pf_s(h)(x)=\frac{d}{dz}\frac{f((s+zh)^{-1}(x))}{(s+zh)'((s+zh)^{-1}(x))}\Big|_{z=0}.
\]
\end{corollary}
\end{comment}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm: PF2deriv} 
Under the conditions of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, further assume that the random variable $s(Y)$ has a twice
continuously differentiable density function $g_s(x):=Pf(s)(x)$ with $g_s''\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$.
Assume in addition that 
\begin{enumerate}[label=(A\arabic*),itemsep=0.4em]
\setcounter{enumi}{3}
\item
There exists a function $M_2\in L^1(\mathbb R)$ and a constant $K>0$ such that for all
$h,\Tilde h\in\mathcal{S}$ and all $0<|\epsilon|<K$,
\begin{align}\label{eq:M2_dom}
|t|^2\,
\Big|\mathbb E\!\Big[
e^{it\left((s+\epsilon\Tilde h)(Y)-\mathbb E[(s+\epsilon\Tilde h)(Y)]\right)}
\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\big(\Tilde h(Y)-\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]\big)
\Big]\Big|
\;\le\; M_2(t)\,\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|.
\end{align}
\end{enumerate}
Then the derivative map $Pf':\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)$ is Fr\'echet differentiable at $s$, i.e., there exists a bounded linear operator $Qf_s \in \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S}, \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0))$ such that:
\[\|Pf'(s+\Tilde{h}) - Pf'(s) - Qf_s(\Tilde{h})\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z})} = o(\|\Tilde{h}\|)
\]
as $\|\Tilde{h}\| \to 0$. The second Fr\'echet derivative $Pf''(s) := Qf_s$ evaluated at $h,\Tilde{h}\in\mathcal{S}$ is given by:
\begin{align*}
&Qf_s(h)(\Tilde{h})(x) \\
& \quad =\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R} e^{itx} e^{-it\,\mathbb E [s(Y)] }\mathbb E\Bigg[ e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E [s(Y)])}
\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\bigg(t^2\big(\Tilde{h}(Y)-\mathbb E[\Tilde{h}(Y)]\big)+it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde{h}(Y)]\bigg)\Bigg]dt\\
&\qquad+g_s''(x)\,\mathbb E [h(Y)] \mathbb E [\Tilde{h}(Y)].
\end{align*}
\end{theorem}

\section{Local Geometry of the Constraints}\label{sec:local-geometry}
In the previous section, we derived explicit first and second variations for the objective and for the pushforward constraint through the Perron–Frobenius operator. These derivatives determine the local linearization of feasibility and, consequently, the local geometry of the feasible set. Let $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}$ be any feasible point of (P). Recall that $\bar{s}$ is feasible if the constraint mapping $\mathcal{H}(s) := Pf(s) - g$ satisfies $\mathcal{H}(\bar{s}) = 0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$, where $\mathcal{Z}_0 := \left\{w\in L^1(\mathcal{X}) : \int_{\mathcal{X}} w(x)dx = 0 \right\}$ is the mean-zero subspace of $L^1(\mathcal{X})$.

In this section, we show that if the solution space $\mathcal{S}$ is finite-dimensional, every feasible point $\bar{s}$ is isolated as a direct consequence of the injectivity of $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$, the first variation operator of $Pf$ evaluated at $\bar{s}$. In contrast, when $\mathcal{S}$ is infinite-dimensional, injectivity alone no longer guarantees a uniform lower bound, meaning the minimum modulus may vanish and the isolation of feasible points cannot be concluded in general. Nevertheless, under appropriate conditions, we can establish the closedness of the range of $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$, which allows us to recover full surjectivity.

We start by establishing that the first variation operator at a feasible point is unconditionally injective. We start by recalling the following classical consequence of the Stone-Weierstrass theorem regarding the completeness of complex exponentials.
\begin{lemma}\label{lem:completeness}
Let $\mu$ be a finite Borel measure on $\mathbb{R}$ with compact support $\mathcal{X} \subset \mathbb{R}$. Then the family of functions $\{e^{itx} : t \in \mathbb{R}\}$ is complete in $L^2(\mu)$. That is, if for some $u\in L^2(\mu)$ we have
\[ \int_{\mathcal{X}} u(x) e^{itx} \, d\mu(x) = 0 \quad \text{for all } t \in \mathbb{R}, \]
then $u(x) = 0$ $\mu$--almost everywhere.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proposition}\label{prop:injective}
Let $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}$ be a feasible transport map for (P) such that $Pf(\bar{s})=g$. Suppose that Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}, \ref{ass:density-cont}, and \ref{ass:mono} hold, and assume further that the regularity conditions \emph{(A1)--(A3)} of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} are satisfied at $\bar{s}$. Then, the first variation operator $Pf_{\bar{s}}: \mathcal{S} \to \mathcal{Z}_0$ is injective. In particular, if $Pf_{\bar{s}}(h) = 0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$, then $h = 0$ $\mu$--a.e.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
Define the centered random variable $\bar{h}:=h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]$. Using the first-variation formula in~\eqref{eq:first-var}, the assumption $Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)=0_{\mathcal{Z}}$ yields:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:F_inv_equals}
\mathscr{F}^{-1}\!\big(e^{-it\,\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)]}\phi_h\big)(x) 
= g'(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)] 
\quad\text{for a.e. }x\in\mathcal{X},
\end{equation}
where the function $\phi_h(t):=it\,\mathbb E\!\left[\bar{h}\,e^{it(\bar{s}(Y)-\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)])}\right]$. By Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}\,(A3) evaluated at $\epsilon=0$, $\phi_h(t)$ is bounded by an $L^1(\mathbb{R})$ dominating envelope. Thus, applying the Fourier transform $\mathscr{F}$ to both sides of \eqref{eq:F_inv_equals} in the classical $L^1$ sense, we obtain the transform-domain identity:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:Fourier_identity}
e^{-it\,\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)]}\phi_h(t)=\mathscr{F}(g')(t)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)] \qquad \text{for a.e. }t\in\mathbb R.
\end{equation}
For the right-hand side, since
\[
\mathscr F(g')(t) = \int_{\mathcal{X}} e^{-itx} g'(x)dx,
\]
we have
\[
\mathscr F(g')'(0)= -i\int_{\mathcal{X}} x\,g'(x)dx.
\]
Since $\mathrm{supp}(g)=(a,b)\subset \mathcal{X}$ is an open interval by Assumption~\ref{ass:domain-support}, adn $g$ vanishes outside $(a,b)$, we have $g(a)=g(b)=0$. Therefore integration by
parts on $(a,b)$ yields
\[
\int_{\mathcal{X}} xg'(x)dx = \int_a^b xg'(x)dx
= \Big[xg(x)\Big]_a^b - \int_a^b g(x)dx = -1,
\]
since $g$ is a probability density.
Consequently,
\[
\mathscr F(g')'(0) = -i(-1)= i \neq 0.
\]
Equating the derivatives at $t=0$ now yields
\[
0=\mathscr F(g')'(0)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)]=i\mathbb E[h(Y)],
\]
which forces $\mathbb E[h(Y)]=0$.

With $\mathbb E[h(Y)]=0$, \eqref{eq:Fourier_identity} immediately reduces to $e^{-it\,\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)]}\phi_h(t) \equiv 0$, which implies $\phi_h(t) \equiv 0$ for all $t \in \mathbb{R}$. Substituting the definition of $\phi_h$ and dividing by $it$ for $t \neq 0$, we have
\begin{equation}\label{eq:completeness_ortho}
\mathbb E\!\left[h(Y)\,e^{it(\bar{s}(Y)-\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)])}\right] = 0, \qquad \forall t \in \mathbb{R}.
\end{equation}

Now, let $\mu_{\bar{s}}$ denote the pushforward measure of $\bar{s}(Y)$. By the tower property of expectation, this implies
\[ 
\int_{\mathcal{X}} \mathbb E[h(Y)\mid \bar{s}(Y)=x]\,e^{it(x-\mathbb E[\bar{s}(Y)])}\,d\mu_{\bar{s}}(x) = 0 \qquad \forall t\in\mathbb R. 
\]
Define $u(x):=\mathbb E[h(Y)\mid \bar s(Y)=x]$, so that
\[
\mathbb E[h(Y)e^{it(\bar s(Y)-\mathbb E[\bar s(Y)])}]
= \int_{\mathcal{X}} u(x)\,e^{it(x-\mathbb E[\bar s(Y)])}\,d\mu_{\bar s}(x),
\qquad \forall t\in\mathbb R,
\]
where $\mu_{\bar s}$ denotes the pushforward measure of $\bar s(Y)$.
Hence,
\[
\int_{\mathcal{X}} u(x)\,e^{itx}\,d\mu_{\bar s}(x)=0,\qquad \forall t\in\mathbb R,
\]
which means that $u$ is orthogonal in $L^2(\mu_{\bar s})$ to the collection of
complex exponentials $\{e^{it(\cdot)}:t\in\mathbb R\}$.
Since $\mathcal{X}$ is compact, this collection is complete in $L^2(\mu_{\bar s})$
(by Lemma~\ref{lem:completeness}), and therefore $u(x)=0$ $\mu_{\bar s}$-a.e. Equivalently,
\[
\mathbb E[h(Y)\mid \bar s(Y)] \;=\; u(\bar s(Y)) \;=\; 0 \qquad \mu\text{-a.s.}
\]

Moreover, since $\bar s$ is a bijection by Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, it follows that $Y$ is a
measurable function of $\bar s(Y)$. This implies that $h(Y)$ is $\sigma(\bar s(Y))$-measurable.
Thus $\mathbb E[h(Y)\mid \bar s(Y)] = h(Y)$ $\mu$-a.s., and we conclude that
$h(Y)=0$ $\mu$-a.s. Since $Y\sim \mu$, this implies $h=0$ $\mu$-a.e, completing the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Isolation of Feasible Points in Finite Dimensions}
When the solution space $\mathcal{S}$ is restricted to a finite-dimensional approximation space $\mathcal{S}_n$ (as is necessary for any computational implementation), the injectivity of $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ dictates a highly rigid local geometry.
\begin{proposition}[Isolation of feasible points in finite-dimensional spaces]
\label{prop:isolated-feasible}
Suppose that $\mathcal{S}_n$ is a finite-dimensional Banach space of transport maps and that $Pf:\mathcal{S}_n\to \mathcal{Z}_0$ is continuously Fr\'echet differentiable. Let $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}_n$ be a feasible point. Then, $\bar{s}$ is an isolated feasible point in $\mathcal{S}_n$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
Let $A:=P f_{\bar{s}}\in \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S}_n,\mathcal{Z}_0)$. Since $\mathcal{S}_n$ is finite-dimensional, the unit sphere
\[
\mathbb S:=\{h\in\mathcal{S}_n:\|h\|=1\}
\]
is compact. The map $h\mapsto \|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}}$ is continuous on $\mathbb S$, and $A$ is injective, hence
\[
\sigma_{\min}(A):=\min_{h\in\mathbb S}\|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}>0,
\]
we have
\begin{align}\label{eqn:sigma-min}
\|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}\ge \sigma_{\min}(A)\|h\|\ \ \forall h\in\mathcal{S}_n.     
\end{align}
Next, by continuity of the derivative/first variation map $s\mapsto P f_s\in\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S}_n,\mathcal{Z}_0)$ at $\bar{s}$
(implied by the differentiability of $P f_s$),
there exists a neighborhood $W$ of $\bar{s}$ such that
\begin{align}\label{eqn:neigbourhood}
\|P f_s-A\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S}_n,\mathcal{Z})}\le \frac{\sigma_{\min}(A)}{2},
\qquad \forall s\in W,   
\end{align}
Now suppose we have another feasible $s\in W$ and set $h:=s-\bar{s}$.
By the integral mean value theorem for Fr\'echet differentiable maps
\[
0=P f(s)-P f(\bar{s})=\int_0^1 P f_{\bar{s}+t h}(h)\,dt.
\]
Add and subtract $A(h)$ inside the integral:
\[
0=\int_0^1\bigl(P f_{\bar{s}+t h}(h)-A(h)\bigr)\,dt+\int_0^1 A(h)\,dt,
\]
hence
\[
A(h)=-\int_0^1\bigl(P f_{\bar{s}+t h}-A\bigr)(h)\,dt.
\]
Taking norms and using (\ref{eqn:neigbourhood}),
\[
\|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}
\le \int_0^1 \|P f_{\bar{s}+t h}-A\|_{\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S}_n,\mathcal{Z})}\,\|h\|\,dt
\le \frac{\sigma_{\min}(A)}{2}\|h\|.
\]
Combining with (\ref{eqn:sigma-min}) yields
\[
\sigma_{\min}(A)\|h\|\le \|A(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}\le \frac{\sigma_{\min}(A)}{2}\|h\|,
\]
which is only possible if $\|h\|=0$, and therefore if $s=\bar{s}$. Thus $\bar{s}$ is an isolated feasible point.
\end{proof}

We stress that the proof of Proposition~\ref{prop:isolated-feasible} essentially depends on the finite-dimensionality of $\mathcal{S}_n$.
When $\dim(\mathcal{S}_n)<\infty$, the Rank--Nullity Theorem~\citep{hoffmann1971linear} implies that the range $R(Pf_{\bar{s}})$ is finite-dimensional, and hence closed by \cite{Rudin1974FunctionalAnalysis}[Theorem~1.21] (every finite-dimensional subspace of a normed space is closed).
Therefore, since $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ is also injective, \cite{hildebrand2012methods}[Theorem~8.6] yields that $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ has a strictly positive minimum modulus.
Equivalently, there exists $\sigma>0$ such that
\[
\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}\ge \sigma\|h\|\qquad \forall\,h\in\mathcal{S}_n.
\]
This lower bound is the key ingredient of the isolation of feasible points.

\subsection{Non-Isolation and Surjectivity in Infinite Dimensions}
In contrast, the isolated feasible points implication may fail in infinite-dimensional spaces where injectivity of $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ does not ensure that it is bounded below or that its range is closed.
Example~\ref{ex:infinite-counterexample} in Appendix~\ref{proof:loc} constructs a sequence $\{h_n\}\subset \mathcal{S}$ with $\|h_n\|=1$ but
$\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h_n)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}\to 0$, showing that the minimum modulus can be zero.
Consequently, in the infinite-dimensional setting one cannot infer isolation of feasible points from injectivity alone. 

When $\mathcal{S}$ is infinite dimensional, injectivity of $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ at a feasible $\bar{s}$ alone does not imply that the operator is bounded below. Consequently, the range of $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ need not be closed. However, by imposing natural structural and integrability conditions on the optimal map, we can recover not just a closed range, but full surjectivity onto $\mathcal{Z}_0$. This requires establishing two properties: adjoint injectivity (yielding a dense image) and a weighted Hardy inequality (yielding a closed range).

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:density-mean-zero}
Suppose Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}, \ref{ass:density-cont}, and \ref{ass:mono} hold, and that $\mathcal{S}$ contains functions from $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ to $\mathrm{supp}(g)$. Then the mean-zero elements of $\mathcal{S}$, 
\[ \mathcal{S}_0 := \{h \in \mathcal{S} : \mathbb{E}[h(Y)] = 0\}, \]
are dense in $L^2_0(\mu) := \{\psi \in L^2(\mu) : \mathbb{E}[\psi(Y)] = 0\}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $\psi \in L^2_0(\mu)$ and $\epsilon > 0$ be given. Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support} and \ref{ass:density-cont}, $\mu$ has a continuous and bounded density $f$ on the compact domain $\mathcal{X}$. Consequently, the norms $\|\cdot\|$ (the $L^2(\mu)$ norm) and $\|\cdot\|_{L^2(\mathcal{X})}$ are equivalent on bounded subsets. By the standard density of smooth functions in $L^2(\mathcal{X})$, there exists $\tilde{\psi} \in C^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ such that $\|\tilde{\psi} - \psi\| < \epsilon/2$.

Defining $\tilde{\psi}_0 := \tilde{\psi} - \mathbb{E}[\tilde{\psi}(Y)]$, we guarantee that $\tilde{\psi}_0 \in C^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ and $\mathbb{E}[\tilde{\psi}_0(Y)] = 0$, which places $\tilde{\psi}_0$ in $L^2_0(\mu)$. The approximation error is controlled by the triangle inequality:
\[
\|\tilde{\psi}_0 - \psi\| \le \|\tilde{\psi} - \psi\| + |\mathbb{E}[\tilde{\psi}(Y) - \psi(Y)]| \le 2\|\tilde{\psi} - \psi\| < \epsilon.
\]
To verify that $\tilde{\psi}_0$ is a valid transport direction at any reference point $s\in\mathcal{S}$, we consider the perturbed map $s_\delta := s + \delta \tilde{\psi}_0$ for some $\delta > 0$. By Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, $s'$ is bounded away from zero almost everywhere. Because $\tilde{\psi}_0 \in C^\infty$, its derivative is bounded. Therefore, for sufficiently small $\delta$, we have $|s_\delta'(y)| \ge c_0 - \delta\|\tilde{\psi}_0'\|_{L^\infty} > 0$ a.s., ensuring $s_\delta$ remains strictly monotone and satisfies Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}. Because $\mathbb{E}[\tilde{\psi}_0(Y)]=0$, we conclude $\tilde{\psi}_0 \in \mathcal{S}_0$. This establishes that $\mathcal{S}_0$ is dense in $L^2_0(\mu)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Adjoint Injectivity]\label{lem:adjoint-injectivity}
Suppose the Lagrange multiplier $[\lambda] \in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}$ satisfies $\lambda \in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X})$. Under the conditions of Lemma \ref{lem:density-mean-zero} and the conditions of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, the adjoint operator $Pf_{\bar{s}}^* : \mathcal{Z}_0^* \to \mathcal{S}^*$ is injective at feasible point $\bar{s}$. 
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Suppose $\lambda\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ (equivalently, $[\lambda] \in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}$) satisfies $Pf_{\bar{s}}^*(\lambda) = 0$, which requires $\int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x) Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)(x)dx = 0$ for all $h \in \mathcal{S}$. Restricting this identity to $h \in \mathcal{S}_0$, substituting the expression for $Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)$ from Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, and applying Fubini's theorem yields:
\[
\mathbb{E}\left[h(Y) \cdot \frac{1}{2\pi} \int_{\mathbb{R}} it \hat{\lambda}(t) e^{it\bar{s}(Y)}dt \right] = 0,
\]
where $\hat{\lambda}(t) = \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x)e^{-itx}dx$. Because $\lambda \in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X})$, $it\hat{\lambda}(t)$ acts as the Fourier transform of $\lambda'$ in the $L^2$ sense. The inner integral evaluates to $\lambda'(\bar{s}(Y))$, simplifying the condition to $\mathbb{E}[h(Y)\lambda'(\bar{s}(Y))] = 0$ for all $h \in \mathcal{S}_0$. 

By Lemma~\ref{lem:density-mean-zero}, $\mathcal{S}_0$ is dense in $L^2_0(\mu)$, implying that $\lambda'(\bar{s}(Y))$ is orthogonal to $L^2_0(\mu)$. This orthogonality forces $\lambda'(\bar{s}(Y)) = c_0$ almost surely for some constant $c_0 \in \mathbb{R}$. As $\bar{s}$ is surjective onto $\mathrm{supp}(g)$, $\lambda' = c_0$ almost everywhere on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$, integrating to $\lambda(x) = c_0 x + c_1$. 

To determine $c_0$, we extend the evaluation to any general $h \in \mathcal{S}$ with a non-zero mean. The variation condition simplifies to $\int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda(x) g'(x)dx = 0$. Substituting $\lambda(x) = c_0 x + c_1$ and integrating by parts on $\mathrm{supp}(g) = (a,b)$ yields:
\[
c_0 \left( [xg(x)]_a^b - \int_a^b g(x)dx \right) = c_0(0 - 1) = -c_0 = 0.
\]
Thus $c_0 = 0$, making $\lambda$ constant almost everywhere. Consequently, $[\lambda] = [0]$ in $\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}$, proving the injectivity of the adjoint operator.
\end{proof}

Having established adjoint injectivity, the remaining ingredient required for full surjectivity is ensuring the operator's range is closed. This corresponds to proving a strict lower bound, which we establish next via a weighted Hardy inequality reduction.

\begin{proposition}[Hardy inequality reduction]\label{prop:hardy-reduction}
Suppose Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}, \ref{ass:density-cont}, and \ref{ass:mono} hold. Let $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}$ be a reference transport map such that the ratio function $v(y) := f(y)/\bar{s}'(y)$ vanishes at the boundaries $\partial \mathrm{supp}(f)$, and the weight integrability condition $f/(\bar{s}')^2 \in L^1(\mathrm{supp}(f))$ is satisfied. Then the lower bound $\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0} \ge \sigma\|h\|$ for all $h\in\mathcal{S}_0$ is equivalent to the weighted Hardy inequality:
\[
\|(v h)'\|_{L^1(\mathrm{supp}(f))} \ge \sigma\|h\|.
\]
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, $\bar{s}$ is a strictly monotone bijection. Differentiating the change-of-variables formula $Pf(\bar{s})(x) = f(\bar{s}^{-1}(x))(\bar{s}^{-1})'(x)$ yields the first variation $Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)(\bar{s}(y)) = -\frac{1}{\bar{s}'(y)}\frac{d}{dy}[v(y)h(y)]$, where $v(y) := f(y)/\bar{s}'(y)$. Computing the $L^1$ norm via the substitution $x = \bar{s}(y)$ with $dx = \bar{s}'(y)dy$ provides:
\[
\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})} = \int_{\mathrm{supp}(g)} |Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)(x)|dx = \int_{\mathrm{supp}(f)} \left|\frac{(v h)'(y)}{\bar{s}'(y)}\right|\bar{s}'(y)dy = \|(v h)'\|_{L^1(\mathrm{supp}(f))}.
\]
By assumption, the function $v(y)$ vanishes at $\partial \mathrm{supp}(f)$. Because $h$ is bounded in our domain, the product $v h$ therefore strictly vanishes at the boundaries. This ensures that $vh$ belongs to the proper absolutely continuous space with zero boundary conditions, making the direct substitution valid and establishing the equivalence.
\end{proof}

We rely on the bilateral Hardy inequality of Opic and Kufner for functions vanishing at both endpoints, $AC_{LR}(c,d)$.
\begin{theorem}[Opic-Kufner \cite{opic1990hardy}]\label{thm:opic-kufner}
Let $(c,d) \subset \mathbb{R}$ be a bounded open interval and let $w$ be a positive measurable weight function. The inequality
\[
\left(\int_c^d |u(y)|^2 w(y) dy\right)^{1/2} \le C \int_c^d |u'(y)| dy \qquad \forall u \in AC_{LR}(c,d)
\]
holds with a finite constant $C>0$ if and only if the value
\[
\mathcal{B} := \sup_{c < \xi < \eta < d} \left(\int_\xi^\eta w(t)dt\right)^{1/2} \min(\xi-c, d-\eta)
\]
is finite.
\end{theorem}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:hardy}
For any feasible $s\in\mathcal{S}$, we have $\int_{\mathrm{supp}(f)} \frac{s'(y)^2}{f(y)}\,dy < \infty$.
\end{assumption}

\begin{lemma}[Reduction to $L^1$ integrability]\label{lem:L1-reduction}
With weight $w(y) = \bar{s}'(y)^2/f(y)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(f) = (c,d)$, Assumption~\ref{ass:hardy} implies the condition $\mathcal{B} < \infty$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $I := \int_c^d w(y)dy = \int_c^d \frac{\bar{s}'(y)^2}{f(y)}dy$. Because $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}$ is a feasible transport map, Assumption~\ref{ass:hardy} guarantees that $I < \infty$. For any $c < \xi < \eta < d$, the integral over the subinterval is bounded by the total integral: $\int_\xi^\eta w(t)dt \le I$. Furthermore, the distance to the boundaries is bounded by half the total interval length: $\min(\xi-c, d-\eta) \le \frac{d-c}{2}$. 
Taking the supremum over all such $\xi$ and $\eta$ yields:
\[
\mathcal{B} \le I^{1/2} \frac{d-c}{2} < \infty.
\]
Thus, the condition $\mathcal{B} < \infty$ is satisfied.
\end{proof}

Combining these components yields the complete geometric characterization of the functional constraint mapping.

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:pf-surjectivity}
Let $\bar{s} \in \mathcal{S}$ be a feasible transport map for (P) such that $Pf(\bar{s})=g$. Under the assumptions of Lemma~\ref{lem:adjoint-injectivity} and Assumption~\ref{ass:hardy}, the operator $Pf_{\bar{s}} : \mathcal{S} \to \mathcal{Z}_0$ is surjective. 
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
By Lemma~\ref{lem:adjoint-injectivity}, the adjoint operator $Pf_{\bar{s}}^* : \mathcal{Z}_0^* \to \mathcal{S}^*$ is injective. The Hahn-Banach theorem~\citep[Theorem 3.2]{Rudin1974FunctionalAnalysis} dictates that a bounded linear operator with an injective adjoint inherently possesses a dense image, establishing that $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\mathcal{S})$ is dense in $\mathcal{Z}_0$. 

Furthermore, by Proposition~\ref{prop:hardy-reduction}, the closed range lower bound is equivalent to the Hardy inequality on the space $AC_{LR}$. Under Assumption~\ref{ass:hardy}, Lemma~\ref{lem:L1-reduction} guarantees that the relevant Opic-Kufner constant satisfies $\mathcal{B} < \infty$. Applying Theorem~\ref{thm:opic-kufner} confirms the Hardy inequality holds. This provides the strict lower bound $\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0} \ge \sigma\|h\|$, confirming that $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ has a closed range in $\mathcal{Z}_0$.
