The combination of a dense image and a closed range proves that $Pf_{\bar{s}}$ is surjective, meaning $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\mathcal{S}) = \mathcal{Z}_0$. 
\end{proof}

\section{Computational Formulation and Consistency}\label{sec:cp}
%Since the infinite-dimensional problem (P) cannot be solved directly, 
Any implementable method must restrict the search to a finite-dimensional model of $\mathcal{S}_n$ and replace the functional constraint by finitely many constraints. In this section, we construct a computable approximate problem (CP) by (i) restricting $s$ to a finite-dimensional parameterization and (ii) discretizing the pushforward constraint by enforcing it on a finite grid.
This section specifies the problem formulation and states in which sense does (CP) approximate (P) as the discretization is refined.

\subsection{Approximation and Discretization}\label{subsec:approx}
Recall our original objective 
\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s\in\Omega} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s(x))f(x)dx \tag{P}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & Pf(s)-g =0_{\mathcal{Z}}.
\end{align*}
The above infinite-dimensional problem cannot be solved directly, in general. 

\subsubsection{Finite-Dimensional Function Space Approximation}
To make the problem computationally tractable, we first approximate the continuous transport map $s$ using finite-dimensional parameterizations. Let $\mathcal{S}_n \subset \mathcal{S}$ be an $n$-dimensional approximation space for the transport maps, where each map $s_n \in \mathcal{S}_n$ is uniquely determined by a parameter vector $a = (a_0, a_1, \dots, a_{n-1}) \in \mathbb{R}^n$. We define $\Omega_n:=\mathcal{S}_n\cap\Omega$, the set of admissible maps in the finite-dimensional approximation space. Two common and computationally convenient choices are described below.

\begin{example}{Polynomial approximation.}
Consider a transport map as a degree $n-1$ polynomial of the form
\begin{align*}
    s_n(x) = a_0 + a_1x + \cdots+ a_{n-1}x^{n-1},
\end{align*}
with parameter vector $a:=(a_0, a_1, \cdots, a_{n-1})\in\mathbb R^n$.
\end{example}

\begin{example}{Piecewise linear approximation.}
Consider a transport map as an $n$-piece piecewise linear function on $\mathcal{X}$. For a set of grid points $(x_0, \dots, x_n)$, let $a:= (a_0, \dots, a_{n-1})\in\mathbb R^n$ denote the map values at these locations. The approximated piecewise linear transport map is represented by:
\begin{align*}
    s_n(x) = \frac{a_{i+1}-a_i}{x_{i+1}-x_i}(x-x_i)+a_i,\quad x\in [x_i, x_{i+1}].
\end{align*}
\end{example}

\subsubsection{Constraint Discretization}
We next approximate the measure-preserving constraint by imposing it only at a finite collection of $m$ grid points: $(d_1, \dots, d_m)\in\mathbb{R}^m$. We evaluate the Perron--Frobenius operator associated with $s_n \in \mathcal{S}_n$ on each grid point. Hence, our constraints become:
\begin{align*}
    \tau_1^{(m)}(s_n) &= Pf(s_n)(d_1)-g(d_1) = 0,\\
    &\vdots\\
    \tau_m^{(m)}(s_n) &= Pf(s_n)(d_m)-g(d_m) = 0.
\end{align*}
The computational objective (CP) is:
\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s_n\in\Omega_n} J(s_n)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s_n(x)) f(x)dx \tag{CP}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & \tau_1^{(m)}(s_n) = \tau_2^{(m)}(s_n) =\cdots=\tau_m^{(m)}(s_n) = 0.
\end{align*}

For simplicity, denote $\tau^{(m)}(s_n) :=(\tau_1^{(m)}(s_n),\dots,\tau_m^{(m)}(s_n))$. The Jacobian of the constraints at $s_n$ is the matrix $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n)\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times n}$ with:
\[
\bigl(\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n)\bigr)_{i,j}=\frac{\partial \tau_i^{(m)}}{\partial a_j}(s_n).
\]

Let $s_n^*$ be a feasible point of (CP). 
If $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)$ has full row rank $m$ (i.e., LICQ holds at $s_n^*$, as shown in Proposition~\ref{prop:discrete-LICQ}), then the implicit function theorem implies that the feasible set
\[
\mathcal{F}_{nm}:=\{s_n\in\mathcal{S}_{n}:\ \tau^{(m)}(s_n)=0\}
\]
is locally an $(n-m)$-dimensional manifold through $s_n^*$.
In particular, $s_n^*$ can be isolated only if $n-m=0$, i.e., only in the square case $m=n$ where $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)$ is nonsingular.
When $m<n$, the Jacobian $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times n}$ has fewer rows than columns and therefore cannot be injective as a linear map from $\mathbb{R}^n$ to $\mathbb{R}^m$. 
In particular,
\[
\dim\ker(\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)) \;=\; n-\operatorname{rank}(\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)) \;\ge\; n-m \;>\; 0,
\]
so there exist nonzero directions $p$ such that $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)p=0$. 
These vectors are the first-order feasible directions for the discretized constraints, i.e., directions along which the linearly approximated constraints do not change.
Therefore, under LICQ and $m<n$, no feasible point of (CP) can be isolated.

This contrasts with the discussion in Section~\ref{sec:local-geometry}, where the first variation of the functional constraint in \textnormal{(P)} is injective at feasible points and, in finite-dimensional solution spaces, such injectivity leads to a feasible set that consists of isolated points.
For (CP), unless the discretization dimensions satisfy $m=n$ and $\nabla \tau^{(m)}(s_n^*)$ is nonsingular, the feasible set cannot consist of isolated points.
This introduces well-defined tangent directions along which the objective can be further decreased while staying feasible.

\subsection{Consistency of the Computational Objective}\label{subsec:comp_consistency}
% By \cite{philippis2013regularity}, for bounded open sets $X$ and $Y$ in $\mathbb R^d$, a set $\Gamma \subset X \times Y$ is $c$-cyclically monotone if:
% \begin{align*}
% \sum_{i=1}^m c(x_i, y_i) \leq \sum_{i=1}^m c(x_i, y_{\sigma(i)}), \quad \forall m \in \mathbb{N}, \, (x_i, y_i) \in \Gamma, \, \sigma \in \Sigma_h,
% \end{align*}
% where $\Sigma_h$ is the set of all permutations of $h$ elements. 
% A function $u : X \to \mathbb{R} \cup \{+\infty\}$ is $c$-convex if it can be written as:
% \begin{align*}
% u(x) = \sup_{y \in Y} \{-c(x, y) + \lambda_y\},
% \end{align*}
% for some constants $\lambda_y \in \mathbb{R} \cup \{-\infty\}$. 
% For a $c$-convex function $u : X \to \mathbb{R} \cup \{+\infty\}$, the $c$-subdifferential of $u$ at $x \in X$ is the (nonempty) set:
% \begin{align*}
% \partial_c u(x) := \{y \in Y : u(z) \geq -c(z, y) + c(x, y) + u(x), \, \forall z \in X\}.
% \end{align*}

% \begin{remark}[Rockafellar Theorem]\label{thm:Rockafellar}\cite{philippis2013regularity}
% A set $\Gamma\subset X$ is c-cyclically monotone if and only if it is included in the c-subdifferential of a c-convex function.   
% \end{remark}
We begin by recording existence and uniqueness results for the Monge problem (P), as well as an almost-everywhere bijectivity characterization of the optimal transport map.
Since our consistency analysis compares the computational objective (CP) to the true objective (P), we first ensure that (P) admits a well-defined, unique optimizer $s^*$.

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:philippis-twist}
For each fixed $y\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$, the map $x\mapsto D_x c(x,y)$ is injective, and for each fixed
$x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)$, the map $y\mapsto D_y c(x,y)$ is injective. % Use D_yc(x,y) for c'
\end{assumption}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:philippis-nondeg}
The mixed derivative satisfies
\[
D^2_{xy}c(x,y)\neq 0, \qquad \forall\, x\in \mathrm{supp}(f),\ \forall\, y\in \mathrm{supp}(g).
\]
\end{assumption}

\begin{assumption}\label{ass:philippis-Calpha}
The cost satisfies
\[
c \in C^{2,\alpha}_{\mathrm{loc}}\!\big(\mathrm{supp}(f)\times \mathrm{supp}(g)\big) \quad\text{for some }\alpha\in(0,1).
\]
\end{assumption}

\begin{theorem}[\cite{philippis2013regularity}, Theorem 1.28]\label{thm:philippis}
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}, \ref{ass:density-cont}, \ref{ass:philippis-C2}, and \ref{ass:philippis-twist},
the Monge problem (P) admits a unique optimal transport map $s^*$ from $f$ to $g$ (unique $\mu$--a.e.).
Moreover, under the additional regularity Assumptions~\ref{ass:philippis-nondeg}--\ref{ass:philippis-Calpha},
there exist relatively closed sets $\Sigma_f\subset \mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\Sigma_g\subset \mathrm{supp}(g)$ of measure zero such that
\[
s^*:\mathrm{supp}(f)\setminus \Sigma_f \ \longrightarrow\ \mathrm{supp}(g)\setminus \Sigma_g
\]
is a bijection of class $C^{1,\alpha}_{\mathrm{loc}}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[\cite{GangboMcCann1996}, Theorem 4.5]\label{thm:gangbo-mccann}
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:domain-support} alone, assume further that the cost is of the transportation form
\[
c(x,y)=\tilde c(x-y),
\]
where $\tilde c:\mathbb R\to[0,\infty)$ is strictly convex. Then the Monge problem (P) admits a unique optimal transport map
$s^*$ (unique $f$--a.e.) pushing $f$ forward to $g$. Moreover, letting $t^*$ denote the optimal transport map of the reversed Monge problem
(from $g$ to $f$), one has
\[
t^*(s^*(x)) = x \quad f\text{--a.e.}, 
\qquad 
s^*(t^*(y)) = y \quad g\text{--a.e.}.
\]
In particular, $s^*$ is bijective between full-measure subsets of $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
\end{theorem}

Under conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, the Monge problem (P) admits a unique optimal transport map $s^*$ (unique $f$--a.e.) pushing $f$ forward to $g$,
and $s^*$ is bijective between full-measure subsets of $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.

With the well-posedness of (P) stated above,
%we can cleanly separate the two sources of approximation in (CP): the restriction of the transport maps to a finite-dimensional subspace of $\mathcal{S}$, and replacing the functional pushforward constraints by finitely many grid constraints. 
%This leads naturally to
we now consider the intermediate problems (CP-T) and (CP-C) below, each isolating one source of approximation error, to enable a consistency analysis of (CP) $\to$ (P) as the discretization is refined. 

\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s_n \in\Omega_n} J(s_n)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x, s_n(x)) f(x)dx \tag{CP-T}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & Pf(s_n)-g =0_{\mathcal{Z}}.
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
    &\min_{s\in\Omega} J(s)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x,s(x))f(x)dx \tag{CP-C}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & \tau^{(m)}_1(s) = \tau^{(m)}_2(s) =\cdots=\tau^{(m)}_m(s) = 0.
\end{align*}

Denote the feasible set of (P), (CP-T), (CP-C), and (CP) (resp.) as $\mathcal{F}$, $\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$, $\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$ and $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ (resp.). 
Let their solutions be $s^*$, $s_{n\infty}^*$, $s_{\infty m}^*$ and $s_{nm}^*$ (resp.).

\begin{theorem}[Consistency of the Computational Objective]\label{thm:consistency}
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}--\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz},
\ref{ass:mono}, and the conditions of either
Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, the
following hold:
\begin{enumerate}[label={\textnormal{(\roman*)}},itemindent=1em]
    \item\label{thm:consistency-obj}
    \[
    J(s_{nm}^*) \xrightarrow[n,m\to\infty]{} J(s^*).
    \]
    \item\label{thm:consistency-map}
    \[
    s_{nm}^* \xrightarrow[n,m\to\infty]{} s^*
    \qquad \mu\text{--a.e.}
    \]
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We first prove \ref{thm:consistency-obj}. This follows from the two
intermediate consistency results:
\begin{enumerate}[label={(\arabic*)},itemindent=1em]
    \item\label{consistency1}
    \[
    J(s_{nm}^*) \xrightarrow[n\to\infty]{} J(s_{\infty m}^*)
    \quad\text{uniformly in } m
    \]
    (Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc} for piecewise linear
    $\mathcal S_n$, and
    Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc-poly} for polynomial
    $\mathcal S_n$);
    \item\label{consistency2}
    \[
    J(s_{\infty m}^*) \xrightarrow[m\to\infty]{} J(s^*)
    \]
    (Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}%
    \ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp2}).
\end{enumerate}
Step~\ref{consistency1} shows that for each fixed $m$, the limit
$\lim_{n\to\infty}J(s_{nm}^*)$ exists and equals $J(s_{\infty m}^*)$,
and that this convergence is uniform in $m$. Step~\ref{consistency2}
identifies the remaining outer limit. Therefore, by the
Moore--Osgood theorem \citep[Theorem~7.11]{rudin1976principles},
\[
J(s_{nm}^*) \xrightarrow[n,m\to\infty]{} J(s^*).
\]

We next prove \ref{thm:consistency-map}. The argument is by sequential compactness and uniqueness of the minimizer.
By Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, each $s_{nm}^*$ is monotone on
$\mathrm{supp}(f)=[c,d]$ and takes values in the compact interval
$\mathrm{supp}(g)=[a,b]$. Hence $\{s_{nm}^*\}$ is a uniformly bounded
family of monotone functions on $[c,d]$. By the monotone function
special case of Helly's selection theorem
\citep[Chapter~10, Theorem~5]{kolmogorov1970intro}, every sequence in $\{s_{nm}^*\}$
admits a subsequence $\{s_{n_km_k}^*\}$, such that
\[
s_{n_km_k}^*(x)\to \bar s(x)
\qquad\text{for every }x\in[c,d],
\]
for some monotone function $\bar s:[c,d]\to[a,b]$.

Since the feasible sets $\mathcal F_{nm}$ are nested and their limit
recovers the feasible set of the original problem
(Theorem~\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv}), any such subsequential limit is
feasible, i.e.,
\[
\bar s\in\mathcal F.
\]

We now pass to the limit in the objective functional along this
subsequence. For each $x\in[c,d]$, continuity of the cost in its second
argument and the pointwise convergence
$s_{n_km_k}^*(x)\to \bar s(x)$ imply
\[
c(x,s_{n_km_k}^*(x))\,f(x)\to c(x,\bar s(x))\,f(x).
\]
Moreover, since all values of $s_{n_km_k}^*$ and $\bar s$ lie in
$[a,b]$, Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz} yields the uniform bound
\[
|c(x,s_{n_km_k}^*(x))|
\le |c(x,a)| + K|s_{n_km_k}^*(x)-a|
\le \sup_{x\in[c,d]}|c(x,a)| + K(b-a),
\]
and therefore
\[
|c(x,s_{n_km_k}^*(x))\,f(x)|
\le \Big(\sup_{x\in[c,d]}|c(x,a)| + K(b-a)\Big) f(x).
\]
The dominating function on the right is integrable because
$f$ is a probability density on the compact interval $[c,d]$.
Hence the dominated convergence theorem gives
\[
J(s_{n_km_k}^*) \to J(\bar s).
\]

On the other hand, \ref{thm:consistency-obj} implies
\[
J(s_{n_km_k}^*) \to J(s^*).
\]
Therefore
\[
J(\bar s)=J(s^*).
\]
Since $s^*$ is the unique minimizer of $(P)$ over $\mathcal F$
under either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or
Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, it follows that
\[
\bar s = s^*
\qquad \mu\text{--a.e.}
\]

We have thus shown that every subsequence of $\{s_{nm}^*\}$ admits a
further subsequence converging to $s^*$ $\mu$--a.e. Hence the full
family converges:
\[
s_{nm}^* \xrightarrow[n,m\to\infty]{} s^*
\qquad \mu\text{--a.e.}
\]
This proves \ref{thm:consistency-map}.
\end{proof}


\begin{comment}
\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: obj-convex} 
If the cost function $c(x, y)$ is convex, then the objective functional $J(\cdot)$ is convex on $\mathcal{S}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof} 
For any $s_1, s_2\in\mathcal{S}$ and $0\leq\alpha\leq 1$, we have
\begin{align*}
    &J(\alpha s_1 + (1-\alpha) s_2)\\
    =&\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(x,(\alpha s_1(x) + (1-\alpha) s_2(x)))f(x)dx\\
    =&\int_{\mathcal{X}} c(\alpha x + (1-\alpha)x-(\alpha s_1(x) + (1-\alpha) s_2(x)))f(x)dx\\
    \leq&\int_{\mathcal{X}}\alpha c(x,s_1(x)) + (1-\alpha)c(x,s_2(x)) f(x)dx\\
    =&\alpha J(s_1) + (1-\alpha)J(s_2).
\end{align*}

\end{proof}
\end{comment}


\begin{proposition}~\label{prop: obj-continuity} 
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:domain-support} and Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, the objective functional $J(\cdot)$ is K-Lipschitz on $\mathcal{S}$ under the supremum norm $\|\cdot\|_{\infty}$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
For $s_1$, $s_2\in\mathcal{S}$, we have
    \begin{align*}
        &\left|J(s_1)-J(s_2)\right|\\
        =&\left|\int_{\mathcal{X}} \left(c(x, s_1(x))-c(x, s_2(x))\right) f(x)dx\right|\\
        \leq&\int_{\mathcal{X}} \left|c(x, s_1(x))-c(x, s_2(x))\right| f(x)dx\\
        \leq&\int_{\mathcal{X}} K\left|s_1(x)-s_2(x)\right| f(x)dx\\
        \leq&K\cdot\|s_1-s_2\|_{\infty}.
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: feasible-set-conv} 
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:density-cont} and Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, we have the following:
\begin{enumerate}[label={(\roman*)},itemindent=1em]
\item For any fixed $n>0$, there exists a sequence of sets $\{\mathcal{F}_{nm}: m \geq 1\}$ such that, let $\cap_{m\geq 1} \mathcal{F}_{nm}:= \tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}$, then $ \tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty} = \mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$.\label{thm: feasible-set-conv1} 
\item There exists a sequence sets $\{\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}: m \geq 1\}$ such that, let $\cap_{m\geq 1} \mathcal{F}_{\infty m}:= \tilde{\mathcal{F}}$, then $ \tilde{\mathcal{F}} = \mathcal{F}$. \label{thm: feasible-set-conv2} 
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
To show \ref{thm: feasible-set-conv1}, denote $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ as $[a, b]$. Note that all connected sets in $\mathbb R$ are intervals, therefore both $\mathrm{supp}(f)$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ are intervals by Assumption~\ref{ass:density-cont}. Let $\{\mathcal{P}_m\}$ a sequence of partitions of $[a, b]$ such that $\mathcal{P}_1\subseteq\mathcal{P}_2\subseteq\cdots$, and $\lim_{m\rightarrow\infty}\mathcal{P}_m=\mathcal{D}$ where $\mathcal{D}\subset \mathrm{supp}(g)$ is dense in $\mathrm{supp}(g)$. For the fixed $n>0$, Let $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ be the feasible set for (CP) with $n$ parameters for the transport map and $m$ number of constraints whose grid is defined by $\mathcal{P}_m$. Hence, $\{\mathcal{F}_{nm}: m \geq 1\}$ is a monotone non-increasing sequence of sets, i.e., $\mathcal{F}_{n1}\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n2}\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n3},\cdots$. Let $\tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}:=\cap_{m\rightarrow\infty}\mathcal{F}_{nm}$. $\mathcal{F}_{nm} \supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$ for each $m$, therefore, $ \tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$. We further show $\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}=\tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}$ by showing $\tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}\setminus\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}=\emptyset$.
By Lemma~\ref{lem:pf-continuous-version} in Appendix~\ref{sec:pf-regularity}, under Assumption~\ref{ass:density-cont} and Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, the function $Pf(s)$ admits a continuous version on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$, which we identify with $Pf(s)$ throughout. Two continuous functions that agree on a dense subset of $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ must also agree on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$. Therefore, there is no such element $s\in\tilde{\mathcal{F}}_{n\infty}\setminus\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$ such that $Pf(s)$ satisfies the density constraint only on a dense subset of $\mathrm{supp}(g)$, hence we complete the proof.

Now we show \ref{thm: feasible-set-conv2}. With the same sequence of partitions $\{\mathcal{P}_m\}$ on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ that goes to a dense set $\mathcal{D}$, let $\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$ be the feasible set of (CP-C) with $m$ number of density constraints on grid points defined by $\mathcal{P}_m$. It is clear that $\{\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}\}$ is also a monotone non-increasing sequence of sets such that $\mathcal{F}_{\infty 1}\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{\infty 1}\supseteq\cdots$. Let $\tilde{\mathcal{F}}:=  \cap_{m\rightarrow\infty}\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$, $\mathcal{F}_{\infty  m} \supseteq \mathcal{F}$ for each $m$, thus $ \tilde{\mathcal{F}}\supseteq \mathcal{F}$. Similarly as in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv}\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv1}, we can argue that $\tilde{\mathcal{F}}\setminus\mathcal{F}=\emptyset$ since two continuous functions that agree on a dense subset of $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ must also agree on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp} 
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}--\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, \ref{ass:mono}, and the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, we have the following:
\begin{enumerate}[label={(\roman*)},itemindent=1em]
\item For a fixed $n>0$,  $J(s_{nm}^*)\rightarrow J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ as $m\rightarrow\infty$.\label{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp1} 
\item $J(s_{\infty m}^*)\rightarrow J(s^*)$ as $m\rightarrow\infty$. \label{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp2} 
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof} 
We begin by showing \ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp1}. Clearly, we have $\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}\subset\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ because any transport map that satisfies the PF constraint would satisfy the set of discretized PF constraints. $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ is non-increasing in $m$, so $J(\mathcal{F}_{nm})$ is non-decreasing in $m$. Therefore, for any fixed $n>0$, we have $J(s_{n\infty}^*)\geq J(s_{nm}^*)\quad\forall m >0$. 
To show that $J(s_{nm}^*)\rightarrow J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ as $m\rightarrow\infty$, we are left with the other direction, that is $\lim_{m\rightarrow\infty} J(s_{nm}^*)\geq J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ for all $n>0$.
   
For a fixed $n>0$, let $s_{nm}^*\in \mathcal{F}_{nm}$ be the solution to (CP) with $m$ constraints. $\{s_{nm}^*\}$ is then a sequence indexed by $m$. Note that the objective function value $J(\cdot)$ is bounded over $\mathcal{S}$ because the integral of a bounded function $c(x, y)$ with respect to a density function must also be bounded. Hence, that $J(s_{nm}^*)$ converges as  $m\rightarrow\infty$ because it is a bounded non-decreasing sequence in $m$. Let $V_{n\infty}^*:=\lim_{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{nm}^*)$. As mentioned above, we need to show that $V_{n\infty}^*\geq J(s_{n\infty}^*)$. By Theorem~\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv}\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv1}, we know that for any $\epsilon>0$, we can choose $M_\epsilon$ such that the Hausdorff distance between $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ and $\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$ on the metric space $(\mathcal{S}, \|\cdot\|_{\infty})$ is smaller than $\epsilon$ for $m>M_\epsilon$. By the K-Lipschitz continuity of the objective functional $J(\cdot)$ shown in Proposition~\ref{prop: obj-continuity}, we know that for any $\epsilon>0$, there also exists a $\tilde{M}_{\epsilon}>0$ such that a point $\tilde{s}_{nm}^* \in\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$ can be  found with $|J(\tilde{s}_{nm}^*)  - J(s_{nm}^*)|<\epsilon$ for all $m\geq \tilde{M}_{\epsilon}$. Now, suppose $\lim_{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{nm}^*) =V_{n\infty}^* < J(s_{n\infty}^*)$. Set $\epsilon = J(s_{n\infty}^*)-V_{n\infty}^*$, then, $J(\tilde{s}_{nm}^*)< J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ for $m > \tilde{M}_{\epsilon},$ 
which leads to a contradiction to the definition of $s_{n\infty}^*$.
Therefore, we have
\[\lim_{m\rightarrow\infty} J(s_{nm}^*)=V_{n\infty}^*\geq J(s_{n\infty}^*),
\]
for a fixed $n>0$.
So far, we have shown both directions and established 
$J(s_{nm}^*)\rightarrow J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ as $m\rightarrow\infty$.
 
The proof of \ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp2} follows from Theorem~\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv}\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv2}, similarly to the proof of \ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp1}. Here we consider $s_{\infty m}^*\in\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$ in place of $s_{nm}^*\in\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ and $s^*\in\mathcal{F}$ in place of $s_{n\infty}^*\in\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$.
\end{proof}


\begin{theorem}~\label{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc} 
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}--\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, \ref{ass:mono}, the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, and assuming $\mathcal{S}_n$ is the space of continuous piecewise linear functions, $J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow{n\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ uniformly in $m$.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
We first show the point-wise convergence of $J(s_{nm}^*)$ to $J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ fixing a $m\geq 1$. We know that $J(s_{\infty m}^*)\leq J(s_{nm}^*)$ for all $n>0$ because they are the optimal objective functional values of the same problem over the space $\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$ and $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ respectively, and $\mathcal{F}_{1m}\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{2m}\subseteq\cdots\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$. Now we show the other direction, i.e., $J(s_{\infty m}^*)\geq \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{nm}^*)$. Let $\mathrm{supp}(g)= [a,b]$ and $\mathrm{supp}(f)= [c,d]$. For $s_1, s_2\in\mathcal{S}$, assume without loss of generality that $s_1, s_2$ are both increasing, we have

\begin{align*}
    &\left|Pf(s_1)(x) - Pf(s_2)(x)\right|\\
    =&\left|\frac{d}{dx}\int_{s_1^{-1}((a, x])} f(y)dy - \frac{d}{dx}\int_{s_2^{-1}((a, x])} f(y)dy\right|\\
    =&\left|\frac{d}{dx}\int_{s_1^{-1}(a)}^{s_1^{-1}(x)} f(y)dy - \frac{d}{dx}\int_{s_2^{-1}(a)}^{s_2^{-1}(x)} f(y)dy\right|\\
    =&\left|f(s_1^{-1}(x))(s_1^{-1})'(x) - f(s_2^{-1}(x))(s_2^{-1})'(x) \right|,
\end{align*}
where the last equality is by Leibniz rule. We know from the last equation that for any $x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)$, if $s_1^{-1}$ and $s_2^{-1}$ have the same function values, and the same first derivatives at $x$, then $\left|Pf(s_1)(x) - Pf(s_2)(x)\right|=0$. For $s_{\infty m}^*\in \mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$, we can always construct a sequence of continuous piecewise linear functions $\{\hat{s}^*_{nm}\}\in\mathcal{S}_n$ in the way that for any $\epsilon>0$, there exists a $N_{\epsilon}$ such that if $n>N_{\epsilon}$, we have 
$\|s_{\infty m}^*-\hat{s}^*_{nm}\|_{\infty}<\epsilon$ and $\left|Pf(s_1) - Pf(s_2)\right|=0$ on $m$ grid points associated with the $m$ constraints, namely, $\hat{s}^*_{nm}\in \mathcal{F}_{nm}$. It is well known that the space of piecewise linear function is dense in the space of continuous functions. Therefore, a piecewise linear continuous approximation of  $s_{\infty m}^*$ that uses the first-order approximation in a small neighborhood around each grid point would suffice.
With such $\hat{s}^*_{nm}$, we have
   \begin{align*}
       J(s_{\infty m}^*) &= \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{nm}) + (J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{nm}))\\
       &= \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{nm}) + \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} (J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{nm}))\\
       &=\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{nm})\\
       &\geq \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(s_{nm}^*).
   \end{align*}
The last inequality is due to the definition of $s_{nm}^*$ and the penultimate equation is by Proposition~\ref{prop: obj-continuity}, specifically,
\begin{align*}
    &\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{nm})|\\
    &\leq\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}K\|s_{nm}^*-\hat{s}^*_{nm}\|_{\infty}\\
    &=0.
\end{align*}
Therefore, $\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}(J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*))=0$ for a fixed $m$.

To show the uniform convergence of $J(s_{nm}^*)$, we use Dini's theorem. Recall that $J(s_{1m}^*)\geq J(s_{2m}^*)\geq\cdots\geq J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ because $\mathcal{F}_{1m}\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{2m}\subseteq\cdots\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$. Now let 
\begin{align*}
l_n(x)
:=
\begin{cases}
J\!\left(s_{n\frac{1}{x}}^*\right), & x \in \left\{1,\frac12,\frac13,\ldots\right\},\\[4pt]
J\!\left(s_{n\infty}^*\right), & x=0,\\[8pt]
J\!\left(s_{n1}^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-1}{\frac12-1}\Big(J\!\left(s_{n2}^*\right)-J\!\left(s_{n1}^*\right)\Big),
& \frac12 < x < 1,\\[10pt]
J\!\left(s_{n i}^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-\frac{1}{i}}{\frac{1}{i-1}-\frac{1}{i}}
\Big(J\!\left(s_{n,i-1}^*\right)-J\!\left(s_{n i}^*\right)\Big),
& \frac{1}{i} < x < \frac{1}{i-1},\quad i\ge 3,\\[12pt]
J\!\left(s_{n\infty}^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-0}{\frac12-0}\Big(J\!\left(s_{n2}^*\right)-J\!\left(s_{n\infty}^*\right)\Big),
& 0 < x < \frac12.
\end{cases}
\end{align*}

\begin{align*}
l(x)
:=
\begin{cases}
J\!\left(s_{\infty\frac{1}{x}}^*\right), & x \in \left\{1,\frac12,\frac13,\ldots\right\},\\[4pt]
J\!\left(s^*\right), & x=0,\\[8pt]
J\!\left(s_{\infty 1}^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-1}{\frac12-1}\Big(J\!\left(s_{\infty 2}^*\right)-J\!\left(s_{\infty 1}^*\right)\Big),
& \frac12 < x < 1,\\[10pt]
J\!\left(s_{\infty i}^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-\frac{1}{i}}{\frac{1}{i-1}-\frac{1}{i}}
\Big(J\!\left(s_{\infty,i-1}^*\right)-J\!\left(s_{\infty i}^*\right)\Big),
& \frac{1}{i} < x < \frac{1}{i-1},\quad i\ge 3,\\[12pt]
J\!\left(s^*\right)
+ \dfrac{x-0}{\frac12-0}\Big(J\!\left(s_{\infty 2}^*\right)-J\!\left(s^*\right)\Big),
& 0 < x < \frac12.
\end{cases}
\end{align*}
Now note that for any $x\in [0, 1]$, it is true that $l_1(x) \geq l_2(x)\geq\cdots\geq l(x)$. That is, $l_n$ is point-wise convergent to $l$ monotonically. By construction, $l_n$ and $l$ are continuous on $(0,1]$. By Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp1}, $J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ for a fixed $n$, hence $l_n(x)$ is right continuous at $0$. Similarly, $l(x)$ is also right continuous at $0$ because $J(s_{\infty m}^*)\xrightarrow{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s^*)$ by Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp2}. Therefore, $l_n\rightarrow l$ uniformly on $[0, 1]$ by Dini's Theorem. This implies $J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow{n\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ uniformly in $m$.
\end{proof}

\begin{comment}
\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: cpt-conv-to-nlp} 
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:domain-support} and Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, $J(s_{n\infty}^*)\rightarrow J(s^*)$ as $n\rightarrow\infty$. 
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof} 
We know that $J(s^*)\leq J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ for all $n>0$, because $\mathcal{F}_{1\infty}\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{2\infty}\subseteq\cdots\subseteq\mathcal{F}$. It is well known that the space of piecewise linear function is dense in $\mathcal{F}$, therefore, we have that $\cup _{n=1}^{\infty}\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}=\mathcal{F}$. Denote the closest point in $\mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$ of $s^*$ as $\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}$, we also have
   \begin{align*}
       J(s^*) &= \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}) + (J(s^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}))\\
       &= \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}) + \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} (J(s^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}))\\
       &=\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty})\\
       &\geq \lim_{n\rightarrow\infty} J(s_{n\infty}^*).
   \end{align*}
The last inequality is due to the definition of $s_{n\infty}^*$ and the penultimate equation is by Proposition~\ref{prop: obj-continuity}, specifically,
\begin{align*}
    &\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}|J(s^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty})|\\
    &\leq\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}K|s^*-\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}|_{\infty}\\
    &=0.
\end{align*}
Therefore, $\lim_{n\rightarrow\infty}(J(s^*)-J(\hat{s}^*_{n\infty}))=0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-uniform} 
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:domain-support} and Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, $J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ uniformly in $n$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
To show that the convergence is uniform in $n$, recall that $\{\mathcal{F}_{nm}: m \geq 1\}$ is a monotone non-increasing sequence of sets such that $\mathcal{F}_{n1}\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n2}\supseteq\cdots\supseteq \mathcal{F}_{n\infty}$. This gives us $J(s_{n1}^*)\leq J(s_{n2}^*)\leq\cdots\leq J(s_{n\infty}^*)$ which is again a monotone sequence of functions that converges point-wise for any fixed $n$. Using a similar construction in Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc} and Dini's theorem, uniform convergence follows.    
\end{proof}
\end{comment}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc-poly}
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}--\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, \ref{ass:mono}, the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, and assuming
$\mathcal{S}_n$ is the intersection of $\mathcal{S}$ and the space of real polynomials on
$\mathrm{supp}(f)$ of degree at most $n-1$, then $J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow{n\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ uniformly in $m$.
\end{theorem}
assuming $\mathcal{S}_n=\mathcal{S}\cap\mathbb P_{n-1}$, where
$\mathbb P_{n-1}$ denotes the space of real polynomials on
$\mathrm{supp}(f)$ of degree at most $n-1$, we have
\[
J(s_{nm}^*) \xrightarrow[n\to\infty]{} J(s_{\infty m}^*)
\qquad\text{uniformly in }m.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Let $\mathrm{supp}(f)=[c,d]$ and $\mathrm{supp}(g)=[a,b]$, and denote
by $\mathbb P_k$ the space of real polynomials of degree at most $k$
on $[c,d]$.

We first prove pointwise convergence in $n$ for each fixed $m$, and
then upgrade to uniform convergence in $m$ by Dini's theorem, exactly
as in Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc}.

For each fixed $m\ge 1$,
\[
\mathcal F_{1m}\subseteq \mathcal F_{2m}\subseteq \cdots
\subseteq \mathcal F_{\infty m},
\]
hence
\[
J(s_{\infty m}^*)\le J(s_{nm}^*)\qquad \forall n\ge 1.
\]
Therefore it suffices to show
\[
\limsup_{n\to\infty}J(s_{nm}^*)\le J(s_{\infty m}^*).
\]

Fix $m\ge 1$, and let $d_1,\dots,d_m\in\mathrm{supp}(g)$ be the $m$
constraint grid points. Define
\[
y_j:=(s_{\infty m}^*)^{-1}(d_j),\qquad j=1,\dots,m.
\]
By Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, $s_{\infty m}^*$ is monotone, so its
inverse is well-defined, and the one-dimensional change-of-variables
formula gives
\begin{equation}\label{eq:pf-cov-poly-proof}
g(d_j)
=Pf(s_{\infty m}^*)(d_j)
=\frac{f(y_j)}{(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)},
\qquad j=1,\dots,m.
\end{equation}

We now construct a polynomial approximation that is feasible for the
$m$ discretized constraints. The idea is as follows. We first choose a
polynomial $q_n$ that approximates $s_{\infty m}^*$ and its derivative
globally on $[c,d]$. This polynomial need not satisfy the $m$
discrete constraints exactly. We then amend $q_n$ by adding a small
Hermite interpolation correction $r_n$, chosen so that the corrected
polynomial
\[
\hat s_{nm}:=q_n+r_n
\]
matches both the values and the derivatives of $s_{\infty m}^*$ at the
special nodes $y_1,\dots,y_m$. This guarantees that the discrete
constraints are satisfied exactly, while the correction remains small
because the value and derivative mismatches of $q_n$ at those nodes go
to zero.

By the Weierstrass approximation theorem
\citep[Theorem~7.26]{rudin1976principles}, there exists a sequence of
polynomials $p_n\in\mathbb P_{n-2}$ such that
\[
\|p_n-(s_{\infty m}^*)'\|_\infty\to 0.
\]
Define
\[
q_n(x):=s_{\infty m}^*(c)+\int_c^x p_n(t)\,dt.
\]
Then $q_n\in\mathbb P_{n-1}$, $q_n'=p_n$, and
\[
\|q_n-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty
=
\sup_{x\in[c,d]}
\left|\int_c^x\bigl(p_n(t)-(s_{\infty m}^*)'(t)\bigr)\,dt\right|
\le
(d-c)\,\|p_n-(s_{\infty m}^*)'\|_\infty \to 0.
\]
Hence
\[
\|q_n-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty
+
\|q_n'-(s_{\infty m}^*)'\|_\infty
\to 0.
\]

We now correct $q_n$ at the finitely many nodes
$y_1,\dots,y_m$. By the classical first-order Hermite interpolation
theorem, for any prescribed values and first derivatives at $m$
distinct nodes, there exists a unique interpolating polynomial in
$\mathbb P_{2m-1}$; equivalently, there exist basis polynomials
$\{A_j,B_j\}_{j=1}^m\subset \mathbb P_{2m-1}$ such that ~\cite[Section 8.2]{hildebrand1987introduction}
\[
A_j(y_i)=\delta_{ij},\quad A_j'(y_i)=0,
\qquad
B_j(y_i)=0,\quad B_j'(y_i)=\delta_{ij},
\qquad i,j=1,\dots,m,
\]
where $\delta_{ij}=1$ if $i=j$ and $\delta_{ij}=0$ otherwise. 

The role of these basis polynomials are: $A_j$ corrects only the
function value at the node $y_j$, while $B_j$ corrects only the
derivative at the node $y_j$. Therefore, if we define
\[
r_n(y)
:=
\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl(d_j-q_n(y_j)\bigr)A_j(y)
+
\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl((s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)-q_n'(y_j)\bigr)B_j(y),
\]
and set
\[
\hat s_{nm}:=q_n+r_n,
\]
then $\hat s_{nm}$ agrees with $s_{\infty m}^*$ in both value and
slope at each node $y_j$.

Indeed, by the defining properties of the Hermite basis,
\begin{align*}
\hat s_{nm}(y_i)
&=
q_n(y_i)
+\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl(d_j-q_n(y_j)\bigr)A_j(y_i)
+\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl((s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)-q_n'(y_j)\bigr)B_j(y_i)\\
&=
q_n(y_i)+\bigl(d_i-q_n(y_i)\bigr)=d_i,
\end{align*}
and similarly
\begin{align*}
\hat s_{nm}'(y_i)
&=
q_n'(y_i)
+\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl(d_j-q_n(y_j)\bigr)A_j'(y_i)
+\sum_{j=1}^m\bigl((s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)-q_n'(y_j)\bigr)B_j'(y_i)\\
&=
q_n'(y_i)+\bigl((s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_i)-q_n'(y_i)\bigr)
=(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_i).
\end{align*}
Thus
\begin{equation}\label{eq:hermite-match-poly-proof}
\hat s_{nm}(y_j)=d_j=s_{\infty m}^*(y_j),
\qquad
\hat s_{nm}'(y_j)=(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j),
\qquad j=1,\dots,m.
\end{equation}

Since $q_n\in\mathbb P_{n-1}$ and $r_n\in\mathbb P_{2m-1}$, we have
$\hat s_{nm}\in\mathbb P_{n-1}$ whenever $n\ge 2m$.

Next we quantify the size of the correction $r_n$. For fixed $m$ and
fixed nodes $\{y_j\}_{j=1}^m$, the Hermite basis polynomials
$A_j,B_j$ are fixed continuous functions on the compact interval
$[c,d]$. Hence their sup norms, and the sup norms of their
derivatives, are finite. Define
\begin{equation}\label{eq:C_m}
C_m
:=
\sum_{j=1}^m
\Bigl(
\|A_j\|_\infty+\|A_j'\|_\infty+\|B_j\|_\infty+\|B_j'\|_\infty
\Bigr)
<\infty.    
\end{equation}

Using the definition of $r_n$ and the triangle inequality, we obtain
\begin{align*}
\|r_n\|_\infty+\|r_n'\|_\infty
&\le
C_m\left(
\max_{1\le j\le m}|d_j-q_n(y_j)|
+
\max_{1\le j\le m}|(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)-q_n'(y_j)|
\right)\\
&=
C_m\left(
\max_{1\le j\le m}|s_{\infty m}^*(y_j)-q_n(y_j)|
+
\max_{1\le j\le m}|(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)-q_n'(y_j)|
\right)\to 0.
\end{align*}
Therefore
\[
\|r_n\|_\infty+\|r_n'\|_\infty\to 0.
\]
In particular,
\[
\|\hat s_{nm}-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty
\le
\|q_n-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty+\|r_n\|_\infty\to 0,
\]
and
\[
\|\hat s_{nm}'-(s_{\infty m}^*)'\|_\infty
\le
\|q_n'-(s_{\infty m}^*)'\|_\infty+\|r_n'\|_\infty\to 0.
\]

By Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, $s_{\infty m}^*\in C^1([c,d])$ and is
strictly monotone. Hence $(s_{\infty m}^*)'$ has a fixed sign on
$[c,d]$. Without loss of generality, we treat the increasing case, so
that
\[
(s_{\infty m}^*)'(x)>0,\qquad x\in[c,d].
\]
Since $\hat s_{nm}'\to (s_{\infty m}^*)'$ uniformly on $[c,d]$, it
follows that $\hat s_{nm}'>0$ on $[c,d]$ for all sufficiently large
$n$.

Finally, combining \eqref{eq:hermite-match-poly-proof} with
\eqref{eq:pf-cov-poly-proof}, we obtain
\[
Pf(\hat s_{nm})(d_j)
=
\frac{f(y_j)}{\hat s_{nm}'(y_j)}
=
\frac{f(y_j)}{(s_{\infty m}^*)'(y_j)}
=
g(d_j),
\qquad j=1,\dots,m.
\]
Thus $\hat s_{nm}\in\mathcal F_{nm}$.

By Proposition~\ref{prop: obj-continuity} and the optimality of
$s_{nm}^*$ over $\mathcal F_{nm}$,
\[
J(s_{\infty m}^*)
\le
J(s_{nm}^*)
\le
J(\hat s_{nm})
\le
J(s_{\infty m}^*)+K\|\hat s_{nm}-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty.
\]
Since $\|\hat s_{nm}-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty\to 0$, we conclude that
\[
J(s_{nm}^*)\to J(s_{\infty m}^*)
\qquad\text{for each fixed }m.
\]

To upgrade from pointwise convergence to uniform convergence in $m$,
define the auxiliary continuous functions $l_n,l:[0,1]\to\mathbb R$
exactly as in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc}. Since
$\mathcal S_n$ is nested, the sequence $n\mapsto J(s_{nm}^*)$ is
non-increasing for each fixed $m$, so $l_n\downarrow l$ pointwise on
$[0,1]$. By construction, $l_n$ and $l$ are continuous on $(0,1]$,
and right-continuous at $0$ by
Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}%
\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp1} and
Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}%
\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp2}, respectively.
Therefore, by Dini's theorem on the compact interval $[0,1]$,
$l_n\to l$ uniformly. Evaluating at $x=1/m$ yields
\[
J(s_{nm}^*)\xrightarrow[n\to\infty]{}J(s_{\infty m}^*)
\qquad\text{uniformly in }m.
\]
\end{proof}


\subsection{Rate of Convergence}\label{subsec:rate}

In this section, we establish the rate of convergence of
$J(s_{nm}^*)$ to $J(s^*)$.  All results in this section require
Assumptions~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, and the conditions for general
cost functions in Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or conditions for
strictly convex translation cost in
Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}.  The rate decomposes along the
triangle inequality
\[
\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|
\leq
\underbrace{\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\bigr|}_{\text{approximation error in }n}
+
\underbrace{\bigl|J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|}_{\text{discretization error in }m}.
\]
The second term is bounded by $O(1/m)$ in
Proposition~\ref{prop:rate2} and is independent of the choice of
approximation space $\mathcal{S}_n$.  The first term depends on the choice of $\mathcal{S}_n$.  For
continuous piecewise linear functions, we establish an $O(1/n)$ bound
with a constant that is uniform in $m$ in Proposition~\ref{prop:rate1}, yielding the combined rate
$O(1/n+1/m)$ in Theorem~\ref{thm:rate-main}.  For polynomial
subspaces, the analogous bound carries an $m$-dependent constant
(see Remark~\ref{rem:poly-rate}), so the combined rate result is
stated only for piecewise linear approximation spaces.

\begin{proposition}\label{prop:rate1}
 Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support},~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, and assuming $\mathcal{S}_n$ is the space of continuous piecewise linear functions on $\mathrm{supp}(f)$, there exists a constant $W_1>0$ independent of $m$, such that for every fixed $m\ge 1$ and all $n\ge 1$,
    \[
    \bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\bigr|\le \frac{W_1}{n}.
    \]
    Equivalently, for each fixed $m$, $\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\bigr|=O(1/n)$ with constants that are independent of $m$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
First, let us note that for a fixed $m>1$, $\mathcal{F}_{nm}\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$. Therefore, we have
\begin{align*}
    J(s_{\infty m}^*)\leq J(s_{nm}^*)\leq J(s_{nm}),
\end{align*}
for any $s_{nm}\in\mathcal{F}_{n m}$. Hence, the proof strategy is to construct a sequence $\{\hat{s}_{nm}\}_{n\geq 1}\in\mathcal{F}_{nm}$, such that $J(\hat{s}_{nm})\rightarrow J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ with linear rate, which ensures that $J(s_{nm}^*)\rightarrow J(s_{\infty m}^*)$ with at least linear rate. Consider the even partition $\{x_1, x_2\cdots, x_n\}$ of $\mathrm{supp}(f)=[c,d]$ where $t_n:=\frac{d-c}{n}$ is the length between two consecutive points in the partition. Here we fix by convention that $x_1 = c$, $x_n = d$. For any $x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)$, we know $x_i\leq x\leq x_{i+1}$ for some $1\leq i\leq m-1$. Consider $\hat{s}_{nm}$ that:
\begin{align*}
    \hat{s}_{nm}(x) = s_{\infty m}^*(x_{i+1})\frac{x-x_i}{t_n} + s_{\infty m}^*(x_i)\frac{x_{i+1}-x}{t_n}.
\end{align*}

This choice of $\hat{s}_{nm}$ is essentially a $n$-pieces piecewise linear approximation of $s_{\infty m}^*$ such that it is in space $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$. Note that both $\hat{s}_{nm}$ and $s_{\infty m}^*$ are Lipschitz, denote the larger Lipschitz constant of the two as $L$.
Now, we have 
\begin{align*}
    &J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\\
    \leq&|J(\hat{s}_{nm})-J(s_{\infty m}^*)|\\
    \leq& K\|\hat{s}_{nm}-s_{\infty m}^*\|_{\infty}\\
    \leq& K\sup_{x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)}\left(\frac{L(x-x_i)}{t_n}\left(x-x_i\right) + \frac{L(x_{i+1}-x)}{t_n}\left(x_{i+1}-x\right)\right)\\
    =& K\sup_{x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)}\left(\frac{L}{t_n}((x-x_i)^2+(x_{i+1}-x)^2)\right)\\
    \leq& K\sup_{x\in \mathrm{supp}(f)}\left(\frac{L}{t_n}2t_n^2\right)\\
    \leq& 2KLt_n\\
    \leq& \frac{2KL(c-d)}{n},
\end{align*}
for all $m>0$, where the numerator is a constant.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}\label{rem:poly-rate}
In the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:cp-conv-to-cpc-poly}, the bound
$\|\hat{s}_{nm}-s_{\infty m}^*\|_\infty\to 0$ depends on the
constant $C_m$ in~\eqref{eq:C_m}, which depends on $m$.  This
prevents a convergence rate in $n$ that is uniform in $m$, in
contrast with the piecewise linear case
(Proposition~\ref{prop:rate1}), where the interpolation error is
intrinsically local and independent of~$m$.  For this reason, the
combined rate $O(1/n+1/m)$ in Theorem~\ref{thm:rate-main} is stated
only for piecewise linear approximation spaces.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}\label{prop:rate2}
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support},~\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz} and the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, there exists a constant $W_2>0$ such that for all $m\ge 1$,
\[
\bigl|J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|\le \frac{W_2}{m}.
\]
Equivalently, $\bigl|J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|=O(1/m)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
First, note that we have $\mathcal{F}\subseteq\cdots\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{\infty m+1}\subseteq\mathcal{F}_{\infty m}$. Therefore, for any $\hat{s}\in\mathcal{F}$ we have 
\begin{align*}
    J(s_{\infty m}^*)\leq J(s_{\infty m+1}^*)\leq\cdots\leq J(\hat{s}).
\end{align*}  
We know that $J(s_{\infty m}^*)\xrightarrow{m\rightarrow\infty}J(s^*)$ monotonically from below, therefore $\{J(s_{\infty m}^*)\}_{m\geq 1}$ is a Cauchy sequence. First, observe that we have
\begin{align*}
    0\leq J(s_{\infty m+1}^*) - J(s_{\infty m}^*)\leq J(s_{\infty m+1}) - J(s_{\infty m}^*),
\end{align*}
for any $s_{\infty m+1}\in\mathcal{F}_{\infty m+1}$. Moreover,
\begin{align*}
    &|J(s^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)|\\
    =&J(s^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\\
    =&\lim_{i\rightarrow\infty}J(s_{\infty i}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\\
    =&\lim_{i\rightarrow\infty}(-J(s_{\infty m}^*)+J(s_{\infty m+1}^*)-J(s_{\infty m+1}^*)+\\
    &\cdots+J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)+J(s_{\infty i}^*))\\
    =&\lim_{j\rightarrow\infty}\sum_{i=m+1}^{j} J(s_{\infty i}^*)-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*).
\end{align*}
The last limit exists because we proved the second equality in Theorem~\ref{thm: cp-conv-to-cpt-cpc-conv-to-nlp}(ii). Therefore, we can write
\begin{align*}
    &|J(s^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)|\\
    =&\lim_{j\rightarrow\infty}\sum_{i=m+1}^{j} J(s_{\infty i}^*)-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)\\
    =&\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty} J(s_{\infty i}^*)-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)\\
    \leq&\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty} J(\hat{s}_{\infty i})-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)
\end{align*}
for any sequence $\{\hat{s}_{\infty i}\}_{i\geq 1}\subseteq \cup_{i\geq 1}\mathcal{F}_{\infty i}$.

Our proof strategy, therefore, is to
construct a sequence $\{\hat{s}_{\infty i}\}_{i\geq 1}\subseteq \cup_{i\geq 1}\mathcal{F}_{\infty i}$ such that 
\[
\left\{\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty}J(\hat{s}_{\infty i})-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)\right\}_{m\geq 1}
\]
goes to $0$ as $m\rightarrow\infty$ at a certain rate.
Now, let us construct such a sequence. Recall the sequence of partitions in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: feasible-set-conv} where $\{\mathcal{P}_i:=\{d_1, d_2, \cdots,d_i\}\}$ is a sequence of partitions of $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ such that $\mathcal{P}_1\subseteq\mathcal{P}_2\subseteq\cdots$, and $\lim_{i\rightarrow\infty}\mathcal{P}_i=\mathcal{D}$ where $\mathcal{D}\subset \mathrm{supp}(g)$ is dense. We assume without loss of generality that each $\mathcal{P}_i$ is a uniform partition such that $d_{n+1}-d_n=h_i,\quad\forall 1\leq n\leq i-1$ with $d_1=a$, $d_2=b$ given that $\mathrm{supp}(g) = [a,b]$.


Consider two consecutive terms $\mathcal{P}_i$ and $\mathcal{P}_{i+1}$ in the sequence of partitions on which the density constraint must be satisfied, let $D:= \mathcal{P}_{i+1}\setminus\mathcal{P}_i$. First, note that for $s_{\infty i}^*$, we have 
\begin{align*}
Pf(s_{\infty i}^*)(d_1)&=g(d_1),\\
&\vdots\\
Pf(s_{\infty i}^*)(d_i)&=g(d_i).
\end{align*}
Now let $\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}=s_{\infty i}^*$ so we have
\begin{align*}
Pf(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})(d_1)&=g(d_1),\\
&\vdots\\
Pf(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})(d_i)&=g(d_i).
\end{align*}
Now we want to perturb $\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}$ such that the density constraint is satisfied at all points in $\mathcal{D}$ as well as the other grid points where the constraint is already satisfied. Recall that by Leibniz rule, we have $Pf(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})(d)=f((\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})^{-1}(d))(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}^{-1})'(d)$. Note that for any $d\in D$ it must be true that $d\in(d_n, d_{n+1})$ for some $1\leq n\leq i-1$. Now, we redefine $\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}$ in a small neighborhood around $d$ as a piecewise linear function with 3 pieces. Since $\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}$ is a bijection, we redefine $\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}$ by redefining $\hat{s}^{-1}_{\infty i+1}$ for simplicity. 
\begin{figure}[hbt!]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[scale=.5]{graphs/construction.png}
    \caption{}
    \label{fig:2d}
\end{figure}
Consider
\begin{align*}
    \hat{s}^{-1}_{\infty i+1}(y)= 
\begin{cases}
    s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(y),& \text{if } y\in\{d-\frac{1}{i}(d-d_n),d,d+\frac{1}{i}(d_{n+1}-d)\}\\
    s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(y)-\frac{1}{2i}(d-d_{n})\frac{g(d)}{f(s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d))},& \text{if } y=d-\frac{1}{2i}(d-d_n)\\
    s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(y)+\frac{1}{2i}(d_{n+1}-d)\frac{g(d)}{f(s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d))},& \text{if } y=d+\frac{1}{2i}(d_{n+1}-d)\\
    \text{linear interpolated value}, & \text{else if } y\in(d-\frac{1}{i}(d-d_n),d+\frac{1}{i}(d_{n+1}-d)).
\end{cases}
\end{align*}
$\frac{g(d)}{f(s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d))}$ is the slope we impose on the modified $\hat{s}^{-1}_{\infty i+1}$ around $d$ while maintaining $\hat{s}^{-1}_{\infty i+1}(d) = s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d)$. Therefore, we have
\begin{align*}
    Pf(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})(d)&=f((\hat{s}_{\infty i+1})^{-1}(d))(\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}^{-1})'(d)\\
    &=f(s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d))\frac{g(d)}{f(s_{\infty i}^{*-1}(d))}\\
    &=g(d),
\end{align*}
and we can confirm that the constraint is satisfied on $d$ while the constraint at other locations is not disrupted. By construction, $\|s_{\infty i}^{*}-\hat{s}_{\infty i+1}\|_{\infty}\leq \frac{1}{i}(h_i)=\frac{b-a}{i^2}$ and 
\begin{align*}
    &|J(s^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)|\\
    \leq&\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty} J(\hat{s}_{\infty i})-J(s_{\infty i-1}^*)\\
    \leq&\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty}K \|\hat{s}_{\infty i}-s_{\infty i-1}^{*}\|_{\infty}\\
    \leq&K(b-a)\sum_{i=m+1}^{\infty}\frac{1}{i^2}\\
    \sim&\frac{K(b-a)}{m}\quad\text{for large } m.
\end{align*}
Where the numerator is a constant.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:rate-main}
Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}--\ref{ass:cost-lipschitz}, \ref{ass:mono}, the conditions of either Theorem~\ref{thm:philippis} or Theorem~\ref{thm:gangbo-mccann}, and assuming $\mathcal{S}_n$ is the space of continuous piecewise linear functions on $\mathrm{supp}(f)$, there exist constants $W_1,W_2>0$, independent of $n$ and $m$, such that for all $n,m\ge 1$,
\[
\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|
\;\le\;
\frac{W_1}{n}+\frac{W_2}{m}
\;=\;
O\!\left(\frac1n+\frac1m\right).
\]
In particular, $J(s_{nm}^*)\to J(s^*)$ as $n,m\to\infty$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By the triangle inequality,
\[
\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|
\le
\bigl|J(s_{nm}^*)-J(s_{\infty m}^*)\bigr|
+
\bigl|J(s_{\infty m}^*)-J(s^*)\bigr|.
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:rate1} and Proposition~\ref{prop:rate2}, the two terms on the right admit upper bounds of order $O(1/n)$ and $O(1/m)$ respectively, with constants $W_1$, $W_2$. Combining them yields the result.
\end{proof}


For the computational problem (CP), where the constraint is discretized to $m$ grid points, the local geometry of feasibility depends on the relationship between $n$ and $m$. If $n = m$ and the constraint Jacobian is invertible, feasible points remain isolated. However, in the standard approximation regime where $n > m$, Proposition~\ref{prop:discrete-LICQ} guarantees LICQ for appropriate grid placement, making the feasible set $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ locally an $(n - m)$-dimensional manifold. In this case, feasible points are not isolated, and first-order feasible directions exist. The difficulty is that the manifold $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ is defined by a complex nonlinear system. Moving along it while strictly maintaining feasibility requires solving a nonlinear root-finding problem at every continuous step. Sequential quadratic programming avoids this computational bottleneck by linearizing the pushforward constraint at each iterate, approximating the manifold locally by its tangent space and reducing each step to a structured quadratic program with linear constraints. This linearization is what makes SQP computationally viable and well-suited to the geometry of (CP).

To summarize, Section~\ref{sec:cp} has established three things. First, the optimal objective value of the computational problem (CP) converges to the optimal value of the infinite-dimensional problem (P) at rate $O(1/n + 1/m)$ as the approximation dimension $n$ and constraint grid size $m$ are refined (Theorem~\ref{thm:rate-main}). Second, under LICQ, the feasible set $\mathcal{F}_{nm}$ of (CP) is locally an $(n-m)$-dimensional manifold, providing a well-defined geometric setting for constrained optimization. Third, the nonlinear geometry of the feasible sets motivates the use of a method that linearizes the pushforward constraint at each iterate rather than attempting to enforce it exactly. Motivated by these geometric insights, Section~\ref{sec:SQP} formally develops the Sequential Quadratic Programming framework, assembling our variational derivatives into a tractable algorithm with convergence guarantees.

\section{Sequential Quadratic Programming}\label{sec:SQP}
\subsection{The Functional SQP}\label{subsec:SQP}
As discussed, the Monge formulation yields a constrained nonconvex problem, while local optima are still characterized by the standard KKT conditions. From a computational standpoint, any implementable method must restrict the search to a finite-dimensional subspace of $\mathcal{S}$. In this setting, feasibility can be poorly conditioned, so it is natural to use a method that allows infeasible iterates while controlling the constraint residual. Sequential quadratic programming (SQP) is well suited for this purpose. Moreover, the Fr\'echet derivatives derived in Section~\ref{sec:deriv} provide the required local structures: the first and second variations of the objective to form the quadratic approximation, and the first variation of the pushforward constraint to build its linear approximation.
In this section we present the SQP method by defining the local quadratic subproblem (QP\textsuperscript{k}) for (P) and its discrete analogue (CPQP\textsuperscript{k}) for (CP), and we summarize the corresponding convergence properties in both the infinite-dimensional and computational settings.

We begin with the infinite-dimensional setting. Given the first- and second-order information, we now consider the SQP procedure for (P). Let $s_k$ represent the transport map computed at the $k$th step of the procedure and let $h\in\mathcal{S}$ be a perturbation direction. We approximate $J(s_k+h)$ up to second order by $\Tilde{J}(s_k+h)$, where for any $h\in\mathcal{S}$,
\begin{align*}
    \Tilde{J}(s_k+ h) := J(s_k) + J_{s_k}(h)+ \frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h)(h),
\end{align*}
and $J_{s_k}(h)$ and $H_{s_k}(h)(h)$ are the first and second Fr\'echet derivatives defined in Theorems~\ref{thm: J1deriv} and~\ref{thm: J2deriv}.

Next, we approximate the constraint up to first order. Recall the original constraint $Pf(s_k)-g = 0$, and define
\begin{align*}
    \Tilde{P}f(s_k+h)(x) := Pf(s_k)(x) + Pf_{s_k}(h)(x),
\end{align*}
which is a first-order linearization of the Perron--Frobenius operator $Pf(s_k)$. Here, $Pf_{s_k}(h)(x)$ is the Fr\'echet derivative of the F--P operator as derived in Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}. We now propose to solve the following quadratic problem:
\begin{align*}
      &\min_{h\in\Omega}\Tilde{J}(s_k + h)\tag{QP\textsuperscript{k}}\\
    \text{s.t.}\quad &\Tilde{P}f(s_k+h)-g = 0.  
\end{align*}

Consider the Lagrangian of (QP\textsuperscript{k}):
\begin{align*}
   \mathcal{L}(h, z_k^*)
   &= \Tilde{J}(s_k+ h) + \big\langle z_k^*,\,\Tilde{P}f(s_k+h)-g\big\rangle_{\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{Z}}\\
   &=  J(s_k) + J_{s_k}(h)+ \frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h)(h)
    + \big\langle z_k^*,\,Pf(s_k) + Pf_{s_k}(h)-g\big\rangle_{\mathcal{Z}^*,\mathcal{Z}},
\end{align*}
where $\mathcal{Z}:=L^1(\mathcal{X})$ and $\mathcal{Z}^*\cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$. In particular, when $z_k^*$ is represented by a function $\lambda_k\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ under $\mathcal{Z}^*\cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$, the Lagrangian admits the integral form
\begin{align*}
   \mathcal{L}(h, \lambda_k)
   &= \Tilde{J}(s_k+ h) + \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda_k(x)\big(\Tilde{P}f(s_k+h)-g\big)(x)dx\\
   &=  J(s_k) + J_{s_k}(h)+ \frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h)(h)
    + \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda_k(x)\big(Pf(s_k)(x) + Pf_{s_k}(h)(x)-g(x)\big)dx,
\end{align*}
where $\lambda_k$ is the Lagrange multiplier at the current step.

To derive the first-order optimality conditions for the SQP subproblem, we compute the directional derivative of each term in $\Tilde{\mathcal{L}}$ with respect to $h$ along a direction $u\in\mathcal{S}$. The first term $J(s_k)$ is independent of $h$, so its directional derivative is zero. Since $J_{s_k}(\cdot)$ is linear, we have $J_{s_k,h}(h)(u)=J_{s_k}(u)$. For the quadratic term, using the bilinear form representation
\[
H_{s_k}(a)(b)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} a(x)b(x)\,D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s_k(x)\big)\,f(x)dx,
\]
we obtain
\begin{align*}
    &\left(\frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h)(h)\right)_h(u)
    =\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}\frac{\frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h+\epsilon u)(h+\epsilon u)-\frac{1}{2}H_{s_k}(h)(h)}{\epsilon}\\
    &=\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}\frac{1}{\epsilon}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\Big(h(x)\epsilon u(x)+\tfrac{1}{2}\epsilon^2u(x)^2\Big)\,D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s_k(x)\big)\,f(x)dx\\
    &=\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)u(x)\,D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s_k(x)\big)\,f(x)dx\\
    &=H_{s_k}(h)(u).
\end{align*}

Finally, for the constraint term, since $Pf_{s_k}(\cdot)$ is linear, we have $Pf_{s_k,h}(h)(u)=Pf_{s_k}(u)$ and hence
\[
\left(\int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda_k(x)\big(Pf(s_k)(x)+Pf_{s_k}(h)(x)-g(x)\big)dx\right)_h(u)
=\int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda_k(x)\,Pf_{s_k}(u)(x)dx.
\]

Putting the pieces together, the first-order optimality conditions for (QP\textsuperscript{k}) are:
\begin{align*}
     J_{s_k}(\cdot) + H_{s_k}(h)(\cdot) + \int_{\mathcal{X}} \lambda_k(x)\, Pf_{s_k}(\cdot)(x)dx &= 0,\\
     \Tilde{P}f(s_k+h)(x)-g(x) &= 0,\qquad \forall x\in\mathcal{X}.
\end{align*}
Let $(h_k, z_k^*)$ be the solution of the first-order optimality conditions above, where $z_k^*$ is represented by $\lambda_k\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ under $\mathcal{Z}^*\cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$. We then update
\begin{align*}
    s_{k+1} = s_k + \alpha_k h_k,
\end{align*}
where $\alpha_k$ is a suitable step size.



\subsection{Local Convergence}\label{subsec:loc_conv}

In this section, we establish the local convergence of the SQP procedure by verifying the assumptions of Alt~\cite{alt1990lagrange} at a local solution $s_0$ of (P).Recall that our constraint space is
\[\mathcal{Z}_0=\Big\{ \eta \in L^1(\mathcal{X}) : \int_{\mathcal{X}}\eta(x)\,dx = 0 \Big\},
\]
whose dual is $\mathcal{Z}_0^* \cong L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb R$. By identifying any abstract multiplier $z^*\in \mathcal{Z}_0^*$ with a functional representative $\lambda\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ (determined up to an additive constant), we can express the Lagrangian $\mathcal{L}(s,\lambda)$ directly in integral form:
\[
\mathcal{L}(s,\lambda) := J(s)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda(x)\mathcal{H}(s)(x)\,dx,
\]
where $\mathcal{H}(s):=Pf(s)-g\in \mathcal{Z}_0$.Accordingly, we will henceforth refer to a local KKT pair by its representative $(s_0,\lambda_0)$, which satisfies primal feasibility, $\mathcal{H}(s_0)=0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$, and the stationarity condition:
\[
J_{s_0}(h)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_0(x)\,\mathcal{H}_{s_0}(h)(x)\,dx=0, \qquad \forall h\in\mathcal{S}.
\]

\begin{proposition}\label{prop:verify-alt-condition}Fix a local KKT pair $(s_0,\lambda_0)$ of (P).Under Assumptions~\ref{ass:domain-support}, \ref{ass:density-cont}, \ref{ass:cost-second}, \ref{ass:mono}, \ref{ass:hardy}, assuming $\lambda_0 \in W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X})$, and the conditions of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} and Theorem~\ref{thm: PF2deriv}, the following conditions hold at $s_0$.
\begin{itemize}
\item[(B1)] The objective functional $J:\mathcal{S}\to\mathbb R$ and the Perron--Frobenius operator $Pf:\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{Z}$ are twice continuously Fr\'echet differentiable on a neighborhood $N_1(s_0)\subset \mathcal{S}$.
\item[(B2)] The second-variation operators $J'':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{S}^{**}$ and $Pf'':\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{B}\big(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)\big)$ are Lipschitz continuous on $N_1(s_0)\subset \mathcal{S}$.
\item [(B3)] $0_{\mathcal{Z}_0} \in \mathrm{int}\Big\{Pf_{s_0}(h)\mid h\in\mathcal{S}\Big\}$ in $\mathcal{Z}_0$, where $0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$ is the zero \item [(B4)] There exists an $\alpha>0$ such that $\mathcal{L}_{s_0 s_0}(h)(h)\geq\alpha\|h\|^2$
for all $h\in\mathcal{S}$ with $Pf_{s_0}(h)=0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$.
\end{itemize}
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\noindent Proof of (B1) and (B2):
Since \(s_0\in\Omega\) and \(\Omega\) is open in \(W^{1,\infty}(\mathcal{X})\) (see Section~\ref{subsec:notation}), there exists \(\epsilon>0\) such that
\[
B^{W^{1,\infty}}_\epsilon(s_0)\subset \Omega.
\]
We therefore work on the neighborhood
\[
N_1(s_0):=\mathcal{S}\cap B^{W^{1,\infty}}_\epsilon(s_0).
\]
By construction, every \(s\in N_1(s_0)\) remains in the admissible class, hence remains monotone and nondegenerate. In particular, \(Pf(s)\) is well defined throughout \(N_1(s_0)\) (see Lemma~\ref{lem:pf-continuous-version}), and Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, and Theorem~\ref{thm: PF2deriv} apply on this neighborhood.

We first verify the required properties for \(J\). For any \(s_1,s_2\in N_1(s_0)\),
\begin{align*}
&\|J'(s_1)-J'(s_2)\|_{\mathcal{S}^*}\\
=& \sup_{\substack{h\in\mathcal{S}\\ \|h\|=1}}
\left|
\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\big(D_yc(x,s_2(x))-D_yc(x,s_1(x))\big)f(x)\,dx
\right|\\
\leq&
\sup_{\substack{h\in\mathcal{S}\\ \|h\|=1}}
\int_{\mathcal{X}} |h(x)|\,L\,\|s_1-s_2\|_\infty f(x)\,dx\\
\leq& L\|s_1-s_2\|_\infty,
\end{align*}
where \(L\) is the Lipschitz constant of \(D_yc(x,\cdot)\) from Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-second}. Likewise,
\begin{align*}
&\|J''(s_1)-J''(s_2)\|_{\mathcal{S}^{**}}\\
=& \sup_{\substack{h,\tilde h\in\mathcal{S}\\ \|h\|=\|\tilde h\|=1}}
\left|
\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\tilde h(x)\big(D_{yy}c(x,s_1(x))-D_{yy}c(x,s_2(x))\big)f(x)\,dx
\right|\\
\leq&
\sup_{\substack{h,\tilde h\in\mathcal{S}\\ \|h\|=\|\tilde h\|=1}}
\int_{\mathcal{X}} |h(x)|\,|\tilde h(x)|\,M\,\|s_1-s_2\|_\infty f(x)\,dx\\
\leq& M\|s_1-s_2\|_\infty,
\end{align*}
where \(M\) is the Lipschitz constant of \(D_{yy}c(x,\cdot)\). Hence \(J\) is twice continuously Fr\'echet differentiable on \(N_1(s_0)\), and both \(J'\) and \(J''\) are Lipschitz continuous there.

For the Perron--Frobenius operator, the corresponding properties on \(N_1(s_0)\) follow from Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} and Theorem~\ref{thm: PF2deriv}. More precisely, \(Pf:\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{Z}\) is twice continuously Fr\'echet differentiable on \(N_1(s_0)\), while
\[
Pf':\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
Pf'':\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{B}\bigl(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)\bigr)
\]
are Lipschitz continuous on \(N_1(s_0)\).

Therefore, conditions (B1) and (B2) hold at \(s_0\).
\end{proof}

\noindent Proof of (B3):
By Theorem~\ref{thm:pf-surjectivity}, under the stated assumptions, the operator $Pf_{s_0}:\mathcal{S}\to\mathcal{Z}_0$ is surjective. By the Open Mapping Theorem \citep[Theorem~2.11]{Rudin1974FunctionalAnalysis}, $Pf_{s_0}$ is an open map, so $0_{\mathcal{Z}_0} = Pf_{s_0}(0)$ is an interior point of $\{Pf_{s_0}(h) \mid h \in \mathcal{S}\}$ in $\mathcal{Z}_0$.

\noindent Proof of (B4):
Recall that the Lagrangian of problem (P) at a local minimizer $(s_0,\lambda_0)$ is
\[
\mathcal{L}(s,\lambda_0)
=
J(s)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_0(x)\big(Pf(s)(x)-g(x)\big)\,dx,
\]
where $\lambda_0\in L^\infty(\mathcal{X})$ represents the multiplier.

Its second Fr\'echet derivative with respect to $s$ at $s_0$ is
\[
\mathcal{L}_{s_0s_0}(h)(h)
=
H_{s_0}(h)(h)
+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_0(x)\,Qf_{s_0}(h)(h)(x)\,dx.
\]

To verify (B4), we only need to consider directions $h\in\mathcal{S}$ satisfying the linearized constraint
\[
Pf_{s_0}(h)=0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}.
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:injective}, the operator $Pf_{s_0}$ is injective. Hence $Pf_{s_0}(h)=0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$ implies $h=0$.
Therefore, the set of feasible critical directions in (B4) is the singleton $\{0\}$.
Evaluating the second derivative in the only such direction gives
\[
\mathcal{L}_{s_0s_0}(0)(0)=0.
\]
Thus, for any $\alpha>0$,
\[
\mathcal{L}_{s_0s_0}(h)(h)
=
0
\ge \alpha \|h\|^2
\qquad
\text{whenever } Pf_{s_0}(h)=0_{\mathcal{Z}_0},
\]
since necessarily $h=0$. Hence condition (B4) holds.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Local Quadratic Convergence]
\label{thm: local-conv}
Let $s_0$ be a local solution of (P), and let $\lambda_0$ denote the Lagrange multiplier function associated with $s_0$.
Assume that the regularity and stability conditions \emph{(B1)--(B4)} in Proposition~\ref{prop:verify-alt-condition} hold at $s_0$.

Then there exist constants $0<\delta<1$, and $\rho>0$, and a ball
$B_{\rho\delta}\big((s_0,\lambda_0)\big)$ such that $s\in N_1(s_0)$ for every
$(s,\lambda)\in B_{\rho\delta}\big((s_0,\lambda_0)\big)$.
Moreover, for any starting point
$(s_1,\lambda_1) \in B_{\rho\delta}\big((s_0,\lambda_0)\big)$, the SQP method generates a
unique sequence $\{(s_k,\lambda_k)\}_{k\ge 1}$ satisfying, for all $k\ge 2$,
\[
\big\|(s_k,\lambda_k)-(s_0,\lambda_0)\big\|_{\mathcal{S}\times (L^\infty/\mathbb{R})}
:=
\left( \|s_k-s_0\|^2 + \|\lambda_k-\lambda_0\|_{L^\infty(\mathcal{X})/\mathbb{R}}^2 \right)^{1/2}
\;\le\;
\rho\,\delta^{2^{k-1}}.
\]
In particular, the iterates converge \emph{quadratically} to $(s_0,\lambda_0)$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Proposition~\ref{prop:verify-alt-condition} verifies (B1)--(B4) for our problem.
Therefore the conclusion follows by applying Alt~(1990, Theorem~3.3) to the SQP mapping written in the $(s,\lambda)$ variables.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Global Convergence}\label{subsec:glo_conv}
Consider the merit functional:
\[
M(s,\eta)=J(s)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda(x)(Pf(s)(x)-g(x))dx+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(s)(x)-g(x)|dx
\]
where $\eta$ is the penalty parameter of the merit functional.
\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: Merit} 
    For the above-defined merit functional $M(s,\eta)$, under conditions for Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, we have the following,
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $s^*$ is a local min of (P) if and only if it is a local min of the merit functional with sufficiently large $\eta$.
        \item If $s_k$ is not a local min of (P), then $h_k$, the descent direction computed in the $(QP\textsuperscript{k})$ iterations at $s_k$ must also be a descent direction for the merit functional with sufficiently large $\eta$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
    Proof of $(1)$: Denote the feasible set of (P) as $\mathcal{F}$. For the forward direction, if $s^*$ is a local min of (P), i.e., $\exists \epsilon$ such that for a neighbourhood $\mathcal{N}_{\epsilon}(s^*)$ of $s^*$, we have 
    \[
    J(s^*)\leq J(s)\quad\forall s\in \mathcal{F}\cap\mathcal{N}_{\epsilon}(s^*).
    \]
    Now suppose that $\exists\Tilde{s}\in \mathcal{N}_{\epsilon}(s^*)$ and $\Tilde{\eta}>0$ such that 
    \[
    M(\Tilde{s}, \eta)<M(s^*, \eta),\quad\forall\eta>\Tilde{\eta}.
    \]
    Since we know that $s^*$ is feasible, we could simplify the former inequality to:
    \[
    J(\Tilde{s})+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(\Tilde{s})(x)-g(x)|dx<J(s^*),\quad\forall\eta>\Tilde{\eta}.
    \]
    Here and throughout the proof of $(1)$, we choose to omit the second term in the merit functional because it will be dominated by the third term for large enough $\eta$. Consider two cases. In the first case, $\Tilde{s}\in\mathcal{F}$. Note that the former inequality implies
    \[
    J(\Tilde{s})<J(s^*),\quad\forall\eta>\Tilde{\eta}
    \]
    which leads to a contradiction. In the second case, $\Tilde{s}\notin\mathcal{F}$, then $\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(\Tilde{s})(x)-g(x)|dx>0$. We could increase $\eta$ until $J(\Tilde{s})+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(\Tilde{s})(x)-g(x)|dx<J(s^*)$ is violated. The second case also leads to a contradiction. Hence we have shown the forward direction of $(1)$. For the backward direction, let's first observe that, if $s^*$ is a local min of the merit functional, then it must be feasible for (P). To see this, first note that the first order necessary condition for the merit functional must be satisfied at $s^*$. Recall the merit functional after omitting the second term:
    \begin{align*}
        M(s,\eta)=J(s)+G(s,\eta),
    \end{align*}
    where $G(s,\eta)=\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(s)(x)-g(x)|dx$. The absolute value function $|x|$ is not differentiable at 0, therefore, we use the function $2\epsilon log(1+e^{x/\epsilon})-x-2\epsilon log(2)$ to approximate $|x|$. The derivative of $2\epsilon log(1+e^{x/\epsilon})-x-2\epsilon log(2)$ is $2\frac{e^{x/\epsilon}}{1+e^{x/\epsilon}}-1$. Note that both the approximating function and its derivative converge to $|x|$ and its derivative pointwise as $\epsilon\to 0$ except when $x=0$ since $|x|$ is not differentiable at 0. The approximating function, on the other hand, is differentiable at 0, and its derivative at 0 is 0. Now, applying the approximation of $|x|$ to the merit functional, we obtain the approximating merit functional:
    \begin{align*}
        M^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(\cdot)&=J(s^*)+G^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)\\
        &= J(s^*)+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}2\epsilon log(1+e^{Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x)/\epsilon})-(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))-2\epsilon log(2)dx.
    \end{align*}
The first variation with respect to $s$ of the approximating merit functional is then
 \begin{align*}
        M_{s^*}^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(\cdot)&=J_{s^*}(\cdot)+G_{s^*}^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(\cdot)\\
        &= J_{s^*}(\cdot)+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\left(2\frac{e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}{1+e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}-1\right)Pf_{s^*}(\cdot)(x)dx.
    \end{align*}
    Note that $G_{s^*}^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(h)\to G_{s^*}(s^*,\eta)(h)$ for any $h\in\mathcal{S}$ by bounded convergence theorem. 
    Setting the first variation to 0, we get the first necessary condition of the merit functional:
    \begin{align*}
        M_{s^*}^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(\cdot)=J_{s^*}(\cdot)+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\left(2\frac{e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}{1+e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}-1\right)Pf_{s^*}(\cdot)(x)dx = 0
    \end{align*}
    where $=0$ means that the first variation is a zero-operator, i.e., $M_{s^*}^\epsilon(s^*,\eta)(h)=0$ for any $h\in\mathcal{S}$.
    In the proof of (B3) in Proposition~\ref{prop:verify-alt-condition}, we showed that $Pf_{s^*}(h)(x)=0$ if and only if $h=0$, that $Pf_{s^*}(\cdot)(x)=0$ is not a zero operator. In order for $\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\left(2\frac{e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}{1+e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}-1\right)Pf_{s^*}(\cdot)(x)dx=0$ for all $\eta$. We need to set $2\frac{e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}{1+e^{(Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x))/\epsilon}}-1=0$, and this only happens when $Pf(s^*)(x)-g(x)=0$, namely, when $s^*$ is feasible. So far, we have shown that $s^*$ is necessarily a feasible point of (P) if it is a local min of the merit functional. Now suppose that $\exists \Tilde{s}\in\mathcal{F}\cap\mathcal{N}_{\epsilon}(s^*)$ such that $J(\Tilde{s})<J(s^*)$. Equivalently, we have $M(\Tilde{s},\eta)<M(s^*,\eta)$ which contradicts the fact that $s^*$ is a local min of the merit functional. Now we have completed the proof of (1).\\
    Proof of $(2)$: To show that $h_k$ is a descent direction for the merit functional, note that $h_k$ is the solution of the $k-th$ step of (QP\textsuperscript{k}), hence it must satisfy the first-order necessary condition of (QP\textsuperscript{k}). Namely, we have
    \begin{align*}
        J_{s_k}(\cdot)+H_{s_k}(h_k)(\cdot)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_k(x)Pf_{s_k}(\cdot)(x)dx&=0\\
        Pf(s_k)(x)+Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)-g(x)&=0.
    \end{align*}
    Recall the merit functional:
    \[
    M(s_k,\eta)=J(s_k)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_k(x)(Pf(s_k)(x)-g(x))dx+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}|Pf(s_k)(x)-g(x)|dx.
    \]
    $h_k$ is a descent direction if the first variation of the merit functional along $h_k$ is negative. The first variation of the merit functional along $h_k$ is
    \begin{align*}
        M_{s_k}(h_k)&=J_{s_k}(h_k)+\int_{\mathcal{X}}\lambda_k(x)Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)dx+ G_{s_k}(h_k)\\
        &=-H_{s_k}(h_k)(h_k)+G_{s_k}(h_k)\\
        &=-H_{s_k}(h_k)(h_k)+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}} \frac{Pf(s_k)(x)-g(x)}{|Pf(s_k)(x)-g(x)|}Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x) dx\\
        &=-H_{s_k}(h_k)(h_k)+\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}} \frac{-Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)}{|Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)|}Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x) dx\\
        &=-H_{s_k}(h_k)(h_k)-\frac{\eta}{2}\int_{\mathcal{X}}\left|Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)\right|dx,
    \end{align*}
    where the second equality is due to the first equation in the first-order condition of $(QP\textsuperscript{k})$ and the penultimate equality is due to the second equation in the first-order condition of $(QP\textsuperscript{k})$. Observe the final expression to conclude that, $ M_{s_k}(h_k)<0$ for large enough $\eta$ since $Pf_{s_k}(h_k)(x)\neq 0$ for $h_k\neq 0$. 
\end{proof}    

For each iteration of (QP\textsuperscript{k}), we compute a descent direction $h_k$ for the merit functional. We could choose the step size of $\alpha_k$ according to suitable line search criteria, for example, the Armijo rule:
\begin{align*}
    M(s_k+\alpha_k h_k,\eta)\leq M(s_k,\eta)+c\alpha_k M_{s_k}(h_k),
\end{align*}
for some $0<c<1$.
\begin{theorem}~\label{thm: global-convergence} 
 For large enough $\eta$, the SQP iterations with step size chosen according to the Armijo rule are guaranteed to converge to a critical point of the merit functional.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
    As shown in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: Merit}, $M_{s_k}(h_k)$ is negative for any non-negative direction $h_k$. By imposing the Armijo rule, we have
    \begin{align*}
         M(s_k+\alpha_k h_k,\eta)\leq M(s_k,\eta)+c\alpha_k M_{s_k}(h_k).
    \end{align*}  
    By definition of the first variation of the merit functional, we have: 
    \begin{align*}
         M(s_k+\alpha_k h_k,\eta)-M(s_k,\eta) = M_{s_k}(\alpha_k h_k)+o(\|\alpha_k h_k\|).
    \end{align*}
    Recall that the first variation $M_{s_k}(\alpha_k h_k)$ is linear in $\alpha_k$ for a fixed $h_k$, we obtain:
    \begin{align*}
        M(s_k+\alpha_k h_k,\eta)
        &= M(s_k,\eta) + M_{s_k}(\alpha_k h_k)+o(\|\alpha_k h_k\|)\\
        &= M(s_k,\eta) + \alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k)+o(\|\alpha_k h_k\|)\\
        &= M(s_k,\eta) + c \alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k)+ (1-c) \alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k)+o(\|\alpha_k h_k\|).
    \end{align*}
    Observe the last two terms, $(1-c) \alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k)<0$ and $\epsilon \alpha_k \|h_k\|>0$. Since $\epsilon\rightarrow 0$ as $\|\alpha_k h_k\|\rightarrow 0$, we know that for any descent direction $h_k$, there exists a $\alpha_k$ small enough such that $(1-c) \alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k)+o(\|\alpha_k h_k\|)<0$. Therefore, an $\alpha_k$ that satisfies the Armijo rule can always be found.
    Now, we have
    \begin{align*}
        \sum_{k=0}^{K} M(s_k+\alpha_k h_k,\eta)- M(s_k,\eta) \leq c \sum_{k=0}^{K}\alpha_kM_{s_k}(h_k).
    \end{align*}
    This implies that:
    \begin{align*}
        M(s_K+\alpha_K h_K,\eta)-M(s_0,\eta)\leq c \sum_{k=0}^{K}\alpha_k M_{s_k}(h_k).
    \end{align*}
    Note that $\alpha_k M_{s_k}(h_k)< 0$ for all $k$. Since the left-hand side is negative and bounded from below, the right-hand side summation must be convergent, therefore, we necessarily have either $\alpha_k \rightarrow 0$ or $M_{s_k}(h_k) \rightarrow 0$ and either implies that the merit functional converges to a critical point.
\end{proof}
The convergence results we have discussed so far are at the level of the original problem (P) and its SQP iterates. In practice, however, the infinite-dimensional formulation cannot be solved directly. Any implementable algorithm must replace the transport map with a finite-dimensional parameterization and approximate the pushforward constraint accordingly. The next section makes this transition explicit by introducing the computational problem and discussing the consistency of these approximations.

\subsection{The Discrete SQP}\label{subsec:discr-SQP}

At the $k$th iteration of SQP, and at the current iterate $s_{n,k} \in \mathcal{S}_n$, we construct the $k$th quadratic subproblem of (CP):
\begin{align*}
    &\min_{\delta_{s_n,k}\in\Omega_n} \nabla J(s_{n,k})^T\delta_{s_n,k} + \frac{1}{2}\delta_{s_n,k}^T H_k \delta_{s_n,k} \tag{CPQP\textsuperscript{k}}\\
    &\text{s.t.} \\
    & \nabla \tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k})^T \delta_{s_n,k} + \tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k}) = 0,
\end{align*}
where $s_{n,k+1} = s_{n,k} + \delta_{s_n,k}$. The KKT condition of (CPQP\textsuperscript{k}) is given by the linear system:
\begin{align*}
    G(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k})\delta^k = -\Psi(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}),
\end{align*}
where
\begin{align*}
\Psi(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}) = 
\begin{bmatrix}
\nabla \mathcal{L}(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}) \\
\tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k})
\end{bmatrix}, \quad
G(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}) = 
\begin{bmatrix}
H\mathcal{L}(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}) & \nabla \tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k}) \\
\nabla \tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k})^T & 0
\end{bmatrix}.
\end{align*}
The solution vector $\delta^k = [\delta_{s_{n,k}}^T, \delta_{\lambda_{m,k}}^T]^T$ contains the descent directions for the optimization variables and the updates for the Lagrange multipliers.

To control the step size, consider the merit function with penalty parameter $\eta > 0$:
\begin{align*}
    M(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}, \eta) = \mathcal{L}(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}) + \frac{1}{2}\eta\|\tau^{(m)}(s_{n,k})\|^2_2.
\end{align*}
Starting from $\alpha_k = 1$, we decrease the step size until it satisfies the Armijo line search criterion:
\begin{align*}
    M(s_{n,k+1}, \lambda_{m,k+1}, \eta) \leq M(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}, \eta) + c\alpha_k \nabla M(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k}, \eta)^T \delta_{s_{n,k}}.
\end{align*}

\begin{definition}[Second-Order Sufficient Condition]
\label{def:SOSC}
Let $(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})$ satisfy the first-order KKT system:
\begin{align*}
\nabla \mathcal{L}(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0}) = 0,
\qquad
\tau^{(m)}(s_{n,0}) = 0,
\end{align*}
and assume the Jacobian $\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_{n,0})$ has full row rank (LICQ). Denote by
\begin{align*}
\mathcal{C}(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})
\;=\;
\Bigl\{\,w\in \mathcal{S}_{n}
       :\;
       \nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_{n,0})^{\top}w = 0
\Bigr\}
\end{align*}
the critical cone (null-space of the Jacobian at the solution). We say that \textnormal{(CP)} satisfies the second-order sufficient condition at $(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})$ if there exists a constant $\alpha > 0$ such that:
\begin{align*}
w^{\top} H\mathcal{L}(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})\,w
\;\;\ge\;\;
\alpha\,\|w\|^{2},
\qquad
\forall\,w\in\mathcal{C}(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})\setminus\{0\}.
\end{align*}
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}\label{prop:discrete-LICQ}
For any $m$ and any $n > m$, and any feasible transport map $s_n \in \mathcal{S}_n$ for \textnormal{(CP)}, there exists a partition $\mathcal{P}_m$ for the discretized pushforward constraints such that the computational objective \textnormal{(CP)} satisfies the linearly independent constraint qualification (LICQ) at $s_n$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Recall that the first variation of the PF operator at a feasible $s_n$ along $h$ is:
\begin{align*}
Pf_{s_n}(h)(x) =& \frac{1}{2\pi} \int_{\mathbb{R}} -e^{itx}\,e^{-it\,\mathbb E[s_n(Y)]}\mathbb E[ite^{it(s_n(Y)-\mathbb E[s_n(Y)]}(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)])] dt\\
    -&g_{s_n}'(x)\mathbb E [h(Y)],    
\end{align*}
and $Pf_{s_n}(h)$ is Fr\'echet, meaning that it is linear in $h$. For the computational objective \textnormal{(CP)} with an $n$-dimensional approximation to the solution space and $m$-dimensional discretization of the functional constraint, the direction $h \in \mathcal{S}_n$ is parameterized by $b:=(b_0,\cdots,b_{n-1})\in\mathbb{R}^n$ such that $h = \sum_{j=0}^{n-1} b_j \chi_j$, where $\{\chi_0, \dots, \chi_{n-1}\}$ form an orthonormal function basis. Combining these facts, we have $Pf_{s_n}(h) = \sum_{j=0}^{n-1} b_j Pf_{s_n}(\chi_j)$. Therefore, the Jacobian matrix of the discretized constraints $\tau^{(m)}$ is:
\begin{align*}
\nabla\tau^{(m)}(s_n) =
\begin{bmatrix}
Pf_{s_n}(\chi_0)(d_1) & \cdots & Pf_{s_n}(\chi_{n-1})(d_1) \\
\vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
Pf_{s_n}(\chi_0)(d_m) & \cdots & Pf_{s_n}(\chi_{n-1})(d_m)
\end{bmatrix}.
\end{align*}


Since $\{\chi_0, \dots, \chi_{n-1}\}$ are orthonormal, they are linearly independent. We can always find a partition $\mathcal{P}_m$ such that 
\begin{align*}
\begin{bmatrix}
\chi_0(d_1) & \cdots & \chi_{n-1}(d_1) \\
\vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
\chi_0(d_m) & \cdots & \chi_{n-1}(d_m)
\end{bmatrix}
\end{align*}
is row independent—observe that if $\sum_{j}\alpha_j \chi_j \equiv 0$, then for all $i\in\{0,\cdots,n-1\}$, we have:
\begin{align*}
    &\langle \chi_i, \sum_{j}\alpha_j \chi_j \rangle = \alpha_i \|\chi_i\|= 0,
\end{align*}
