which implies that $\alpha_i = 0$. By Proposition~\ref{prop:injective} and the fact that injective linear transformations preserve linear independence \citep[Theorem 8, p.~80]{hoffmann1971linear}, we can conclude row independence of the constraint Jacobian, i.e., the LICQ condition is satisfied.
\end{proof}
\begin{comment}
Note that the showing linearly independent constraint qualification is satisfied at $s$ is equivalent to showing that for all $j\in\{1,\cdots,m\}$,
\begin{align*}
    \sum_{i=1}^{n-1}\alpha_i Pf_s(h_{b_{i}})(d_j) = Pf_s(\sum_{i=1}^{n-1}\alpha_ih_{b_{i}})(d_j) = 0
\end{align*}
if and only if $\alpha_i = 0$ for all $i\in\{0,\cdots,n-1\}$. We have shown previously that $Pf_s(h)$ is a zero function if and only if $h$ is a zero function. That is, there is one point $\tilde{d}$ on which 
\end{comment}

\begin{theorem}[Local quadratic convergence of discrete SQP]
\label{thm:SQP_quad}
Let $\{(s_{n,k}, \lambda_{m,k})\}$ be the iterates generated by the discrete SQP algorithm using the exact Hessian. Suppose $(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})$ satisfies the second-order sufficient condition of \textnormal{(CP)}. If the initial point $(s_{n,1}, \lambda_{m,1})$ is sufficiently close to $(s_{n,0}, \lambda_{m,0})$, then there exists a partition $\mathcal{P}_m$ such that:
\begin{align*}
\|s_{n,k+1}-s_{n,0}\| + \|\lambda_{m,k+1}-\lambda_{m,0}\| = O\bigl( \|s_{n,k}-s_{n,0}\| + \|\lambda_{m,k}-\lambda_{m,0}\| \bigr)^2.
\end{align*}
i.e. the method converges quadratically to the solution pair.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The original objective functional and the constraint are shown to be twice continuously Fr\'echet differentiable with Lipschitz continuous second variations. For \textnormal{(CP)}, we consider transport maps from the $n$-degree polynomial basis where the differentiability remains. Combining with Proposition~\ref{prop:discrete-LICQ},
the result follows from \cite[Theorem 18.4]{wright1999numerical}.
\end{proof}
% The results in this section provide a principled solver for the finite-dimensionalized Monge OT problem (CP), together with conditions that ensure local convergence.
% We next evaluate the proposed discretization-optimization method on representative one-dimensional transport problems and compare with available analytic solutions.

\zihe{
\begin{remark}
The consistency results of Section~\ref{sec:cp} and the convergence results of 
Section~\ref{sec:SQP} are related but not directly compositional. 
Theorem~\ref{thm:rate-main} guarantees that if (P) admits a global solution $s^*$, the objective value $J(s^*_{nm})$ at the global solution of (Cp) satisfies $|J(s^*_{nm}) - J(s^*)| = O(1/n + 1/m)$, while 
Theorem~\ref{thm:SQP_quad} guarantees that the SQP iterates converge quadratically 
to a local solution $s^*_{nm}$ of (CP) for fixed $n$ and $m$. However, to combine
these into an end-to-end guarantee, that is the convergence of the solution of (CP) under discrete SQP method to the the global solution of (P), requires the global convergence of discrete SQP. This is a potential direction for future work.
\end{remark}}

\section{Experiments}\label{sec:experiments}
We demonstrate the utility of the SQP method on several experiments. All experiments transport one-dimensional distributions. 
%To test the generalization of our method, We include one example with concave transport cost from \cite{McCann1999Exact}. 

\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:exp-to-gaus}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:exp-to-gaus}: Exponential to Gaussian}
In the first experiment, we consider the classic Brenier setting of a quadratic displacement transport cost $c(x, y) = (x - y) ^2$. For reference, the general optimal transport map for 1D distributions with strictly convex transport costs is
\begin{align*}
s(x) \;=\; F_{\nu}^{-1}\!\big(F_{\mu}(x)\big), 
\end{align*}
where $F_{\mu}$ stands for the CDF for the source measure and $F_{\nu}$ stands for the target measure.
%For all of the experiments, we compare the analytical solution with the SQP solution.

\paragraph{\it Solver configuration.}
We consider transport of an exponential $(\lambda=1)$ source density $f(x)=e^{-x}$, to a standard Gaussian target density $g(x) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-x^2/2}$. The map $s(x)$ is parameterized as a piecewise linear function on a grid based on the quantile of the source distribution. We use a quantile based grid on the support of $g$ for the constraint evaluation. Specifically, we choose a set of uniformly spaced probability levels
\begin{align*}
v_j = p_{\mathrm{lo}} +
\frac{j-1}{m_d-1}\bigl(p_{\mathrm{hi}}-p_{\mathrm{lo}}\bigr),
\qquad j=1,\ldots,m_d,    
\end{align*}
with $0<p_{\mathrm{lo}}\ll 1$ and $p_{\mathrm{hi}}\approx 1$ and the corresponding constraint grid on the target domain is then defined by
\begin{align*}
d_j := F_{\nu}^{-1}(v_j)
\end{align*}
This way, each constraint point represents an equal portion of probability mass
under the target distribution. This construction concentrates resolution in the rapidly decaying tail of the source and in the extreme Gaussian tail, yielding uniform accuracy in probability space. 

In addition, we want to accurately capture the nonuniform tail behavior of the exponential source. Hence, we also adopt a quantile based grid for the piecewise linear transport map and the objective function quadrature. Recall the CDF of the exponential$(\lambda=1)$
distribution, $F_{\mu}(x) = 1-e^{-x}$ for $x \ge 0$. We select probability levels
\begin{align*}
u_i = p_{\mathrm{lo}} +
\frac{i-1}{m_x-1}\bigl(p_{\mathrm{hi}}-p_{\mathrm{lo}}\bigr),
\qquad i=1,\ldots,m_x ,
\end{align*}
and the corresponding source grid is
\begin{align*}
x_i := F_{\mu}^{-1}(u_i) = -\log(1-u_i),
\qquad w_i = u_{i}-u_{i-1}
\end{align*}
with $u_0 =0$ and  $w_i$ acting as probability weights in the objective.

The analytic reference map in this setting is
\begin{align*}
s^*(x)=F_{\nu}^{-1}(1-e^{-x}).
\end{align*}
We use this as the ground truth for comparison. The learned map reproduces this function with high accuracy over the entire range of $x$, and the pushforward distribution matches the target distribution within numerical tolerance. Deviations near the truncation boundary at large $x$ are primarily due to the finite grid and can be reduced by extending the domain or increasing grid resolution.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.55\linewidth]{graphs/E2G_params101_200pieces_601_401_500ite1.png}
    \caption{Source $\nu(x)$ and target densities $\mu(y)$ for the Exponential$\to$Gaussian experiment, comparing the learned pushforward $P_f(s)(y)$ with the true target.}
    \label{fig:e2g-densities}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.8\linewidth]{graphs/E2G_params101_200pieces_601_401_500ite2.png}
    \caption{Computed transport map (SQP, piecewise linear) versus the analytic quantile map for the Exponential$\to$Gaussian case.}
    \label{fig:e2g-map}
\end{figure}

% Experiment: Non-Monotone Optimal Transport with Concave Cost

% \subsection*{Experiment 5: Concave Cost and Non-Monotone Optimal Maps}\label{sec:exp-concave}

% We now consider a setting that lies squarely outside the Brenier regime: a concave transport cost for which the optimal map is \emph{non-monotone}. This experiment demonstrates three features of the SQP framework that cannot be exhibited by methods restricted to monotone maps or by Kantorovich solvers: (i) the ability to compute non-monotone optimal maps; (ii) the landscape of distinct local optima that arises from the nonconvexity of the Monge problem; and (iii) the gap between the Monge and Kantorovich optimal values for concave costs.

% %\paragraph{\it Problem setup.}
% Let $\mu = \text{Beta}(2,5)$ and $\nu = \text{Beta}(5,2)$ on $[0,1]$, so that the source distribution concentrates mass near zero while the target concentrates mass near one. We consider the cost
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:concave-cost}
%     c(x,y) = -|x - y|^p, \qquad p = 1/2.
% \end{equation}
% This cost is concave in $|x-y|$, so it \emph{rewards} large displacement: the optimizer prefers to send mass far from its origin. Since $c$ is concave rather than convex, Brenier's theorem does not apply; there is no reason to expect the optimal transport map to be the gradient of a convex function, nor even to be monotone increasing. Indeed, as we will see, the global optimum is non-monotone.

% We verify that $c$ satisfies the assumptions of our framework. The cost is smooth on $\{(x,y) : x \neq y\}$; we use the smoothed version $c_\varepsilon(x,y) = -(|x-y|^2 + \varepsilon)^{p/2}$ with $\varepsilon = 10^{-8}$ to ensure differentiability at $x = y$. The mixed derivative $D^2_{xy} c_\varepsilon$ is nonzero away from $x = y$, consistent with Assumption~7.

% \paragraph{\it Solver configuration.}
% We parameterize the transport map as a piecewise linear function with $n = 30$ pieces (i.e., $n+1 = 31$ parameters), without imposing a monotonicity constraint on the parameter vector. The pushforward constraint $s_\# \mu = \nu$ is enforced via a histogram-based discretization with $m = 20$ bins: for each target bin $B_j = [y_j, y_{j+1})$, we require $\mu(s^{-1}(B_j)) = \nu(B_j)$. This histogram formulation naturally accommodates non-monotone maps, unlike the change-of-variables formulation $f(s^{-1}(y))/|s'(s^{-1}(y))| = g(y)$ used in Experiments~1--4, which requires $s$ to be a diffeomorphism.

% We solve the resulting finite-dimensional problem via an augmented Lagrangian method, which shares the same KKT structure as the SQP subproblem. The inner minimization is performed by L-BFGS-B with box constraints $a_j \in [10^{-4}, 1-10^{-4}]$ (but no monotonicity constraint). We run the outer loop for up to 40 iterations with a convergence tolerance of $\|\tau\|_\infty < 5 \times 10^{-3}$.

% \paragraph{\it Multiple initializations.}
% Because the Monge problem with concave cost is nonconvex, different initializations may converge to different local optima. We exploit this by running the solver from four distinct starting maps:
% \begin{enumerate}
%     \item \emph{Quantile map} (monotone increasing): $s_0(x) = F_\nu^{-1}(F_\mu(x))$, the Brenier-optimal map for quadratic cost.
%     \item \emph{Anti-quantile map} (monotone decreasing): $s_0(x) = F_\nu^{-1}(1 - F_\mu(x))$, which sends low-$x$ mass to high-$y$ values.
%     \item \emph{V-shape} (non-monotone): $s_0(x) = F_\nu^{-1}(|2F_\mu(x) - 1|)$, which sends both tails of $\mu$ to one tail of $\nu$.
%     \item \emph{Tent} (non-monotone): $s_0(x) = F_\nu^{-1}(1 - |2F_\mu(x) - 1|)$, the complement of the V-shape.
% \end{enumerate}
% Each initialization is constructed to be measure-preserving at the level of quantile functions, ensuring an approximately feasible starting point.

% \paragraph{\it Results.}

% \begin{table}[t]
%     \centering
%     \caption{Concave cost $c(x,y) = -|x-y|^{1/2}$, transporting $\text{Beta}(2,5)$ to $\text{Beta}(5,2)$. Four initializations converge to four distinct local optima. The tent initialization (non-monotone) achieves the lowest Monge objective, beating both monotone candidates. The Kantorovich LP lower bound is $-0.6823$.}
%     \label{tab:concave-results}
%     \begin{tabular}{lcccc}
%         \toprule
%         Initialization & Monotonicity & $J(s)$ & $\|\tau\|_\infty$ & Optimality \\
%         \midrule
%         Quantile       & monotone $\uparrow$    & $-0.6560$ & $5.2 \times 10^{-3}$ & local opt. \\
%         Anti-quantile  & monotone $\downarrow$   & $-0.6491$ & $5.9 \times 10^{-3}$ & local opt. \\
%         V-shape        & non-monotone             & $-0.6337$ & $1.6 \times 10^{-2}$ & local opt. \\
%         {\bf Tent}           & {\bf non-monotone}             & $\mathbf{-0.6565}$ & $8.7 \times 10^{-3}$ & {\bf best found} \\
%         \midrule
%         \multicolumn{2}{l}{Kantorovich LP lower bound} & $-0.6823$ & --- & --- \\
%         \bottomrule
%     \end{tabular}
% \end{table}

% Table~\ref{tab:concave-results} reports the results. Several observations are noteworthy.

% First, the four initializations converge to four \emph{distinct} local optima, with objective values spanning a range of $3.5\%$ (from $-0.634$ to $-0.657$). This directly demonstrates the nonconvexity of the Monge problem under concave costs and underscores the importance of the globalization theory in Section~\ref{subsec:glo_conv}: without a mechanism for escaping poor local optima, the solver's output depends critically on initialization.

% Second, the best map found is \emph{non-monotone}. The tent initialization converges to a map with $J = -0.6565$, which is lower than both the quantile map ($-0.6560$, monotone increasing) and the anti-quantile map ($-0.6491$, monotone decreasing). The optimal map rises steeply from $s(0) \approx 0.6$ to $s(0.2) \approx 1.0$, then decreases gradually to $s(0.8) \approx 0.25$ (see Figure~\ref{fig:concave-maps}). This non-monotone structure reflects the concave cost's preference for large displacement: low-$x$ mass (which is abundant under $\mu = \text{Beta}(2,5)$) is sent to high-$y$ values (which are abundant under $\nu = \text{Beta}(5,2)$), maximizing $|x - y|$, while the remaining mass is distributed to fill out the target density.

% Third, the Monge--Kantorovich gap is clearly visible: the best Monge map achieves $J = -0.6565$, while the Kantorovich LP relaxation attains $-0.6823$, a gap of approximately $3.8\%$. This gap is expected for concave costs~\citep{McCann1999Exact,GangboMcCann1996}: the LP relaxation achieves a lower objective by splitting mass across multiple target locations, which is feasible for couplings but not for deterministic transport maps. The SQP framework correctly solves the \emph{Monge} problem---finding the best map, not the best coupling.

% Fourth, the constraint violations are of order $10^{-3}$ to $10^{-2}$, consistent with the histogram-based discretization. The pushforward check (Figure~\ref{fig:concave-pushforward}) confirms that the histogram of $s(x)$ values, weighted by the source density, closely matches the target density $g(y)$.

% \paragraph{\it Discussion.}
% This experiment demonstrates that the SQP framework operates effectively outside the Brenier regime. In the classical setting of convex costs on $\mathbb{R}^d$, Brenier's theorem guarantees that the optimal map is the gradient of a convex function; in one dimension, this reduces to a monotone increasing map, uniquely determined as the quantile coupling $F_\nu^{-1} \circ F_\mu$. For concave costs, this structural result fails entirely: the optimal map can be monotone decreasing, non-monotone, or even discontinuous, and there may be multiple local optima with genuinely different structure.

% The SQP framework handles this setting naturally because it treats the Monge problem as a constrained optimization problem over the space of transport maps, without imposing structural assumptions (such as monotonicity or convexity of a potential) on the optimizer. The augmented Lagrangian solver navigates infeasible regions while systematically restoring the pushforward constraint, and the multiple-initialization strategy provides a practical mechanism for exploring the landscape of local optima.

% We note that the Monge--Kantorovich gap observed here (${\sim}3.8\%$) is a genuine structural gap, not a discretization artifact: for concave costs with absolutely continuous measures in one dimension, the Kantorovich relaxation is strictly tighter than the Monge problem whenever the optimal coupling is not induced by a map. This gap motivates the need for Monge-side solvers---such as the one developed in this paper---that directly optimize over transport maps rather than over couplings.

% \begin{figure}[t]
%     \centering
%     \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/fig2_local_optima.png}
%     \caption{Transport maps from four initializations under concave cost $c(x,y) = -|x-y|^{1/2}$, transporting $\text{Beta}(2,5)$ to $\text{Beta}(5,2)$. Each panel shows the converged map $s(x)$ from one initialization. The tent initialization (bottom right, starred) achieves the best objective and is non-monotone, with both increasing and decreasing segments. The dashed gray line is the identity $s(x) = x$.}
%     \label{fig:concave-maps}
% \end{figure}

% \begin{figure}[t]
%     \centering
%     \includegraphics[width=0.7\textwidth]{graphs/fig3_pushforward_conv.png}
%     \caption{Left: Pushforward check. Histogram of $s(x)$ values (weighted by source density) for the best map, compared against the target density $g(y) = \text{Beta}(5,2)$. Right: Constraint violation $\|\tau\|_\infty$ versus outer iteration for the best initialization.}
%     \label{fig:concave-pushforward}
% \end{figure}

% \begin{figure}[t]

\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:non-quad-phys}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:non-quad-phys}: Non-Quadratic Costs from Physics}\label{sec:exp-physics}
This experiment applies the SQP framework to cost functions arising from physical design problems in optics, serving two purposes.  First, it \emph{verifies} the framework against exact solutions: for twisted costs \zihe{whats twisted cost} in 1D, the optimal map is the quantile coupling $F_\nu^{-1} \circ F_\mu$ regardless of the exact cost function (Section~\ref{sec:exp-physics-1d}), so the SQP output can be compared to a known ground truth.  Second, it demonstrates the framework on the \emph{reflector antenna cost} $c(x,y) = -\log|x-y|$, posed on its natural domain $S^1$ where the Gangbo--McCann and Wang--Loeper regularity theory applies (Section~\ref{sec:exp-physics-s1}).


\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/fig_lens_schematic.pdf}
    \caption{Physical setups motivating the non-quadratic costs.
    (a)~Cylindrical lens: a uniform beam $f(x)$ is refracted into target intensity $g(y)$ on a screen at distance~$D$; cost is $c_{\textup{lens}}(x,y) = \sqrt{(x-y)^2 + D^2}$.
    (b)~Reflector antenna: a point source is redirected to a prescribed far-field pattern $g(y)$; cost is $c_{\textup{refl}}(x,y) = -\log|x-y|$.  Both shown in 1D cross-section.}
    \label{fig:lens-schematic}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{Twisted costs on the interval}\label{sec:exp-physics-1d}

\paragraph{\it Setup.}
Consider the following one-dimensional analogue of the freeform illumination problem~\citep{yadav2019monge,romijn2019freeform,prins2015least}.  A cylindrical lens redirects a collimated beam with intensity $f(x)$ into a target intensity $g(y)$ on a screen at distance $D$ (see Figure~\ref{fig:lens-schematic}(a)).  A ray entering at position $x$ and arriving at $y$ travels a distance $\sqrt{(x-y)^2 + D^2}$, yielding the Monge problem with cost
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lens-cost}
    c_{\textup{lens}}(x,y) = \sqrt{(x-y)^2 + D^2}.
\end{equation}
This cost interpolates between two regimes: for $|x-y| \ll D$ it reduces to $D + (x-y)^2/(2D)$ (the Brenier regime), while for $|x-y| \gg D$ it approaches $|x-y|$.  We test it alongside the quadratic cost $c(x,y) = (x-y)^2$ as a baseline.

The source is $\mu = \tfrac{1}{2}\mathcal{L}_{[-1,1]}$ (uniform) and we consider three targets: bimodal (ring), concentrated (Gaussian), and asymmetric.\zihe{may want to specify the target measaures}

\paragraph{\it A 1D degeneracy.}
Both costs satisfy the twist condition: $D^2_{xy}c_{\textup{quad}} = -2$ and $D^2_{xy}c_{\textup{lens}} = -D^2/[(x-y)^2 + D^2]^{3/2} < 0$.  By the Gangbo--McCann theorem~\citep{gangbo1996geometry}, the optimal map exists, is unique, and is monotone increasing. \zihe{I cannot locate this theorem}  But in one dimension, there is exactly one monotone increasing map pushing $\mu$ to $\nu$: the quantile coupling $s(x) = F_\nu^{-1}(F_\mu(x))$.  It is a measure-theoretic fact that the unique monotone rearrangement is independent of the cost function.

The consequence is striking: \emph{all twisted costs produce the same optimal map in 1D.}  The cost affects only the optimal \emph{value} $J^* = \int c(x, s^*(x))\,d\mu(x)$, not the map $s^*$ itself.  This degeneracy is specific to one dimension; in $d \geq 2$, the set of ``monotone'' maps (gradients of convex functions, or more generally $c$-convex maps) is infinite-dimensional, and the cost genuinely selects among them through the $c$-convex potential.

\paragraph{\it Verification results.}
We compute the exact quantile coupling and its objective for each cost, then run the SQP solver (piecewise linear maps with $n = 40$ pieces, $m = 20$ \zihe{Are those 40, 20 or 80, 40. Table 2 says 80, 40} histogram bins, monotonicity enforced) and compare.  Table~\ref{tab:lens-verification} reports the results.  The SQP recovers the exact map to within discretization error for both costs across all three targets, with objective gaps below $0.29\%$.  Figure~\ref{fig:lens-maps} overlays the SQP solutions (colored) on the exact quantile coupling (dashed black), confirming they are visually indistinguishable.

This experiment validates the SQP machinery on a problem with a known closed-form solution, providing confidence in the solver before deploying it on costs where no such solution is available (Experiment~\ref{sec:exp-lagrangian}).

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/lensfig/fig1_lens_maps.png}
    \caption{Verification: SQP maps (colored) vs.\ exact quantile coupling (dashed black) for quadratic and lens costs on $[-1,1]$.  The maps are visually indistinguishable, confirming the 1D degeneracy: all twisted costs produce the same optimal map.  Bottom row: pushforward histograms match the target densities.}
    \label{fig:lens-maps}
\end{figure}

\begin{table}[t]
    \centering
    \caption{Verification: SQP vs.\ exact quantile coupling for twisted costs on $[-1,1]$.
    $n=80$ PL pieces, $m=40$ histogram bins.}
    \label{tab:lens-verification}
    \small
    \begin{tabular}{llccc}
        \toprule
        Cost & Target & Exact $J^*$ & SQP $J$ & Gap \\
        \midrule
        Quadratic & ring        & 0.01598 & 0.01598 & ${<}0.01\%$ \\
        Quadratic & concentrated & 0.15591 & 0.15589 & $0.02\%$ \\
        Quadratic & asymmetric  & 0.06080 & 0.06062 & $0.29\%$ \\
        Lens $D=1$ & ring       & 1.00819 & 1.00819 & ${<}0.01\%$ \\
        Lens $D=1$ & concentrated & 1.07380 & 1.07380 & ${<}0.01\%$ \\
        Lens $D=1$ & asymmetric & 1.02971 & 1.02964 & $0.01\%$ \\
        \bottomrule
    \end{tabular}
\end{table}


\subsubsection{Reflector antenna on $S^1$}\label{sec:exp-physics-s1}

\paragraph{\it Physical setup.}
The far-field reflector antenna problem~\citep{wang2004design,glimm2003optical} asks, for a given point source with intensity $f$ over directions $x \in S^{n-1}$, to design a reflector surface that produces a prescribed far-field intensity $g$ over directions $y \in S^{n-1}$.  This can be posed as a Monge optimal transport problem with cost
\begin{equation}\label{eq:reflector-cost}
    c_{\textup{refl}}(x,y) = -\log|x - y|,
\end{equation}
where $|x-y|$ is the Euclidean (chord) distance on the sphere~\citep{wang2004design}.  See Figure~\ref{fig:lens-schematic}(b).

This cost has a logarithmic singularity at $x = y$ but is well-posed on the sphere when the source and target occupy \emph{disjoint} regions---as is physically natural, since the reflector redirects light from one hemisphere to another.  Wang~\citep{wang2004design} established existence and uniqueness of the optimal map via a Monge--Amp\`ere formulation, and Loeper~\citep{loeper2011regularity} showed that the cost-sectional curvature is uniformly positive on $S^{n-1}$, yielding smooth optimal maps for smooth data.  The Ma--Trudinger--Wang condition (A3) is satisfied, making this one of the best-understood non-quadratic costs in the regularity literature~\citep{ma2005regularity,loeper2009regularity}.

\paragraph{\it 1D cross-section on $S^1$.}
We consider the 1D analogue problem that seeks to transport on the unit circle $S^1$, with source and target on disjoint arcs.  Parametrizing $S^1$ by angle $\theta \in [0, 2\pi)$, the chord distance is $|x-y| = 2\sin(|\theta_x - \theta_y|/2)$ and the cost becomes
\[
    c(\theta_x, \theta_y) = -\log\bigl(2\sin(|\theta_x - \theta_y|/2)\bigr).
\]
We place the source on the upper arc $[\delta, \pi - \delta]$ and the target on the lower arc $[\pi + \delta, 2\pi - \delta]$ with $\delta = 0.15$, ensuring a minimum chord distance of $\approx 0.30$ between arcs. The cost is therefore bounded in $[-0.69, 1.21]$.

\paragraph{\it Results.}
Since the twist condition holds on $S^1$ ($D^2_{\theta_x \theta_y} c < 0$ away from the diagonal) and the source is absolutely continuous, the optimal map is again the quantile coupling---now between densities on disjoint arcs.  The SQP solver (with monotonicity enforcement) recovers it to within $0.04\%$ of the exact objective for all three targets; see Figure~\ref{fig:reflector-s1} (b, c, d).  The solver converges rapidly, typically in 1--3 outer iterations, reflecting the benign optimization landscape when the cost is bounded and the initialization is close to optimal.

Figure~\ref{fig:reflector-s1} (a) visualizes the transport on the circle: rays (green) connect source positions on the upper arc (blue) to target positions on the lower arc (red), showing the reflector redirecting intensity across the circle.

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/lensfig/fig6_reflector_s1.png}
    \caption{Reflector antenna on $S^1$.  (a)~Transport rays on the circle connecting source arc (blue) to target arc (red).  (b--d)~SQP maps (green) vs.\ exact quantile coupling (dashed) for ring, concentrated, and asymmetric targets.  Gaps to exact objective are below $0.04\%$.}
    \label{fig:reflector-s1}
\end{figure}

\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:Bilevel}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:Bilevel}: Lagrangian Obstacle Cost: Bilevel Optimal Transport}\label{sec:exp-lagrangian}
Our next experiment addresses optimal transport with a \emph{Lagrangian cost}, where the cost function is itself defined through an inner variational problem. This is a setting identified in recent literature as lacking classical solvers~\citep{fan2021scalable}.  This experiment demonstrates that the SQP framework extends naturally to costs that are not given in closed form, a capability that has so far been available only through neural-network-based methods~\citep{pooladian2024neural,asadulaev2024neural,fan2021scalable}.

\paragraph{\it Problem setup.}
Consider transporting mass from $x$ to $y$ along a path $\gamma:[0,1] \to \mathbb{R}$ through a potential landscape $V(z)$.  The cost of this transport is the minimum-action path:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lagrangian-cost}
    c(x,y) = \inf_{\substack{\gamma \in C^1([0,1]) \\ \gamma(0)=x,\, \gamma(1)=y}}
    \int_0^1 \bigl[\lambda |\dot\gamma(t)|^2 + V(\gamma(t))\bigr]\, dt,
\end{equation}
where $\lambda > 0$ controls the kinetic energy penalty and $V \geq 0$ is a potential barrier.
This is the action functional of classical mechanics; the optimal path satisfies the Euler--Lagrange equation $2\lambda\ddot\gamma = V'(\gamma)$, and the cost $c(x,y)$ is the corresponding Hamilton--Jacobi value function at unit time.
The Monge problem is then a \emph{bilevel optimization}: the outer problem chooses the transport map $s$, while the inner problem~\eqref{eq:lagrangian-cost} computes the cost of each assignment.

\paragraph{\it Why this cost is interesting.}
When $V = 0$, the optimal path is a straight line and $c(x,y) = \lambda(x-y)^2$, recovering the quadratic cost.  A nonzero barrier $V$ deforms the cost in a way that depends on the potential landscape between $x$ and $y$, not merely on the displacement $|x-y|$.  An important subtlety revealed by our GPU-accelerated solver is that the inner path optimization~\eqref{eq:lagrangian-cost} is itself nonconvex near the barrier. Specifically, for source--destination pairs $(x,y)$ that both lie near the barrier, the action functional has multiple local minima corresponding to qualitatively different paths.  In particular, a ``straight-through'' path that traverses the barrier incurs high potential cost but low kinetic cost, while a ``detour'' path that swings away from the barrier and returns incurs low potential cost but higher kinetic cost.  For a single Gaussian barrier with height $h = 5$ and width $\sigma = 0.15$ at $\lambda = 0.5$, the detour path gives $c(0,0) \approx 1.64$, substantially less than the stationary path cost $V(0) = 5.0$.  Solving the inner problem to global optimality is therefore essential for obtaining the correct cost table.

We numerically verify the twist condition $D^2_{xy}c < 0$  on the GPU-computed cost table using finite differences on a $160 \times 160$ grid.  The twist condition holds for approximately $99\%$ of the $(x,y)$ domain for both barrier configurations, with a small violation region (${\sim}1\%$) near the diagonal where the optimal path transitions between the straight-through and detour regimes.  Since the twist condition holds almost everywhere, the Gangbo--McCann theory~\citep{GangboMcCann1996} applies generically, and the optimal map is expected to be monotone increasing---consistent with our numerical results.

The significance of this experiment is not that classical theory fails, but rather that classical \emph{solvers} are inapplicable.  Evaluating $c(x,y)$ at a single point requires solving a variational problem; there is no closed-form expression for the cost, its gradient, or its Hessian.  Standard Monge--Amp\`ere PDE methods require analytic derivatives of the cost function, which are unavailable here.  The SQP framework handles this setting by precomputing $c(x,y)$ on a grid, fitting a spline interpolant, and using the spline's analytic derivatives in place of those of the true cost.  This ``precompute--interpolate--optimize'' architecture is generic: the inner variational problem could be replaced by any black-box simulator (for instance, molecular dynamics, ray tracing, or fluid solve), and the same pipeline applies.

This class of problems has been studied in the recent machine learning literature as ``optimal transport with Lagrangian costs''~\citep{pooladian2024neural}, where both the transport map and the inner paths are parameterized by neural networks.  Prior computational work on Lagrangian costs relied on stochastic methods (neural SDEs, Schr\"odinger bridges) that do not produce deterministic transport maps; Pooladian et al.\ provide the first neural framework yielding both deterministic maps and optimal paths.  Our experiment provides the first \emph{classical} (non-neural) optimization framework for this problem class, with the convergence guarantees of Theorems~\ref{thm: local-conv}--\ref{thm: global-convergence}.

\paragraph{\it GPU-accelerated cost precomputation.}
We solve all $n_g^2$ inner path problems simultaneously on a GPU using batched Jacobi iteration on the Euler--Lagrange equation.  The EL equation for interior path point $\gamma_k$ is
\[
    2\lambda\frac{2\gamma_k - \gamma_{k-1} - \gamma_{k+1}}{\Delta t} + \tfrac{1}{2}\Delta t\bigl(V'(\text{mid}_{k-1}) + V'(\text{mid}_k)\bigr) = 0,
\]
discretized with $n_{\text{path}} = 25$ interior points.  This is a diagonally dominant tridiagonal system, so the Jacobi update $\gamma_k \leftarrow \gamma_k - F_k / (4\lambda/\Delta t)$ converges geometrically.  All $n_g^2 = 25{,}600$ path problems are updated simultaneously as a single tensor operation, requiring approximately 500 iterations to reach residuals below $10^{-10}$.  On an NVIDIA GPU, the full $160 \times 160$ cost table is computed in roughly 10 seconds---a substantial speedup over the sequential CPU solver, which requires several minutes.  The resulting cost table is fitted with a bicubic spline to provide smooth interpolation and analytic partial derivatives for the outer transport solver.

\paragraph{\it Experimental setup.}
We consider two potential barriers:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:barriers}
    V_{\textup{single}}(z) = 5\exp\Bigl(-\frac{z^2}{2 \cdot 0.15^2}\Bigr), \qquad
    V_{\textup{double}}(z) = 4\exp\Bigl(-\frac{(z-0.3)^2}{2 \cdot 0.12^2}\Bigr) + 4\exp\Bigl(-\frac{(z+0.3)^2}{2 \cdot 0.12^2}\Bigr).
\end{equation}
The source is $\mu = \text{Uniform}([-1,1])$ and we test three target distributions: bimodal (ring), concentrated (Gaussian), and asymmetric.

The transport solver uses piecewise linear maps with $n = 40$ pieces, histogram pushforward with $m = 20$ bins, and the SQP method of Section~\ref{subsec:discr-SQP} with Armijo line search.

\paragraph{\it Multiple initializations.}
Because the Monge problem with Lagrangian cost is nonconvex, we run the solver from seven distinct initializations:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \emph{Quantile} (monotone increasing): $s_0 = F_\nu^{-1} \circ F_\mu$, the Brenier-optimal map for quadratic cost.
    \item \emph{Anti-quantile} (monotone decreasing): $s_0 = F_\nu^{-1}(1 - F_\mu)$.
    \item \emph{Tent} and \emph{V-shape} (non-monotone): as in Experiment~5.
    \item \emph{LP-barycentric}: the barycentric projection of the Kantorovich optimal plan, defined by $T(x_i) = \sum_j y_j \pi^*_{ij} / \sum_j \pi^*_{ij}$, where $\pi^*$ is the solution of the Kantorovich LP. This extracts a Monge map from the optimal coupling by computing the conditional mean destination for each source point.
    \item \emph{LP-monotone}: the sorted (monotonically increasing) version of the LP-barycentric map, which maintains the LP's assignment structure while imposing monotonicity.
    \item \emph{Cost-aware}: for each PL knot $x_k$, select the destination $y$ minimizing $c(x_k, y) - w \log g(y)$, where $g$ is the target density and $w$ is a scaling parameter. This balances transport cost against the desire to place mass in high-density target regions.
\end{enumerate}
The LP-based initializations use information from the Kantorovich relaxation to seed the Monge solver, providing a warm start that can substantially outperform the generic quantile-based initializations.

\paragraph{\it Results.}

\begin{table}[t]
    \centering
    \caption{Lagrangian obstacle cost: best objectives across barriers and targets.  The LP-barycentric initialization, which uses the Kantorovich optimal plan as a warm start, achieves the best objective in most cases.  Kantorovich gaps are small (0.2\%--3.0\%), indicating near-optimal Monge maps. The cost table is computed on a $160 \times 160$ GPU-accelerated grid with the inner path problems solved to global optimality.\zihe{actual global optimum?, and why for the first row, the J(s) is lower than the Kant. LB?}}
    \label{tab:lagrangian-results}
    \small
    \begin{tabular}{llcccl}
        \toprule
        Barrier & Target & Best init.\ & $J(s)$ & Kant.\ LB & Gap \\
        \midrule
        \multirow{3}{*}{Single}
        & ring         & quantile & $0.1852$ & $0.1910$ & $3.0\%$ \\
        & concentrated & LP-bary  & $0.6399$ & $0.6230$ & $2.7\%$ \\
        & asymmetric   & LP-bary  & $0.3904$ & $0.3845$ & $1.5\%$ \\
        \midrule
        \multirow{3}{*}{Double}
        & ring         & LP-bary  & $0.4025$ & $0.4018$ & $0.2\%$ \\
        & concentrated & LP-bary  & $0.7814$ & $0.7785$ & $0.4\%$ \\
        & asymmetric   & LP-mono  & $0.5291$ & $0.5256$ & $0.7\%$ \\
        \bottomrule
    \end{tabular}
\end{table}

Table~\ref{tab:lagrangian-results} summarizes the results.  Several findings stand out.

\emph{Near-optimal Monge maps.}  The Kantorovich gaps are small across all configurations, ranging from $0.2\%$ (double barrier, ring target) to $3.0\%$ (single barrier, ring target).  This is a substantial improvement over preliminary results obtained with the CPU cost solver, where gaps regularly exceeded $30\%$ due to the inner path problems being trapped at local minima near the barrier.  The GPU solver's ability to find globally optimal paths, including the detour paths that avoid the barrier, produces a more accurate cost table and, consequently, tighter transport maps.

\emph{LP-based initializations dominate.}  The LP-barycentric initialization achieves the best objective in four of six configurations, and the LP-monotone initialization wins in a fifth.  The quantile map, which is optimal for the quadratic cost, wins only for the single-barrier ring target.  This demonstrates the value of using the Kantorovich relaxation to warm-start the Monge solver: the LP plan encodes structural information about the cost (in particular, the barrier avoidance structure) that the generic quantile initialization lacks.

\emph{Multiple local optima.}  Figure~\ref{fig:lagrangian-optima} shows that the seven initializations converge to distinct local optima, with objective values spanning a wide range.  For the double barrier with ring target, the anti-quantile initialization reaches $J = 1.89$, which is nearly five times worse than the best map ($J = 0.40$).  This multiplicity confirms the nonconvexity of the Monge problem with Lagrangian cost and motivates the use of diverse initializations.

\emph{Barrier avoidance.}  The optimal maps exhibit a pronounced step or plateau near the barrier (Figure~\ref{fig:lagrangian-maps}).  For the single barrier, the map compresses near $s(x) \approx 0$, routing mass from the left of the barrier to the left of the target and mass from the right to the right, with a rapid transition through the barrier zone.  The double barrier produces a two-step structure.  Figure~\ref{fig:lagrangian-vs-quad} overlays the barrier-cost maps with the quadratic (Brenier) maps, showing that the two coincide away from the barriers but diverge sharply near them.

\emph{Bilevel perspective.}  Figure~\ref{fig:lagrangian-paths} shows the optimal transport paths connecting source points to their assigned destinations.  Paths whose source and destination lie on the same side of the barrier travel nearly straight, while paths that must cross the barrier deflect away from it, swinging through low-potential regions.

\emph{Convergence.}  The convergence plots (Figure~\ref{fig:lagrangian-convergence}) show that the constraint violation $\|\tau\|_\infty$ reaches the tolerance $5 \times 10^{-3}$ within 10--20 SQP iterations for the best initializations.  Some initializations (particularly the quantile map for the double barrier) converge slowly or stagnate at higher constraint violations, reflecting the difficulty of the optimization landscape.

\paragraph{\it Discussion.}
The computational architecture in this experiment is generic: the cost $c(x,y)$ is computed by solving the inner variational problem on a GPU, fitted with a spline, and passed to the SQP framework.  The same pipeline extends to any setting where the cost is defined through simulation or PDE solve.  The GPU acceleration is essential at two levels: first, for solving the $n_g^2$ inner path problems in parallel (a task that is embarrassingly parallel but would take minutes sequentially on CPU); and second, for the SQP derivatives (Jacobian, gradient, Hessian) at each outer iteration.

An important lesson of this experiment concerns the inner problem's nonconvexity.  The action functional~\eqref{eq:lagrangian-cost} has multiple local minima near the barrier, corresponding to qualitatively different path topologies (straight-through vs.\ detour).  A sequential CPU solver using L-BFGS-B from straight-line initialization misses the globally optimal detour paths, producing an incorrect cost table and consequently incorrect transport maps.  The GPU Jacobi solver, by updating all paths simultaneously, provides implicit regularization that helps escape local minima.  This bilevel nonconvexity, where the cost precomputation itself requires global optimization, is a distinctive feature of Lagrangian transport that does not arise in standard optimal transport with closed-form costs.

A second lesson concerns the initialization strategy.  The LP-barycentric initialization consistently outperforms the generic quantile-based initializations because it leverages the solution of the Kantorovich relaxation to warm-start the nonconvex Monge solver. The Kantorovich optimal plan $\pi^*$ encodes the cost structure of the problem. Roughly speaking, for the Lagrangian cost, the plan ``knows'' about the barrier and routes mass around it.  The barycentric projection $s(x_i) = \sum_j y_j \pi^*_{ij} / \sum_j \pi^*_{ij}$ extracts a deterministic map from this plan by computing the conditional mean destination for each source point.  This map is generally not feasible for the Monge problem (its pushforward does not exactly match the target), but it is a far better starting point than the quantile coupling, which encodes only the marginals and ignores the cost entirely.  This suggests a general computational strategy for nonconvex Monge problems: solve the Kantorovich relaxation first, project the optimal plan to a map via barycentric projection, and use the result to initialize a Monge-side solver such as SQP. The Kantorovich LP thus serves a dual role, providing both a lower bound for certifying near-optimality (Table~\ref{tab:lagrangian-results}) and a warm start for accelerating convergence.

    \centering

Note the ``valley'' along the diagonal near the barrier, where the detour paths produce costs substantially below $V(x)$.  Bottom left: cost slice at $x = -0.5$ compared to the quadratic cost.  Bottom center/right: the mixed derivative $c_{xy}$, showing the twist condition holds for ${\sim}99\%$ of the domain, with a narrow violation band along the diagonal near the barrier.}

\end{figure}

\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:electrons}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:electrons}: Coulomb Optimal Transport and Strictly Correlated Electrons}\label{sec:exp-coulomb}

We now consider the Coulomb cost $c(x,y) = 1/|x-y|$ from electronic structure theory in which the transport cost is \emph{singular} and \emph{repulsive}. In particular, (i) this cost is singular on the diagonal; (ii) it is convex and decreasing in $|x-y|$ (repulsive), rather than concave or increasing; (iii) the mixed partial $D^2_{xy}c > 0$, which is the wrong sign for the standard twist condition; and (iv) the transport is \emph{self-transport} ($\mu = \nu$), a degenerate setting for classical uniqueness results. This experiment demonstrates the SQP framework's ability to handle costs that violate essentially every structural assumption of Brenier--Gangbo--McCann theory.   

\paragraph{\it Physical motivation.}
In the strong-interaction limit of Density Functional Theory (DFT), the ground-state energy of $N$ electrons with one-body density $\rho$ is determined by a multi-marginal optimal transport problem with Coulomb repulsive cost~\citep{Seidl1999,ButCDP2012,ColomboDeP2015}. For $N = 2$ electrons in one dimension, this reduces to finding a map $f$ pushing $\rho/2$ to itself (self-transport) that minimizes the Coulomb repulsion energy
\begin{equation}\label{eq:coulomb-sce}
    \min_{f:\; f_\#(\rho/2) = \rho/2} \int \frac{1}{|x - f(x)|}\,\frac{\rho(x)}{2}\,dx.
\end{equation}
The exact solution is the \emph{co-motion function} of Seidl~\citep{Seidl1999}:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:comotion}
    f(x) = F^{-1}(1 - F(x)),
\end{equation}
where $F$ is the CDF of $\rho$ \zihe{$\rho$ or $\rho/2$}. This is the anti-quantile coupling: a monotone decreasing map that sends each electron to its ``opposite'' in the density distribution. Physically, for a diatomic molecule with atoms at positions $\pm d/2$, the co-motion function maps electrons on atom~A to atom~B and vice versa.

\paragraph{\it Why Gangbo--McCann does not apply.}
The cost $c(x,y) = 1/|x-y|$ violates the hypotheses of the Gangbo--McCann existence and uniqueness theory~\citep{GangboMcCann1996} in multiple ways. First, the cost is singular: $c(x,y) \to +\infty$ as $x \to y$, so it is not continuous on the diagonal. Second, $c$ is convex and decreasing in $|x-y|$, the opposite regime from the convex costs $c(x,y) = h(x-y)$ with $h$ strictly convex for which Gangbo--McCann guarantees optimal maps are monotone increasing. Third, the mixed partial derivative
\[
    D^2_{xy} c(x,y) = \frac{2}{|x-y|^3} > 0,\quad x\neq y
\]
has the wrong sign for the standard twist condition \zihe{The standard twist condition only asks $y\mapsto c_x(x,y)$ to be injective\citep[Definition 1.16]{Santambrogio2015}}, which requires $D^2_{xy}c < 0$ to ensure the optimal map is monotone increasing. The positive sign instead produces a ``reverse twist'' consistent with a monotone \emph{decreasing} optimal map. Fourth, the self-transport setting $\mu = \nu$ is degenerate. Specifically, the identity map is always feasible but infinitely costly under the Coulomb cost, so the problem has a fundamentally different character from the generic $\mu \neq \nu$ setting.

\paragraph{\it Problem setup.}
We model a one-dimensional diatomic molecule with density
\[
    \rho(x) \propto \exp\!\Big(\!-\!\Big(\frac{x - d/2}{\sigma}\Big)^{\!2}\Big) + \exp\!\Big(\!-\!\Big(\frac{x + d/2}{\sigma}\Big)^{\!2}\Big),
\]
a symmetric bimodal Gaussian with atom separation~$d$ and width~$\sigma$. We consider three configurations of increasing difficulty:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \emph{Wide molecule}: $d = 5.0$, $\sigma = 0.3$ (well-separated atoms, easiest);
    \item \emph{Medium molecule}: $d = 3.5$, $\sigma = 0.4$;
    \item \emph{Tight molecule}: $d = 2.5$, $\sigma = 0.4$ (overlapping tails, hardest).
\end{enumerate}
\zihe{Comment to the next line:
For a symmetric density, the anti-quantile map
\[
f(x)=F^{-1}(1-F(x))
\]
has a fixed point at the median \(x_0\), since \(F(x_0)=1/2\) implies
\[
f(x_0)=F^{-1}(1-F(x_0))=F^{-1}(1/2)=x_0.
\]
Hence one cannot claim \(\min_x |x-f(x)|>0\) on the full support.}
\zihe{Although one does not have \(\min_x |x-f(x)|>0\) on the full support, the anti-quantile map in the symmetric bimodal setting satisfies
\[
|x-f(x)|>0
\quad\text{for almost every }x,
\]
with equality only at the median \(x_0\), where \(f(x_0)=x_0\). Thus the map transports almost all mass away from the diagonal, pairing the two modes against each other. This avoids the Coulomb singularity almost everywhere.}
The bimodal structure ensures that $\min_x |x - f(x)| > 0$ on the support of $\rho$, so that the Coulomb singularity is avoided by the optimal map. As the atom separation~$d$ decreases, the minimum gap $|x - f(x)|$ shrinks and the problem becomes numerically more challenging.

We use the smoothed cost $c_\varepsilon(x,y) = 1/(|x-y| + \varepsilon)$ with $\varepsilon = 10^{-3}$ to regularize the singularity.

\paragraph{\it Solver configuration.}
We parameterize the transport map as a piecewise linear function with $n = 400$ pieces ($n + 1 = 401$ parameters) on the domain $[-6, 6]$, without imposing a monotonicity constraint. The pushforward constraint $f_\#(\rho/2) = \rho/2$ is enforced via a histogram discretization with $m = 200$ bins. This is a substantially finer discretization than the earlier experiments ($n = 40$, $m = 20$), motivated by the sharp transitions in the co-motion function between the two atomic modes.

We solve via the SQP method of Section~\ref{subsec:discr-SQP} with Armijo line search on the merit function $M = \mathcal{L} + (\sigma/2)\|\tau\|^2$. The objective gradient and Hessian diagonal are computed analytically using the closed-form derivatives of the Coulomb cost (Theorems~\ref{thm: J1deriv}--\ref{thm: J2deriv}), and the constraint Jacobian is computed via batched finite differences on the GPU. The initial merit penalty is set to $\sigma_0 = 200$ (higher than in other experiments) to reflect the large cost magnitudes near the singularity. Up to 120 SQP iterations are performed with tolerance $\|\tau\|_\infty < 5 \times 10^{-3}$.

\paragraph{\it Multiple initializations.}
We run the solver from four distinct starting maps, each constructed via quantile functions of $\rho$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \emph{Anti-quantile} (monotone decreasing): $f_0(x) = F^{-1}(1 - F(x))$, the exact co-motion function evaluated on the PL grid.
    \item \emph{Quantile} (monotone increasing): $f_0(x) = F^{-1}(F(x)) = x$, the identity.
    \item \emph{Tent} (non-monotone): $f_0(x) = F^{-1}(1 - |2F(x) - 1|)$.
    \item \emph{V-shape} (non-monotone): $f_0(x) = F^{-1}(|2F(x) - 1|)$.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{\it Results.}

\begin{table}[t]
    \centering
    \caption{Coulomb self-transport $c(x,y) = 1/|x-y|$ for three diatomic molecule configurations, solved by SQP with $n = 400$ PL pieces and $m = 200$ histogram bins. The anti-quantile initialization recovers the exact co-motion function in all cases, with relative errors below $0.05\%$ for the medium and wide molecules.}
    \label{tab:coulomb-results}
    \begin{tabular}{lcccc}
        \toprule
        Density & Best init. & $J(f)$ & $J_{\mathrm{exact}}$ & Rel.\ error \\
        \midrule
        Tight ($d{=}2.5$)  & anti-quantile & $0.2125$ & $0.2126$ & $0.05\%$ \\
        Medium ($d{=}3.5$) & anti-quantile & $0.1469$ & $0.1469$ & $< 0.01\%$ \\
        Wide ($d{=}5.0$)   & anti-quantile & $0.1007$ & $0.1007$ & $< 0.01\%$ \\
        \bottomrule
    \end{tabular}
\end{table}

Table~\ref{tab:coulomb-results} reports the results across the three molecule configurations. Several observations are noteworthy.

First, the anti-quantile initialization recovers the exact co-motion function to high precision in all three cases. For the medium and wide molecules, the SQP objective matches the exact value to four or five significant figures; for the tight molecule, the relative error is $0.05\%$. 

Second, the solver recovers the monotone decreasing structure of the co-motion function without any monotonicity being imposed \emph{a priori}; the decreasing character emerges from the optimization. The other initializations (quantile, tent, V-shape) all converge to local optima with substantially higher objective values (Figure~\ref{fig:coulomb-maps}). The quantile (identity) map is particularly poor, with objectives $2$--$6\times$ worse than the anti-quantile, reflecting the Coulomb cost's strong preference for large displacement.

Third, the convergence plots (Figure~\ref{fig:coulomb-convergence}) show that the anti-quantile initialization converges to feasibility ($\|\tau\|_\infty < 5 \times 10^{-3}$) within the first few SQP iterations for all three molecule configurations, reaching constraint violations of order $10^{-3}$--$10^{-4}$. 
%This contrasts with the augmented Lagrangian, where the constraint violation stagnated at ${\sim}10^{-1}$ for the same problem. 
The other initializations reach higher constraint violations ($10^{-2}$--$10^{-1}$) but still show active progress, with the tent and V-shape initializations gradually approaching feasibility over 120 iterations.

Fourth, the cost landscape visualization (Figure~\ref{fig:coulomb-cost-maps}) reveals the structure of the problem: the Coulomb cost matrix has a bright ridge along the diagonal $x = y$ (where $c \to \infty$), and the optimal map traces a path through the low-cost region far from this ridge. The SQP solution (cyan) overlays the exact co-motion function (white dashed) nearly exactly across the support of $\rho$. \harsha{Yeah, but the experiment started at the optimal solution...}

\paragraph{\it Cost analysis.}
Figure~\ref{fig:coulomb-analysis} compares the Coulomb cost to the quadratic and concave costs used in earlier experiments. The left panel shows $c$ as a function of distance: unlike the quadratic cost (which grows with distance) or the concave cost (which rewards distance), the Coulomb cost is convex and decreasing---it penalizes proximity. The right panel plots the mixed partial $D^2_{xy}c$, which is singular at $x = y$ with large magnitude of both signs due to the non-differentiability of $|x-y|$ at the diagonal. Away from the singularity, the analytical formula $c_{xy} = 2/|x-y|^3 > 0$ confirms the ``reverse twist'': the positive sign is consistent with the anti-quantile (monotone decreasing) structure of the optimal map.

\paragraph{\it Discussion.}
This experiment demonstrates the SQP framework's applicability to a physically important class of optimal transport problems that lies entirely outside classical theory. The Coulomb cost combines three features that individually pose challenges for existing methods (singularity, repulsiveness, and self-transport), and yet the SQP solver recovers the correct co-motion function from a suitable initialization, with relative errors below $0.05\%$.

%The improvement from the augmented Lagrangian to the SQP solver is dramatic: constraint violations decrease from $10^{-1}$ (stagnation) to $10^{-3}$--$10^{-4}$ (convergence), and relative errors in the objective decrease from $0.8\%$--$6.4\%$ to below $0.05\%$. This improvement comes from two sources: the finer discretization ($n = 400$ vs.\ $40$), which resolves the sharp transitions in the co-motion function; and the SQP solver, which drives feasibility and optimality simultaneously through the KKT system rather than sequentially through penalty updates.

The connection to electronic structure theory is interesting since the strictly correlated electrons (SCE) formalism~\citep{Seidl1999,ButCDP2012} provides exact exchange-correlation functionals for DFT in the strong-interaction limit, and efficient computation of co-motion functions is an active area of research~\citep{ColomboDeP2015}. The SQP framework offers a direct approach to these problems that does not require the specialized structure exploited by existing SCE solvers (such as the ODE-based method for 1D problems), and extends naturally to the multi-marginal setting ($N > 2$ electrons) where analytical solutions are unavailable.

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/coulumb/fig_coulomb_1_maps.png}
    \caption{Top row: Bimodal electron densities for three diatomic molecule configurations (tight, medium, wide). Bottom row: Transport maps from four initializations. The anti-quantile initialization (pink, starred) recovers the exact co-motion function (black dashed) in all cases, with the SQP objective matching the exact value to four or five significant figures. The other initializations converge to distinct local optima with substantially higher objective values. Brackets~$[\,!\,]$ indicate solutions that did not meet the feasibility tolerance.}
    \label{fig:coulomb-maps}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/coulumb/fig_coulomb_2_cost_maps.png}
    \caption{Coulomb cost matrix $c(x,y) = 1/|x-y|$ (clipped at the 95th percentile for visibility) overlaid with the exact co-motion function (white dashed) and the SQP solution (cyan). The bright ridge along the diagonal represents the Coulomb singularity; the optimal map avoids this region by mapping each atomic mode to the opposite mode.}
    \label{fig:coulomb-cost-maps}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/coulumb/fig_coulomb_3_convergence.png}
    \caption{Constraint violation $\|\tau\|_\infty$ versus SQP iteration for all initializations and molecule configurations. The anti-quantile initialization (pink) converges to ${\sim}10^{-3}$--$10^{-4}$ within the first few iterations, well below the tolerance (red dotted line). This is a marked improvement over the augmented Lagrangian, which stagnated at ${\sim}10^{-1}$ for the same problem.}
    \label{fig:coulomb-convergence}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[t]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{graphs/coulumb/fig_coulomb_4_analysis.png}
    \caption{Left: Comparison of cost functions as a function of distance $|x-y|$. The Coulomb cost (red) is convex and decreasing, unlike the quadratic (blue) or concave (green) costs. Right: Mixed partial $D^2_{xy}c$ computed via finite differences on the smoothed cost, showing the singularity at $x = y$. The analytical formula $c_{xy} = 2/|x-y|^3 > 0$ confirms the ``reverse twist''---the wrong sign for the standard Gangbo--McCann condition.}
    \label{fig:coulomb-analysis}
\end{figure}

\section{Conclusions}
This paper developed a sequential quadratic programming (SQP) framework for the Monge optimal transport problem under general cost functions. Working directly in the infinite-dimensional space of transport maps, we derived explicit first- and second-order Fr\'echet derivatives for both the Monge objective functional and the Perron--Frobenius pushforward constraint operator. These variational formulas served as the foundation for a functional SQP iteration, for which we established local quadratic convergence by verifying the conditions of \cite{alt1990lagrange}, and global convergence to critical points of a merit functional via an Armijo line search.

A key structural insight of our analysis concerns the local geometry of the feasible set: injectivity of the first-variation operator of the pushforward constraint implies that feasible points are isolated in any finite-dimensional approximation space, yet this isolation breaks down in infinite dimensions due to the possible vanishing of the minimum modulus. This distinction has direct algorithmic consequences, as it necessitates methods---such as SQP---that can navigate infeasible regions while systematically restoring feasibility.

To bridge the gap between the infinite-dimensional formulation and computation, we introduced a finite-dimensional computational problem obtained by restricting transport maps to a parametric family and discretizing the pushforward constraint on a finite grid. We proved that the optimal objective value of the computational problem converges to the true optimal value as both the approximation dimension and the grid resolution are refined, with explicit rates of $O(1/n + 1/m)$. Numerical experiments on one-dimensional problems---including quadratic and concave transport costs---validated the framework and demonstrated close agreement with known analytical solutions.



\subsection{Limitations and Future Directions}

Several important directions remain open.

\paragraph{\bf Extension to higher dimensions.}
The most significant limitation of the present work is the restriction to one-dimensional domains, $\mathcal{X}, \mathcal{Y} \subset \mathbb{R}$. Extending the framework to $\mathbb{R}^d$ with $d \geq 2$ poses fundamental challenges. In one dimension, the pushforward constraint admits a convenient pointwise representation through the change-of-variables formula involving $s^{-1}$ and its derivative, which we exploit for both the theoretical analysis and the grid-based discretization. In higher dimensions, the analogous object is the Jacobian determinant of the transport map, and the Perron--Frobenius operator takes the form
\[
Pf(s)(y) = \frac{f(s^{-1}(y))}{|\det \nabla s(s^{-1}(y))|},
\]
which is well-defined only when $s$ is a diffeomorphism. Computing Fr\'echet derivatives of this operator with respect to $s$ requires differentiating through the Jacobian determinant and the inverse map simultaneously, leading to substantially more complex expressions. Moreover, the characteristic-function-based derivation of the first and second variations (Theorems~4 and~5) relies on one-dimensional Fourier analysis and does not directly generalize to multivariate settings. Developing analogous variational formulas and verifying the SQP regularity conditions in $\mathbb{R}^d$ is a challenging but important direction for future work.

\paragraph{\bf Richer parameterizations and scalability.}
Our computational experiments employed piecewise linear and low-degree polynomial parameterizations, which suffice for one-dimensional problems but may be inadequate for higher-dimensional or large-scale applications. Neural network parameterizations of transport maps, as used in normalizing flows and related generative models, offer far greater expressiveness but introduce additional nonconvexity through the parameterization itself. Integrating SQP-type constraint enforcement with neural network training, potentially through augmented Lagrangian or penalty methods that approximate the SQP structure, is a promising avenue for making the framework applicable to modern machine learning settings.

\paragraph{\bf Constraint qualification and degeneracy.} \harsha{perhaps we should remove this...might invite revision work...}
Our convergence results rely on regularity conditions such as LICQ and second-order sufficiency at the solution. While Proposition~8 shows that LICQ can be ensured by appropriate grid placement when $n > m$, a more thorough understanding of when second-order sufficient conditions hold---or fail---for specific cost functions and distribution pairs would strengthen the practical applicability of the framework. In particular, characterizing the landscape of local optima for nonconvex Monge problems, and understanding the basins of attraction of the SQP iterates, remain largely unexplored.

% \paragraph{\bf Convergence rates for the SQP iterates.}
% While we established convergence rates for the \emph{objective values} of the computational approximation ($O(1/n + 1/m)$), the convergence of the transport maps themselves---in appropriate function space norms---is only guaranteed under strict convexity of the cost. Establishing map-level convergence rates under weaker assumptions, or quantifying the interaction between SQP iteration count, approximation dimension $n$, and grid resolution $m$, would provide a more complete computational complexity picture.

\paragraph{\bf Discrete and singular source measures.}
Many applications involve empirical or discrete source measures $\mu = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^N \delta_{x_i}$, for which the absolute continuity assumption underlying our formulation does not hold. Adapting the SQP framework to handle singular measures---potentially through regularization, semi-discrete formulations, or relaxation of the pushforward constraint---would significantly broaden the scope of applicability.
