\bibliography{lib}
\newpage
\appendix
\section{Regularities of Pushforward Density}\label{sec:pf-regularity}
Our consistency analysis in Section~\ref{sec:cp} relies on pointwise constraint discretization. To guarantee that solutions to this discretized problem converge to the true continuous solution, the pushforward density $Pf(s)$ must be continuous. This appendix proves that $Pf(s)$ admits a continuous representative, providing the justification for our grid-based approximation.
Recall that
\begin{align}
    Pf(s)(x) := \frac{d}{dx}\int_{s^{-1}((-\infty, x])} f(y)\,dy .
    \label{eq:Pf-def}
\end{align}
Let $\mu_s$ be the pushforward of $\mu$ under $s$, i.e.,
\[
\mu_s(A)=\mu\big(s^{-1}(A)\big), \qquad \forall\text{ measurable sets }  A\subseteq \mathrm{supp}(g).
\]
Let $\mu_L$ denote the Lebesgue measure on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
Whenever $\mu_s\ll \mu_L$, the Radon--Nikodym theorem guarantees the existence of
a $\mu_L$--a.e.\ unique density $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}\in L^1(\mu_L)$ satisfying
\[
\mu_s(A)=\int_A \frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}(x)\,d\mu_L(x),\qquad \forall\text{ measurable sets }  A\subseteq \mathrm{supp}(g).
\]

A measurable function $u:\mathrm{supp}(g)\to\mathbb R$ is called a \emph{version} of
$\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ if $u=\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ holds $\mu_L$--a.e.
In particular, whenever the derivative in~\eqref{eq:Pf-def} exists, $Pf(s)$ can be
viewed as a version of $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ (see, e.g., \cite[Ch.\ 4]{boyarsky2012laws}).
A \emph{continuous version} of $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ means a continuous function on
$\mathrm{supp}(g)$ that agrees with $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ $\mu_L$--a.e.

We will use the following likelihood-ratio criterion to obtain a continuous version.
Fix $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$ and let $\mathcal{U}(x_0)$ be the collection of open neighborhoods of $x_0$.
For any nested sequence $A_1\supset A_2\supset \cdots$ with each $A_n\in\mathcal{U}(x_0)$ and
$\bigcap_{n\ge1}A_n=\{x_0\}$, define the likelihood ratio (when it exists) by
\begin{align}
    \lim_{n\to\infty}\frac{\mu_s(A_n)}{\mu_L(A_n)}.
    \label{eq:likelihood-def}
\end{align}

The following theorem of \cite{piccioni1983continuous} links the pointwise existence of
likelihood ratios to the existence of a continuous Radon--Nikodym representative.

\begin{theorem}[\cite{piccioni1983continuous}]\label{thm:likelihood-ratio}
If for every $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$ the limit~\eqref{eq:likelihood-def} exists (for all nested neighborhood bases as above),
then $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ admits a continuous version $\widetilde p$ on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
Moreover, $\widetilde p(x_0)$ equals the likelihood ratio at $x_0$ for every $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{lemma}\label{lem:pf-continuous-version}
Under Assumption~\ref{ass:density-cont} and Assumption~\ref{ass:mono},
the function $Pf(s)$ can be identified as a continuous version of $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Fix any $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$. It suffices, by Theorem~\ref{thm:likelihood-ratio},
to show that the likelihood ratio exists at $x_0$ and can be identified pointwise.
For $r>0$, set $A_r:=(x_0-r,x_0+r)\cap \mathrm{supp}(g)$.
Then, using the definition of pushforward,
\begin{align*}
\frac{\mu_s(A_r)}{\mu_L(A_r)}
&= \frac{\mu\big(s^{-1}(A_r)\big)}{\mu_L(A_r)}
 = \frac{\int_{\mathrm{supp}(f)} \mathbbm{1}\{y:\, x_0-r<s(y)<x_0+r\}\, f(y)\,dy}{\mu_L(A_r)} .
\end{align*}
Since $\mu_L(A_r)=2r$ for all sufficiently small $r$ (because $\mathrm{supp}(g)$ is an open interval),
we have for small $r$,
\begin{align}
\frac{\mu_s(A_r)}{\mu_L(A_r)}
= \frac{1}{2r}\int_{\mathrm{supp}(f)} \mathbbm{1}\{y:\, x_0-r<s(y)<x_0+r\}\, f(y)\,dy .
\label{eq:likelihood-ball}
\end{align}

By Assumption~\ref{ass:mono}, $s:\mathrm{supp}(f)\to\mathrm{supp}(g)$ is a bijection that is strictly monotone.
Hence $s$ is continuous and admits a continuous inverse $s^{-1}$ on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
Moreover, $s^{-1}$ is monotone and therefore has finite left and right derivatives everywhere, and is differentiable a.e.\ on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.

Assume without loss of generality that $s$ is strictly increasing (the decreasing case is analogous).
Then for all sufficiently small $r>0$,
\[
\{y:\, x_0-r<s(y)<x_0+r\} = \big(s^{-1}(x_0-r),\, s^{-1}(x_0+r)\big),
\]
and~\eqref{eq:likelihood-ball} becomes
\begin{align}
\frac{\mu_s(A_r)}{\mu_L(A_r)}
= \frac{1}{2r}\int_{s^{-1}(x_0-r)}^{s^{-1}(x_0+r)} f(y)\,dy .
\label{eq:likelihood-interval}
\end{align}

We first consider points $x_0$ at which $s^{-1}$ is differentiable.
Define
\[
F(r):=\int_{s^{-1}(x_0)}^{s^{-1}(x_0+r)} f(y)\,dy,
\qquad r \ \text{in a neighborhood of }0.
\]
Then
\[
\int_{s^{-1}(x_0-r)}^{s^{-1}(x_0+r)} f(y)\,dy
= F(r)-F(-r),
\]
so
\[
\frac{1}{2r}\int_{s^{-1}(x_0-r)}^{s^{-1}(x_0+r)} f(y)\,dy
= \frac{F(r)-F(-r)}{2r}.
\]
By the Leibniz rule and continuity of $f$ (Assumption~\ref{ass:density-cont}),
$F$ is differentiable at $0$ with
\[
F'(0)= f\big(s^{-1}(x_0)\big)\,(s^{-1})'(x_0).
\]
Therefore,
\[
\lim_{r\rightarrow0}\frac{F(r)-F(-r)}{2r} = F'(0)= f\big(s^{-1}(x_0)\big)\,(s^{-1})'(x_0),
\]
which shows the likelihood ratio exists at such $x_0$.

At points $x_0$ where $s^{-1}$ is not differentiable, monotonicity still guarantees the existence
of the one-sided derivatives $\partial^- s^{-1}(x_0)$ and $\partial^+ s^{-1}(x_0)$.
Applying the Leibniz rule in one-sided form yields
\[
\partial^- F(0)= f\big(s^{-1}(x_0)\big)\,\partial^- s^{-1}(x_0),
\qquad
\partial^+ F(0)= f\big(s^{-1}(x_0)\big)\,\partial^+ s^{-1}(x_0),
\]
and hence
\[
\lim_{r\rightarrow 0}\frac{F(r)-F(-r)}{2r}
= \frac{1}{2}\partial^-F(0)+\frac{1}{2}\partial^+F(0)
= \frac{f(s^{-1}(x_0))}{2}\Big(\partial^- s^{-1}(x_0)+\partial^+ s^{-1}(x_0)\Big),
\]
so the likelihood ratio still exists for every $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$.

Thus, for each $x_0\in \mathrm{supp}(g)$ the likelihood ratio
$\lim_{r\rightarrow0}\mu_s(A_r)/\mu_L(A_r)$ exists.
By Theorem~\ref{thm:likelihood-ratio}, $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$ admits a continuous version on $\mathrm{supp}(g)$.
Since $Pf(s)$ is a version of $\frac{d\mu_s}{d\mu_L}$,
we may identify $Pf(s)$ with this continuous version, completing the proof.
\end{proof}


\section{Proofs from Section\ref{sec:pre}}\label{proof:pre}

\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: J1deriv}}
Recall that in Theorem~\ref{thm: J1deriv} we define
\[
J_s(h)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\,D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\,f(x)dx .
\]
Then
\begin{align*}
    &\left|J(s+h)-(J(s)+J_s(h))\right|\\
    &\quad\quad=\Bigg|\int_{\mathcal{X}} c\big(x,s(x)+h(x)\big)f(x)dx
          -\int_{\mathcal{X}} c\big(x,s(x)\big)f(x)dx
          -\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\,D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)f(x)dx\Bigg|.
\end{align*}
By the fundamental theorem of calculus, for each fixed $x\in\mathcal{X}$,
\begin{align*}
c\big(x,s(x)+h(x)\big)
&=c\big(x,s(x)\big)+\int_0^1 D_y c\big(x,s(x)+\tilde{\epsilon} h(x)\big)\,h(x)\,d\tilde{\epsilon}\\
&=c\big(x,s(x)\big)+D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)h(x)
    +\int_0^1\!\Big(D_y c\big(x,s(x)+\tilde{\epsilon} h(x)\big)-D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\Big)h(x)\,d\tilde{\epsilon} .
\end{align*}
Hence,
\begin{align*}
\left|c\big(x,s(x)+h(x)\big)-c\big(x,s(x)\big)-D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)h(x)\right|
&\le \int_0^1 \left|D_y c\big(x,s(x)+\tilde{\epsilon} h(x)\big)-D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\right|\,|h(x)|\,d\tilde{\epsilon}\\
&\le \int_0^1 L\,|\tilde{\epsilon} h(x)|\,|h(x)|\,d\tilde{\epsilon} \qquad \text{(by Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-second})}\\
&=\frac{L}{2}|h(x)|^2 .
\end{align*}
Integrating against $f(x)dx$ yields
\begin{align*}
    \left|J(s+h)-(J(s)+J_s(h))\right|
    &\le \int_{\mathcal{X}}\frac{L}{2}|h(x)|^2 f(x)dx
    \le \frac{L}{2}\|h\|
    =o(\|h\|),
\end{align*}
which completes the proof by the definition of the Fr\'echet derivative.

\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: J2deriv}}
Fix $h,\Tilde h\in\mathcal{S}$. Recall
\[
J_s(h)=\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\,D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\,f(x)dx,
\qquad
H_s(h)(\Tilde h)=\int_{\mathcal{X}}\Tilde h(x)h(x)\,D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s(x)\big)\,f(x)dx .
\]
We consider
\begin{align*}
\left|J_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)-\big(J_s(h)+\epsilon H_s(h)(\Tilde h)\big)\right|
&=\Bigg|\int_{\mathcal{X}} h(x)\Big(D_y c\big(x,s(x)+\epsilon\Tilde h(x)\big)-D_y c\big(x,s(x)\big)\Big)f(x)dx\\
&\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad
-\epsilon\int_{\mathcal{X}}\Tilde h(x)h(x)\,D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s(x)\big)f(x)dx\Bigg|.
\end{align*}
By the fundamental theorem of calculus applied to $y\mapsto D_y c(x,y)$,
\[
D_y c\big(x,s+\epsilon\Tilde h\big)-D_y c(x,s)
=\epsilon\,D^2_{yy}c(x,s)\,\Tilde h
+\int_0^1\epsilon\Big(D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s+\tilde{\epsilon}\epsilon\Tilde h\big)-D^2_{yy}c(x,s)\Big)\Tilde h\,d\tilde{\epsilon} .
\]
Therefore,
\begin{align*}
&\left|J_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)-\big(J_s(h)+\epsilon H_s(h)(\Tilde h)\big)\right|\\
&\le \int_{\mathcal{X}}|h(x)|\,f(x)\int_0^1 |\epsilon|\,
\left|D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s(x)+\tilde{\epsilon}\epsilon\Tilde h(x)\big)-D^2_{yy}c\big(x,s(x)\big)\right|\,|\Tilde h(x)|\,d\tilde{\epsilon}dx\\
&\le \int_{\mathcal{X}}|h(x)|\,f(x)\int_0^1 |\epsilon|\, M\,|\tilde{\epsilon}\epsilon\Tilde h(x)|\,|\Tilde h(x)|\,d\tilde{\epsilon}dx
\qquad\text{(by Assumption~\ref{ass:cost-second})}\\
&=\frac{M}{2}\epsilon^2\int_{\mathcal{X}}|h(x)|\,|\Tilde h(x)|^2 f(x)dx
=o(|\epsilon|),
\end{align*}
which implies
\[
\frac{1}{\epsilon}\left(J_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)-J_s(h)\right)-H_s(h)(\Tilde h)\to 0
\quad\text{as }\epsilon\to 0.
\]
Hence $H_s(h)(\cdot)$ is the Fr\'echet derivative of $J_s(h)$ at $s$.

\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}}
Fix $s\in\mathcal{S}$ and $h\in\mathcal{S}$. For $\epsilon\in\mathbb R$ define
\[
X_\epsilon:=(s+\epsilon h)(Y),\qquad m_\epsilon:=\mathbb E[X_\epsilon],
\qquad Z_\epsilon:=X_\epsilon-m_\epsilon.
\]
Let $\kappa_\epsilon(t):=\mathbb E[e^{itZ_\epsilon}]$ denote the characteristic function of $Z_\epsilon$.
Recall that $Pf(s)(x)$ is the density of $X_0=s(Y)$ on $\mathcal{X}$ and that $g_s(x):=Pf(s)(x)$ is assumed
continuously differentiable with $g_s'\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$.
Let $\hat G_s$ and $\hat g_s$ denote the CDF and pdf of $Z_0=s(Y)-m_0$, respectively.
By definition,
\begin{align*}
Pf_s(h)(x)
:=\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{Pf(s+\epsilon h)(x)-Pf(s)(x)}{\epsilon}
=\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\mathbb P(X_\epsilon\le x)-\mathbb P(X_0\le x)\Big),
\end{align*}
whenever the limit exists.
Write
\[
\mathbb P(X_\epsilon\le x)=\mathbb P(Z_\epsilon\le x-m_\epsilon),
\qquad
\mathbb P(X_0\le x)=\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_0).
\]
Add and subtract $\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_\epsilon)=\hat G_s(x-m_\epsilon)$ to obtain the identity
\begin{align*}
\mathbb P(X_\epsilon\le x)-\mathbb P(X_0\le x)
&=\Big(\mathbb P(Z_\epsilon\le x-m_\epsilon)-\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_\epsilon)\Big)\\
&\quad-\Big(\hat G_s(x-m_\epsilon)-\hat G_s(x-m_0)\Big).
\end{align*}
Therefore,
\begin{align}\label{eq:split_two_terms}
\frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\mathbb P(X_\epsilon\le x)-\mathbb P(X_0\le x)\Big)
&=
T_{1,\epsilon}(x)+T_{2,\epsilon}(x),
\end{align}
where
\begin{align*}
T_{1,\epsilon}(x)
&:=
\frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\mathbb P(Z_\epsilon\le x-m_\epsilon)-\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_\epsilon)\Big),\\
T_{2,\epsilon}(x)
&:=
-\frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\hat G_s(x-m_\epsilon)-\hat G_s(x-m_0)\Big).
\end{align*}

By assumption, for all $|\epsilon|<K$ the characteristic functions $\kappa_\epsilon$ and $\kappa_0$ are integrable.
Hence, by \cite[Lemma~3.4.19]{durrett2019probability}, for each fixed $\epsilon$,
\begin{align*}
\mathbb P(Z_\epsilon\le x-m_\epsilon)-\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_\epsilon)
=\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{it(x-m_\epsilon)}\frac{\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)}{it}\,dt.
\end{align*}
Differentiate with respect to $x$. Since $\kappa_\epsilon,\kappa_0\in L^1(\mathbb R)$ and
\[
\left|\frac{d}{dx}\left(-e^{it(x-m_\epsilon)}\frac{1}{it}\right)\right|
=\left| -e^{it(x-m_\epsilon)}\right|=1,
\]
differentiation under the integral sign is justified and yields
\begin{align*}
\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\mathbb P(Z_\epsilon\le x-m_\epsilon)-\mathbb P(Z_0\le x-m_\epsilon)\Big)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{it(x-m_\epsilon)}\big(\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)\big)\,dt.
\end{align*}
Therefore,
\begin{align}\label{eq:T1_eps}
T_{1,\epsilon}(x)
=\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-itm_\epsilon}\,\frac{\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)}{\epsilon}\,dt.
\end{align}
Observe that
\[
Z_\epsilon
= (s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])+\epsilon\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big).
\]
For each fixed $t\in\mathbb R$, the map $\epsilon\mapsto \kappa_\epsilon(t)=\mathbb E[e^{itZ_\epsilon}]$ is
differentiable, with
\[
\frac{d}{d\epsilon}\kappa_\epsilon(t)
=
\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right].
\]
In particular,
\begin{align}\label{eq:kappa_derivative_at0}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)}{\epsilon}
=\frac{d}{d\epsilon}\kappa_\epsilon(t)\Big|_{\epsilon=0}
=
\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right].
\end{align}
Moreover, by Assumption~\eqref{eq:M1_dom}, for all $0<|\epsilon|<K$,
\[
\left|e^{-itm_\epsilon}\frac{\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)}{\epsilon}\right|
=\left|\frac{\kappa_\epsilon(t)-\kappa_0(t)}{\epsilon}\right|
\le
|t|\left|\mathbb E\!\left[e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right]\right|
\le M_1(t)\,\|h\|.
\]
Since $M_1\in L^1(\mathbb R)$, dominated convergence applies to the right-hand side of
\eqref{eq:T1_eps}, giving for each $x\in\mathcal{X}$,
\begin{align}\label{eq:T1_limit}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}T_{1,\epsilon}(x)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right]\,dt.
\end{align}
Since $\hat G_s$ is differentiable with derivative $\hat g_s$, we have
\[
\frac{d}{dx}\hat G_s(x-m_\epsilon)=\hat g_s(x-m_\epsilon),
\qquad
\frac{d}{dx}\hat G_s(x-\mathbb E[X_0])=\hat g_s(x-\mathbb E[X_0]).
\]
Therefore,
\[
T_{2,\epsilon}(x)
=
-\frac{1}{\epsilon}\Big(\hat g_s(x-m_\epsilon)-\hat g_s(x-\mathbb E[X_0])\Big).
\]
Because $\hat g_s$ is continuously differentiable, a first-order Taylor expansion gives
\[
\hat g_s(x-m_\epsilon)
=
\hat g_s(x-\mathbb E[X_0])-\hat g_s'(x-\mathbb E[X_0])\,(m_\epsilon-\mathbb E[X_0])
+o(|m_\epsilon-\mathbb E[X_0]|).
\]
Since $m_\epsilon-\mathbb E[X_0]=\epsilon\,\mathbb E[h(Y)]$, we obtain
\begin{align}\label{eq:T2_limit}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}T_{2,\epsilon}(x)
=
-\hat g_s'(x-\mathbb E[X_0])\,\mathbb E[h(Y)].
\end{align}
Finally, $\hat g_s(u)=g_s(u+\mathbb E[s(Y)])$ implies
$\hat g_s'(x-\mathbb E[X_0])=g_s'(x)$, hence the limit in \eqref{eq:T2_limit} equals $-g_s'(x)\mathbb E[h(Y)]$.
Combining \eqref{eq:split_two_terms}, \eqref{eq:T1_limit}, and \eqref{eq:T2_limit} yields, for each $x\in\mathcal{X}$,
\begin{align*}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx}\Big(\mathbb P(X_\epsilon\le x)-\mathbb P(X_0\le x)\Big)
=&\;\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right]\,dt\\
&\;-\;g_s'(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)].
\end{align*}
This defines the candidate derivative $Pf_s(h)(x)$.

To show $Pf_s\in\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)$, use the inequality
\[
\sup_{x\in\mathcal{X}}\left|\int_{\mathbb R} e^{itx}F(t)\,dt\right|\le \int_{\mathbb R}|F(t)|\,dt
\qquad (F\in L^1(\mathbb R)).
\]
With
\[
F(t):=
\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right],
\]
Assumption~\eqref{eq:M1_dom} (with $\epsilon=0$) gives $\int_{\mathbb R}|F(t)|\,dt\le \|M_1\|_{L^1}\|h\|$.
Hence,
\[
\left\|\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\mathbb E[s(Y)]}F(t)\,dt\right\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}
\le \frac{|\mathcal{X}|}{2\pi}\|M_1\|_{L^1(\mathbb R)}\,\|h\|.
\]
Moreover, since $\mu$ is a probability measure, $|\mathbb E[h(Y)]|\le \|h\|=\|h\|$, so
\[
\|g_s'(\cdot)\mathbb E[h(Y)]\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}\le \|g_s'\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}\,\|h\|.
\]
Combining the two bounds yields $\|Pf_s(h)\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}\le C\|h\|$, proving $Pf_s\in\mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)$.

Finally, the domination \eqref{eq:M1_dom} provides an $L^1(\mathcal{X})$ envelope independent of $\epsilon$ for the integrand
in \eqref{eq:T1_eps}, and $g_s'\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$ controls the shift term. Therefore the difference quotient
$\epsilon^{-1}(Pf(s+\epsilon h)-Pf(s))$ converges in $\mathcal{Z}=L^1(\mathcal{X})$ to $Pf_s(h)$, establishing
Fr\'echet differentiability at $s$ with derivative $Pf'(s)=Pf_s$.

\begin{comment}
  \subsection{Proof of Corollary~\ref{cor: PF1deriv}}
    \begin{align*}
    & Pf_s(h)(x)\\ 
    =&\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0} \frac{1}{\epsilon}(Pf(s+\epsilon h)(x)-Pf(s)(x))\\
    =&\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}  \frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx} \left(\int_{(s+\epsilon h)^{-1}(A)} f(y)dy - \int_{s^{-1}(A)} f(y)dy\right)\\
    =&\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}  \frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx} \left(\int_{-\infty}^{(s+\epsilon h)^{-1}(x)} f(y)dy - \int_{-\infty}^{s^{-1}(x)}f(y)dy\right)\\
    =&\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}  \frac{1}{\epsilon}\frac{d}{dx} \left(\int_{s^{-1}(x)}^{(s+\epsilon h)^{-1}(x)} f(y)dy\right)\\
    =&\lim_{\epsilon\rightarrow 0}\left(f((s+\epsilon h)^{-1}(x))\frac{d}{dx}(s+\epsilon h)^{-1}(x)-f(s^{-1}(x))\frac{d}{dx}s^{-1}(x)\right)\\
    =&\frac{d}{dz}\frac{f((s+zh)^{-1}(x))}{(s+zh)'((s+zh)^{-1}(x))}\Big|_{z=0}.
\end{align*}
The penultimate expression is due to the Leibniz rule and the last expression is based on the derivative of an inverse function. Note that the proof only goes through because strictly increasing functions are invertible, and the summation of two strictly increasing functions is also strictly increasing.  
\end{comment}


\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF2deriv}}
\begin{proof}
Fix $s\in\mathcal{S}$ and $h,\Tilde h\in\mathcal{S}$. For $\epsilon\in\mathbb R$ define
\[
X_\epsilon := (s+\epsilon\Tilde h)(Y),\qquad m_\epsilon:=\mathbb E[X_\epsilon],\qquad
Z_\epsilon := X_\epsilon-m_\epsilon.
\]
Recall from Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} (with the corrected Fourier factors) that for each $\epsilon$,
\begin{align}\label{eq:Pf_first_var_eps_pf2}
Pf_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)(x)
=&\;\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\,m_\epsilon}\,
\mathbb E\!\left[
it\,e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)
\right]\,dt
-\;g_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}'(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)].
\end{align}
Similarly,
\begin{align}\label{eq:Pf_first_var_0_pf2}
Pf_{s}(h)(x)
=&\;\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\,\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\!\left[
it\,e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)
\right]\,dt
-\;g_{s}'(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)].
\end{align}

We consider the difference quotient
\begin{align}\label{eq:second_var_start_newletters}
\frac{1}{\epsilon}\Big(Pf_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)(x)-Pf_s(h)(x)\Big)
=\mathsf{A}_\epsilon(x)+\mathsf{B}_\epsilon(x),
\end{align}
where $\mathsf{A}_\epsilon$ collects the Fourier-integral terms and $\mathsf{B}_\epsilon$ collects the density-derivative terms.
Since $g_s$ is twice continuously differentiable and $g_s''\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$, we have
\[
\mathsf{B}_\epsilon(x)
=
\frac{-g'_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(x)\mathbb E[h(Y)]+g'_s(x)\mathbb E[h(Y)]}{\epsilon}.
\]
By the same shift argument used in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv}, the derivative of the shift contributes
\[
\partial_\epsilon g'_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(x)\big|_{\epsilon=0}
= -g_s''(x)\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)],
\]
and thus
\begin{align}\label{eq:B_limit}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\mathsf{B}_\epsilon(x)
= g_s''(x)\,\mathbb E[h(Y)]\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)].
\end{align}
Define for each $\epsilon$ and $t$,
\[
\alpha_\epsilon(t):=e^{-it\,m_\epsilon},\qquad
\beta_\epsilon(t):=\mathbb E\!\left[it\,e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right].
\]
Then the Fourier term in \eqref{eq:Pf_first_var_eps_pf2} equals
\[
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,\alpha_\epsilon(t)\,\beta_\epsilon(t)\,dt,
\]
and therefore
\begin{align}\label{eq:A_eps}
\mathsf{A}_\epsilon(x)
=\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,
\frac{\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)-\alpha_0(t)\beta_0(t)}{\epsilon}\,dt.
\end{align}
For each fixed $t\in\mathbb R$, both $\epsilon\mapsto \alpha_\epsilon(t)$ and $\epsilon\mapsto \beta_\epsilon(t)$ are differentiable.
Since $m_\epsilon=\mathbb E[s(Y)]+\epsilon\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]$, we have
\[
\alpha_\epsilon'(t)=\frac{d}{d\epsilon}e^{-it m_\epsilon}=-it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]\,e^{-it m_\epsilon}.
\]
Also, because
\[
Z_\epsilon=(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])+\epsilon(\Tilde h(Y)-\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]),
\]
we obtain
\[
\beta_\epsilon'(t)
=\mathbb E\!\left[it\cdot it\,e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(\Tilde h(Y)-\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]\big)\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\right]
=
-\mathbb E\!\left[t^2 e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h-\mathbb E h\big)\big(\Tilde h-\mathbb E\Tilde h\big)\right].
\]
Thus, by the product rule,
\begin{align}\label{eq:alphabeta_prime}
\frac{d}{d\epsilon}\big(\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)\big)
&=\alpha_\epsilon'(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)+\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon'(t)\\
&=\;e^{-it m_\epsilon}\,
\mathbb E\Bigg[
e^{itZ_\epsilon}\big(h-\mathbb E h\big)\Big(t^2\big(\Tilde h-\mathbb E\Tilde h\big)+it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h]\Big)
\Bigg],
\nonumber
\end{align}
where we suppressed the argument $(Y)$ in the last expectation for readability.

Evaluating at $\epsilon=0$ gives
\begin{align}\label{eq:alphabeta_prime_0}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)-\alpha_0(t)\beta_0(t)}{\epsilon}
=
e^{-it\,\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\Bigg[
e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h-\mathbb E h\big)\Big(t^2\big(\Tilde h-\mathbb E\Tilde h\big)+it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h]\Big)
\Bigg].
\end{align}
Using \eqref{eq:alphabeta_prime} and the triangle inequality, for $0<|\epsilon|<K$,
\begin{align*}
\left|\frac{\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)-\alpha_0(t)\beta_0(t)}{\epsilon}\right|
&\le
|t|^2\left|\mathbb E\!\left[e^{itZ_\epsilon}(h-\mathbb Eh)(\Tilde h-\mathbb E\Tilde h)\right]\right|
+|t|\,|\mathbb E[\Tilde h]|\left|\mathbb E\!\left[e^{itZ_\epsilon}(h-\mathbb Eh)\right]\right|.
\end{align*}
By Assumption~\eqref{eq:M2_dom}, the first term is bounded by $M_2(t)\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|$.
For the second term, the domination hypothesis from Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} yields
\[
|t|\left|\mathbb E\!\left[e^{itZ_\epsilon}(h-\mathbb Eh)\right]\right|
\le M_1(t)\,\|h\|,
\qquad
|\mathbb E[\Tilde h]|\le \|\Tilde h\|.
\]
Hence,
\[
\left|\frac{\alpha_\epsilon(t)\beta_\epsilon(t)-\alpha_0(t)\beta_0(t)}{\epsilon}\right|
\le (M_2(t)+M_1(t))\,\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|.
\]
Since $M_1,M_2\in L^1(\mathbb R)$, dominated convergence applies to \eqref{eq:A_eps}, and using \eqref{eq:alphabeta_prime_0} gives,
for each $x\in\mathcal{X}$,
\begin{align}\label{eq:A_limit}
\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\mathsf{A}_\epsilon(x)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R}-e^{itx}\,e^{-it\,\mathbb E[s(Y)]}\,
\mathbb E\Bigg[
e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E[s(Y)])}\big(h-\mathbb E h\big)\Big(t^2\big(\Tilde h-\mathbb E\Tilde h\big)+it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde h]\Big)
\Bigg]\,dt.
\end{align}
Combining \eqref{eq:second_var_start_newletters}, \eqref{eq:A_limit}, and \eqref{eq:B_limit} yields
\begin{align*}
&\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{Pf_{s+\epsilon\Tilde h}(h)(x)-Pf_s(h)(x)}{\epsilon}
\\
&\quad\quad =\;\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R} e^{itx} e^{-it\,\mathbb E [s(Y)] }\mathbb E\Bigg[ e^{it(s(Y)-\mathbb E [s(Y)])}
\big(h(Y)-\mathbb E[h(Y)]\big)\bigg(t^2\big(\Tilde{h}(Y)-\mathbb E[\Tilde{h}(Y)]\big)+it\,\mathbb E[\Tilde{h}(Y)]\bigg)\Bigg]dt\\
&\quad\quad+\;g_s''(x)\,\mathbb E [h(Y)] \mathbb E [\Tilde{h}(Y)].
\end{align*}
This is exactly $Qf_s(h)(\Tilde h)(x)$.
Using the same Fourier bound as in the proof of Theorem~\ref{thm: PF1deriv} and the domination by
$(M_2+M_1)\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|$, we obtain
\[
\|\,Qf_s(h)(\Tilde h)\,\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}
\le
\frac{|\mathcal{X}|}{2\pi}\big(\|M_2\|_{L^1}+\|M_1\|_{L^1}\big)\,\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|
+\|g_s''\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})}\,\|h\|\,\|\Tilde h\|,
\]
since $|\mathbb E[h(Y)]|\le \|h\|$ and $|\mathbb E[\Tilde h(Y)]|\le \|\Tilde h\|$.
Therefore $Qf_s\in \mathcal{B}(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{B}\big(\mathcal{S},\mathcal{Z}_0)\big)$, and the existence of the limit
in $\mathcal{Z}$ with an $L^1(\mathcal{X})$ dominating envelope yields the Fr\'echet differentiability claim.
\end{proof}


\section{Supplementary Material from Section~\ref{sec:SQP}}\label{proof:loc}
\subsection{Example from Section~\ref{sec:local-geometry}}
\begin{example}[Infinite-dimensional counterexample]\label{ex:infinite-counterexample}
In the infinite-dimensional setting, the injectivity of the first variation $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$ does not imply that $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$ is bounded below. The following counterexample shows that the range of the operator $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$ is not closed. Specifically, we can construct a sequence $\{h_n\}$ in $\mathcal{S}$ such that $\|h_n\| = 1$ but $\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h_n)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}\to 0$. Since the operator $Pf_{\bar{s}}(\cdot)$ is injective, it attains $Pf_{\bar{s}}(h) = 0_{\mathcal{Z}_0}$ if and only if $h=0$ $\mu$--a.e., which in turn shows that the range is not closed.

As a reminder of our settings:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item $Y$ is a real random variable with probability measure $\mu$ and density $f$ on a compact set $\mathcal{X}$;
  \item $\bar{s}\in\mathcal{S}$ is the transport map and $Z := \bar{s}(Y)$ has a $C^1$ density $g_{\bar{s}}$ (the pushforward density);
\end{enumerate}
As a special context we also suppose we are considering a target density $g_{\bar{s}}$ where
\begin{align*}
    g_{\bar{s}},g_{\bar{s}}' \in L^1(\mathcal{X}) \cap L^2(\mathcal{X}).
\end{align*}

Recall that the first variation of the PF operator at a feasible $\bar{s}$ is the bounded linear map $Pf_{\bar{s}}:\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{Z}_0$ given by
\begin{equation}\label{eq:Ps-linearized}
Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)(x)
  = \frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb{R}} -e^{itx} e^{-it\,\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)]}\,
    \phi_h(t)\,dt - g_{\bar{s}}'(x)\mathbb{E}[h(Y)],
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phi-def-counter}
\phi_h(t)
 := it\,\mathbb{E}\big[(h(Y)-\mathbb{E}[h(Y)])\,
      e^{it(\bar{s}(Y)-\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)])}\big],\qquad t\in\mathbb{R}.
\end{equation}

Next, define for each integer $n$
\[
h_n(Y) := e^{inZ} = e^{in \bar{s}(Y)} = \cos nZ + i\sin nZ.
\]
Then $|h_n(Y)| = 1$ a.s., so
\[
\|h_n\|^2 = \mathbb{E}[|h_n(Y)|^2] = 1,
\qquad\Rightarrow\qquad
\|h_n\| = 1 \quad \forall n.
\]

Let $\varphi(u) := \mathbb{E}[e^{iuZ}]$ be the characteristic function of $Z$.
Since $g_{\bar{s}}\in L^1(\mathcal{X})$, the Riemann--Lebesgue lemma yields
$\varphi(u)\to 0$ as $|u|\to\infty$.
Using the density $g_{\bar{s}}$ of $Z$,
\[
\mathbb{E}[h_n(Y)]
 = \mathbb{E}[e^{inZ}]
 = \int_{\mathcal{X}} e^{inx} g_{\bar{s}}(x)dx
 = \varphi(n)\longrightarrow 0
\qquad\text{as }|n|\to\infty.
\]

Let us first consider the $\phi_{h_n}$ term. By \eqref{eq:phi-def-counter},
\[
\phi_{h_n}(t)
 = it\,\mathbb{E}\big[(h_n(Y)-\mathbb{E}[h_n(Y)])e^{it(Z-\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)])}\big].
\]
Using the definition of $Z$ and pulling out the constant term $e^{-it\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)]}$, we get
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phi-hn-char}
\phi_{h_n}(t)
 = it\,e^{-it\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)]}\Big(\varphi(n+t) - \varphi(n)\varphi(t)\Big),
\end{equation}
where $\varphi(u) = \mathbb{E}[e^{iuZ}]$ is the characteristic function.
Fix $t\in\mathbb{R}$. As $|n|\to\infty$, the Riemann--Lebesgue lemma gives
$\varphi(n+t)\to 0$ and $\varphi(n)\to 0$, while $\varphi(t)$ is fixed, so
\[
\varphi(n+t) - \varphi(n)\varphi(t) \longrightarrow 0.
\]
Thus
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phi-hn-ptwise}
\phi_{h_n}(t) \longrightarrow 0
\quad\text{for each fixed }t\in\mathbb{R}.
\end{equation}

We now show that $\phi_{h_n}\to 0$ in $L^2(\mathbb{R})$. From
\eqref{eq:phi-hn-char}, we have
\[
|\phi_{h_n}(t)|
 \le |t|\big(|\varphi(n+t)| + |\varphi(n)|\,|\varphi(t)|\big).
\]
Since $g_{\bar{s}},g_{\bar{s}}' \in L^2(\mathcal{X})$, by Plancherel’s Theorem \citep[Chapter 6, Theorem 2.4]{stein2003fourier} and the identity that
$\widehat{g_{\bar{s}}'}(u) = iu\widehat{g_{\bar{s}}}(u)$, we have
\[
\varphi = \widehat{g_{\bar{s}}} \in L^2(\mathbb{R}),
\qquad
t\varphi(t) \in L^2(\mathbb{R}).
\]
Using $|\varphi(n)|\le \|g_{\bar{s}}\|_{L^1}$ and the fact that translations are isometries
in $L^2$, we obtain a uniform upper bound
\[
|\phi_{h_n}(t)|
 \le C\Big(|t||\varphi(t+n)| + |t||\varphi(t)|\Big)
 \le (1+|t|)\,w(t),
\]
for some $w\in L^2(\mathbb{R})$ independent of $n$ and some constant $C>0$.
Combining this uniform bound with the pointwise convergence
\eqref{eq:phi-hn-ptwise} and the dominated convergence theorem in $L^2$ yields
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phi-hn-L2}
\|\phi_{h_n}\|_{L^2(\mathbb{R})} \longrightarrow 0
\qquad\text{as }|n|\to\infty.
\end{equation}

Now define
\[
\psi_n(t) := e^{-i\mathbb{E}[Z]}\,\phi_{h_n}(t).
\]
Since $|e^{-i\mathbb{E}[Z]}|=1$, we have
$\|\psi_n\|_{L^2_t} = \|\phi_{h_n}\|_{L^2_t}$. Let $\mathcal{F}^{-1}$
be the inverse Fourier transform.
By Plancherel's Theorem there exists $C_1>0$
such that
\[
\|\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi_n\|_{L^2_x}
 \le C_1\|\psi_n\|_{L^2_t}
 = C_1\|\phi_{h_n}\|_{L^2_t}.
\]
But the first term in \eqref{eq:Ps-linearized} is exactly
$\mathcal{F}^{-1}\psi_n$, up to the fixed constant $-(2\pi)^{-1}$.
Thus
\[
\left\|\frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb{R}} -e^{itx}e^{-it\,\mathbb{E}[\bar{s}(Y)]}\,
  \phi_{h_n}(t)\,dt \right\|_{L^2_x}
 \le C_2\|\phi_{h_n}\|_{L^2_t}
\]
for some constant $C_2>0$. By \eqref{eq:phi-hn-L2}, the right-hand side tends
to zero, so the Fourier part vanishes in $L^2_x$. Since $\mathcal{X}$ is compact, convergence in $L^2(\mathcal{X})$ implies convergence in $L^1(\mathcal{X})$, and therefore in $\mathcal{Z}_0$.

We now address the second term, $g_{\bar{s}}'(x)\mathbb{E}[h_n(Y)]$. Since $\mathbb{E}[h_n(Y)] = \varphi(n)\longrightarrow 0$,
we have
\[
\|g_{\bar{s}}'(\cdot)\,\mathbb{E}[h_n(Y)]\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0}
 = |\varphi(n)|\,\|g_{\bar{s}}'\|_{L^1(\mathcal{X})} \longrightarrow 0.
\]

Combining the two pieces we obtain
\[
\|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h_n)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0} \longrightarrow 0,
\]
while $\|h_n\|=1$ for all $n$.
Consequently, $Pf_{\bar{s}}:\mathcal{S}\to \mathcal{Z}_0$ is
not bounded below:
\[
\inf_{\substack{h\in\mathcal{S} \\ \|h\|=1}} \|Pf_{\bar{s}}(h)\|_{\mathcal{Z}_0} = 0.
\]
\end{example}

\section{Additional Experiments}
\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:g-to-g}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:g-to-g}: Gaussian to Gaussian}
\paragraph{\it Linear Transport Map}
In our first experiment, we consider the source and target distributions to be both Gaussian. Specifically, the source distribution is taken to be $\mu =\mathcal{N}(-0.4, 1.2)$ and the target distribution is $\mathcal{\nu} = N(1.6, 0.55)$. The transport map is parameterized as a linear function $s(x) = \alpha x + \beta$ with $\alpha > 0$ enforced through an exponential reparameterization $\alpha = e^\gamma$. For numerical implementation, we restrict to a domain that covers at least $95\%$ of the probability mass of both the source and target Gaussians. This domain is then discretized into a uniform grid of evaluation points. On this grid, the pushforward condition is enforced by matching the transported density to the target density.

This experiment provides a controlled and interpretable benchmark: the true optimal affine map is known, the numerical constraints are transparent, and the finite–grid discretization makes the SQP procedure easy to evaluate step by step.

\paragraph{\it Quadratic Transport Map}
We also conducted the same experiment while parameterizing the transport map as a quadratic function of the form
\begin{align*}
s(x) = \alpha x + \beta + \gamma x^2.
\end{align*}
The problem setup, cost function, and discretization remain unchanged. In this case, the additional quadratic term provides extra flexibility, but the SQP solver consistently drives the coefficient $\gamma$ toward zero. This behavior is consistent with the theoretical fact that the optimal map between two one–dimensional Gaussian distributions under quadratic cost is affine. Hence, the experiment not only converges successfully but also validates that the method correctly recovers the linear structure of the true solution even when a richer parameterization is allowed.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}{0.48\textwidth}
        \centering
        \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{graphs/mu0=-1.4, s0=2.2, mu1=0.6, s1=1.25.png}
        \caption{Linear map.}
        \label{fig:sub-linear}
    \end{subfigure}\hfill
    \begin{subfigure}{0.48\textwidth}
        \centering
        \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{graphs/q mu0=-1.4, s0=2.2, mu1=0.6, s1=1.25.png}
        \caption{Quadratic map.}
        \label{fig:sub-quadratic}
    \end{subfigure}
    \caption{Comparison of Target Distribution: (a) linear transport map and (b) quadratic transport map.}
    \label{fig:linear-vs-quadratic}
\end{figure}
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}{0.40\textwidth}
        \centering
        \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{graphs/mu0=-1.4, s0=2.2, mu1=0.6, s1=1.25 benchmark.png}

    \end{subfigure}\hfill

        \label{fig:sub-quadratic}
    \end{subfigure}

    \label{fig:linear-vs-quadratic}
\end{figure}


\refstepcounter{experiment}\label{exp:u-to-b}
\subsection*{Experiment~\ref{exp:u-to-b}: Uniform to Beta}
In this experiment, we transport from a Uniform$[0,1]$ with source density $f(x)=1$ to a Beta$(1,3)$ with target density $g(y)=3(1-y)^2$. We use a piecewise linear approximation of the transport map $s(x)$ on the interval $[0,1]$. As a reminder, we evaluate the constraint on a finite set of grid points: $\{d_j\}_{j=1}^{m_d} \subset \mathrm{supp}(g)$ where $m_d$ is the number of grid points. To improve resolution in regions where the target density $g$ varies rapidly,
we employ an adaptive discretization for the constraints based on quantile locations rather than
uniform grid points. Ideally, we want to evaluate the constraints more frequently at the place where the target probability mass is concentrated. Specifically, let $F_X$ and $F_Y$
denote the CDFs of the Uniform$[0,1]$ source and Beta$(1,3)$ target, we enforce the measure preserving constraints
at equal probability grids in the range of $F_Y$. We choose a set of uniformly spaced probability levels
\begin{align*}
v_j = p_{\mathrm{lo}} +
\frac{j-1}{m_d-1}\bigl(p_{\mathrm{hi}}-p_{\mathrm{lo}}\bigr),
\qquad j=1,\ldots,m_d,    
\end{align*}
with $0<p_{\mathrm{lo}}\ll 1$ and $p_{\mathrm{hi}}\approx 1$
to avoid numerical extremes near $y=0$ and $y=1$. 
The corresponding constraint grid on the target domain is then defined by
\begin{align*}
d_j := F_Y^{-1}(v_j).
\end{align*}
Because the source distribution is Uniform$[0,1]$, we maintain a uniform
grid $\{x_i\}_{i=1}^{m_x}\subset \mathrm{supp}(f)$ as the pieces in the piecewise approximation of the transport map, therefore each piece in the piecewise linear approximation is transporting the same amount of source mass. This quantile based construction concentrates constraint points in
regions where $g(y)$ has significant curvature (e.g., near $y=1$
when $(\alpha,\beta)=(1,3)$), ensuring uniform accuracy in probability
space and improved enforcement of the measure preserving condition.
The resulting SQP iterations remain stable and converge rapidly to the
analytically known optimal map $s^*$, validating the correctness and
robustness of our finite--dimensional formulation.


Because the analytic optimal map is known from quantile matching as 
\begin{align*}
s^*(x)=F^{-1}_{\mathrm{Beta}(1,3)}(x),
\end{align*}
this experiment provides a direct numerical benchmark. The learned map aligns closely with $s^*(x)$ across the entire domain, and the pushforward density $P_f(s)(y)$ matches the target $g(y)$ up to numerical tolerance.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.55\linewidth]{graphs/U2B_params13_24pieces_401_200ite1.png}
    \caption{Source $\mu(x)$ and target densities $\nu(y)$ for the Uniform$\to$Beta experiment, showing close agreement between the learned pushforward $P_f(s)(y)$ and the true target.}
    \label{fig:u2b-densities}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{graphs/U2B_params13_24pieces_401_200ite2.png}
    \caption{Computed transport map (SQP, piecewise linear) compared with the analytic quantile map for the Uniform$\to$Beta case.}
    \label{fig:u2b-map}
\end{figure}
